Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications
intermediate
10 min
The Common Inheritance Case: Istihsan vs Qiyas
Applies istihsan to a disputed inheritance case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the 'common case' in inheritance, compare the Hanafi (qiyas-based) and Maliki/Shafi'i (istihsan-based) positions, and state the practical consequence of the difference.

Core Idea
The common case excludes full brothers under qiyas but includes them via istihsan.

This passage presents a famous inheritance dispute known as 'al-mas'ala al-mushtaraka' (the common case). A woman dies leaving a husband, mother, two uterine brothers, and two full brothers. Under strict qiyas, the uterine brothers take the entire 1/3, leaving nothing for the full brothers. The Hanafi and Hanbali schools adhere to this qiyas. However, 'Umar ibn al-Khattab and others (followed by Maliki and Shafi'i schools) held that the full brothers should share the 1/3 equally with the uterine brothers as an istihsan, because all are children of the same mother. The author considers this the stronger view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the heirs in the common case
List: husband, mother, two uterine brothers, two full brothers. Note that full brothers are 'asaba (residuary heirs).
2
Apply the default qiyas rule
Under qiyas, the fixed shares (husband 1/2, mother 1/6, uterine brothers 1/3) exhaust the estate. The 'asaba (full brothers) receive nothing.
This is the Hanafi/Hanbali position.
3
Identify the injustice and the istihsan solution
The outcome is strange: a uterine brother (half-brother) inherits while a full brother (same mother and father) does not. Istihsan: full brothers share the 1/3 equally with uterine brothers.
This is 'Umar's ijtihad, adopted by Maliki and Shafi'i.
4
Understand the legal reasoning
The istihsan is based on the common mother: all brothers are children of the same mother, so they share in the cause of inheritance from her side.
This removes hardship and achieves justice.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: Hanafi mufti will not give full brothers anything; Maliki/Shafi'i mufti will give them equal share with uterine brothers.

Source Text

Commentary

Under strict qiyas, the uterine brothers take the whole 1/3, leaving nothing for the full brothers (who are 'asaba). This seems unjust because the full brothers share the same mother as the uterine brothers. The Hanafi and Hanbali schools follow this strict qiyas. However, 'Umar ibn al-Khattab and others (followed by Maliki and Shafi'i schools) made the full brothers share equally with the uterine brothers in the 1/3 as an istihsan, because all are children of the same mother. This is presented as the stronger view, as it realizes justice and removes hardship. The passage thus illustrates how istihsan overrides a problematic analogy.

Key Terms
المسألة المشتركة (al-mas'ala al-mushtaraka): The common inheritance case involving husband, mother, uterine brothers, and full brothers.
العصبات ('asabat): Male agnatic relatives who inherit the residue after fixed shares.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the common case, what is the practical difference in inheritance distribution between the Hanafi school and the Maliki/Shafi'i school?
A) Hanafi gives full brothers a share; Maliki/Shafi'i give them nothing.
B) Hanafi gives full brothers nothing; Maliki/Shafi'i give them an equal share with uterine brothers.
C) Both schools give full brothers nothing.
D) Both schools give full brothers a share, but in different proportions.

Key Takeaway
The common inheritance case demonstrates how istihsan can override qiyas to avoid an unjust outcome, highlighting a legitimate ikhtilaf among the schools.

Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications
intermediate

Al-Mas'alah al-Mushtarakah: Istihsan Overriding Qiyas
Illustration of istihsan overriding qiyas in inheritance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the elements of the mushtarakah case and explain how istihsan overrides qiyas to produce a just ruling.

Core Idea
When strict qiyas leads to injustice, istihsan allows full brothers to share in inheritance like uterine brothers.

This passage introduces a landmark case in Hanafi usul al-fiqh known as 'al-mas'alah al-mushtarakah', where the application of strict qiyas produced a harsh outcome that was later corrected by istihsan. By studying this case, you will see how subsidiary sources like istihsan function to refine the law and achieve justice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the heirs and their categories
List the heirs in the case: husband, mother, two uterine brothers, two full brothers. Classify them as ashab al-fara'id (prescribed shares) or 'asabat (residuaries).
Husband: prescribed share 1/2; mother: 1/6; uterine brothers: 1/3; full brothers: 'asabat.
2
Apply strict qiyas to determine shares
Calculate the estate distribution: husband gets 1/2, mother 1/6, uterine brothers 1/3. These exhaust the estate, leaving zero residue for the 'asabat (full brothers).
Under qiyas, full brothers are excluded entirely, while uterine brothers (half-brothers from mother's side) inherit.
3
Identify the injustice (hardship)
The outcome seems unjust: full brothers, who share the same mother (and father) as the uterine brothers, are excluded simply because of their connection through the father. The uterine brothers inherit, but full brothers do not.
This apparent unfairness prompted the use of istihsan.
4
Understand how istihsan overrides qiyas
Umar ibn al-Khattab, after hearing the full brothers' plea, applied istihsan: the common mother outweighs the technical distinction. The full brothers share equally in the one-third with the uterine brothers.
The ruling treats all siblings from the same mother equally, overriding the strict rule of 'asabah.
5
Note the madhhab positions and modern adoption
The Hanafi and Hanbali schools follow the qiyas view (exclusion). The Maliki and Shafi'i schools follow the istihsan view (sharing). Modern laws in Egypt and Syria have adopted the sharing rule.
The author considers the sharing rule 'al-awjah' (the stronger view).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٤) المصادر التبعية الاستحسان
وهذا في الحقيقة نظير استحسان الضرورة في الاجتهاد الحنفي.
١٠/٤ - ومن الأمثلة التي يبرز فيها غلو اطراد القياس الظاهر وجوره، واختلف الاجتهاد في معالجتها بالاستحسان القضية المعروفة باسم المسألة المشتركة في الفرائض أي في أحكام الميراث، وهي أن تموت امرأة عن زوج، وأم، وأخوين لأم، وأخوين شقيقين .
فالزوج والأم والإخوة لأم هم من أصحاب الفرائض الإرثية المحددة. أما الأخوة الأشقاء فهم من العَصَبات (۱) . والقاعدة في الميراث هي: أنَّ العصبات إنما يأخذون ما يزيد عن أصحاب الفرائض .
فمقتضى القواعد القياسية في هذه الحادثة هو: أن يرث الإخوة لأم ولا يرث الأشقاء، لأن الفريضة الإرثية للزوج هنا نصف التركة؛ وللأم السدس؛ وللإخوة من الأم الثلث، فلا يبقى شيء للإخوة الأشقاء وهم العصبات. وهذا يؤدي إلى مشكلة من الغرابة بمكان، إذ يرث عندئذ الأخ لأم، ويحرم الشقيق !! وإلى هذا ذهب بعض الصحابة، وعليه الاجتهادان الحنفي والحنبلي.
ولكن عمر بن الخطاب وفريقاً آخر من الصحابة رضي الله عنهم ذهبوا إلى اشتراك الأشقاء الإخوة لأم في نصيبهم بالثلث استحساناً، باعتبار أنَّ الجميع أولاد أم واحدة، فالأشقاء يشاركونهم في سبب الإرث من جهة الأم.
وبذلك سنّ عمر سُنَّة الاستحسان المقيم للعدالة الدافع للحرج، وإلى هذا ذهب الاجتهادان المالكي والشافعي، وهو الأوجه.
(۱) العصبة في اصطلاح علم الفرائض هو كل قريب ذكر يتصل إلى الميت بمحض الذكورة، أي لا يدخل في نسبته إلى الميت أنثى، وذلك كالعم وابنه، وكالأخ وابنه، فإنهم من العصبات بخلاف العمة مثلاً لأنها أنثى، وبخلاف الخال وابنه لأن واسطة قرابتهما الأم وهي أنثى .
ويروى أنَّ عمر رضي الله عنه لما رُفِعَت إليه هذه القضية، فرأى فيها أولاً أنَّ للإخوة من الأم الثلث فريضةً فلا يبقى شيء للإخوة الأشقاء، قال له هؤلاء: «هب أن أبانا كان حماراً، ألسنا لأم واحدة؟!».
فرَجَع عمر وقضى باشتراكهم جميعاً في الثلث (ر: إعلام الموقعين لابن القيم ،٤٨/٢ ، طبعة الكردي، وبداية المجتهد لابن رشد ۲/ ۲۹۰، وشرح المنظومة الرحبية للسبط المارديني باب المسألة المشتركة)
(۱) قد عدلت الحكومة المصرية بقانون خاص صدر سنة ١٣٦٥ هـ = ١٩٤٦ المذهب الحنفي في المسألة المشتركة، وأوجبت فيها العمل بحكم الاشتراك وفقاً لمذهب مالك والشافعي وحَسَناً فعلت.
وكذلك فعلت سورية أخيراً في قانون الأحوال الشخصية الجديد سنة ١٩٥٣.
Chapter 4: Subsidiary Sources: Istihsan
This is in reality similar to istihsan al-darura (istihsan of necessity) in Hanafi ijtihad.
10/4 - Among the examples that show the exaggeration of apparent qiyas and its injustice, and where ijtihad differed in treating it with istihsan, is the well-known case called 'al-mas'alah al-mushtarakah' (the joint case) in fara'id, i.e., in the rulings of inheritance. It is that a woman dies leaving a husband, a mother, two uterine brothers, and two full brothers.
The husband, mother, and uterine brothers are the prescribed heirs (ashab al-fara'id). As for the full brothers, they are 'asabat (residuaries).(1) The rule in inheritance is that the 'asabat only take what remains after the prescribed heirs.
Thus, the requirement of qiyas rules in this case is that the uterine brothers inherit and the full brothers do not inherit, because the prescribed share of the husband here is half of the estate, the mother gets one-sixth, and the uterine brothers get one-third, so nothing remains for the full brothers, who are the residuaries. This leads to a very strange problem: the uterine brother inherits while the full brother is excluded! Some Companions held this view, and the Hanafi and Hanbali schools follow it.
But 'Umar ibn al-Khattab and another group of Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) held that the full brothers should share with the uterine brothers in the one-third as a matter of istihsan, considering that all are children of one mother, so the full brothers share with them in the cause of inheritance from the mother's side.
Thus 'Umar established a sunnah of istihsan that establishes justice and removes hardship. This is the view of the Maliki and Shafi'i schools, and it is the stronger view.
(1) 'Asabah in the terminology of fara'id is every male relative who connects to the deceased through males only, i.e., no female enters into his relation to the deceased, such as the paternal uncle and his son, and the brother and his son. They are 'asabat, unlike the paternal aunt for example, because she is female, and unlike the maternal uncle and his son because the intermediary of their relation is the mother, who is female.
It is narrated that when this case was presented to 'Umar (RA), he first saw that the uterine brothers get one-third as a prescribed share, so nothing remains for the full brothers. They said to him: 'Suppose our father was a donkey, are we not from one mother?' So 'Umar retracted and judged that they all share in the one-third. (See: I'lam al-Muwaqqi'in by Ibn Qayyim 2/48, Kurdish edition; Bidayat al-Mujtahid by Ibn Rushd 2/290; and Sharh al-Manzumah al-Rahabiyyah by al-Sibt al-Mardini, chapter on al-mas'alah al-mushtarakah.)
(1) The Egyptian government amended by a special law issued in 1365 AH = 1946 CE the Hanafi school's ruling on the joint case, and made it obligatory to act on the ruling of sharing according to the Maliki and Shafi'i schools, and they did well.
Similarly, Syria did so recently in the new Personal Status Law of 1953.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents the concept of istihsan (juristic preference) as a subsidiary source of Islamic law, illustrated by the famous inheritance case known as 'al-mas'alah al-mushtarakah.' The author demonstrates how strict qiyas (analogical reasoning) leads to a result that is both harsh and unjust: the uterine brothers (half-brothers from the mother's side) inherit the entire one-third, while the full brothers (who share the same mother and father) are completely excluded because they are considered 'asabat (agnatic heirs) who only take the residue. The Hanafi and Hanbali schools adhere to this strict qiyas, as narrated from some Companions. However, 'Umar ibn al-Khattab, upon hearing the plea of the full brothers—'Suppose our father was a donkey, are we not from one mother?'—retracted his initial view and applied istihsan, allowing the full brothers to share equally in the one-third with the uterine brothers. The Maliki and Shafi'i schools adopted this ruling, and the author considers it the stronger opinion. The passage also notes modern statutory reforms in Egypt and Syria that have codified the sharing rule, effectively departing from the traditional Hanafi position.

Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides strict qiyas to avoid hardship or achieve justice.
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning from a primary source to a new case.
العصبة (al-'asabah): Male agnatic heir who takes the residue after prescribed shares.
المسألة المشتركة (al-mas'alah al-mushtarakah): The joint case in inheritance where qiyas would exclude full brothers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Uterine brothers
B) Full brothers
C) Husband
D) Mother

Key Takeaway
Istihsan can override strict qiyas to prevent injustice, as exemplified by the mushtarakah case.

مبدأ سد الذرائع وتطبيقاته
Discussion of sadd al-dhara'i with two case applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of sadd al-dhara'i to a given case and evaluate the Hanafi-Maliki difference on exemption clauses.

Core Idea
Blocking means to evil: a permissible act becomes prohibited if it predominantly leads to harm.

This passage introduces sadd al-dhara'i as a subsidiary source of Islamic law, showing how jurists blocked harmful means even when the act itself is permissible. Through two key applications—fleeing divorce and seller's exemption clauses—you will see how this principle operates in family law and transactions. You will also examine the methodological divergence between Hanafi and Maliki schools on its scope.

Guided Path
1
Identify the qawida and its definition
Recognize the Arabic term 'sadd al-dhara'i' and its literal and technical meaning: preventing permissible actions that lead to haram.
2
Analyze the first furu': talaq al-firar
Understand the scenario: a husband divorces his wife during terminal illness to deprive her of inheritance. The ruling: the wife inherits despite the divorce, blocking this means.
The divorce itself is valid, but the inheritance right is preserved.
3
Analyze the second furu': shart al-bara'a with bad faith
Examine the seller's exemption clause for hidden defects. The general rule: the condition is valid. But if the seller knew and concealed the defect, the condition does not exempt him.
This prevents the condition from being a means to fraud.
4
Compare Hanafi vs Maliki positions on the second furu'
Note the Hanafi view: the condition is valid even with bad faith. The Maliki view: it is invalid. Understand the methodological difference: literal contract vs. blocking means.
The author prefers the Maliki view as more aligned with maqasid.
5
Synthesize the limits and exceptions
Review the dabt (condition that the means must predominantly lead to harm) and the istithna' (Hanafi exception). Connect to related maxims like 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha'.
The principle is not applied universally; it requires careful assessment.

Source Text

الفصل (٥) المصادر التبعية : الاستصلاح
الفاعل حسن النية ولذا نهى القرآن عن سب أصنام المشركين، وإن كان الذي يسبها إنما يسبّها إيماناً بالله تعالى، وانتصاراً له .
والفقهاء المجتهدون بدلالة هذه النصوص وأمثالها المتصلة بحكمة التشريع وسياسته والتي ترشد إلى مبدأ سد الذرائع، قد لجؤوا إلى هذا المبدأ في أحكام اجتهادية قرروها على أساسه :
(1) - فمن ذلك ما قرره الاجتهاد في أكثر من مذهب من أنَّ للزوجة المطلقة في طلاق الفرار حق الإرث من زوجها الذي طلقها، لكيلا يتخذ من حقه في الطلاق ذريعة إلى حرمان الزوجة ميراثها المشروع عند يأسه من الحياة بقصد المضارة.
وهذا من قبيل منع التعسف في استعمال الحق، إذ ليس من المصلحة تمكين الناس من هذا التعسف.
- وكذلك قرر الفقهاء أن البائع إذا شرط في عقد البيع أنه يتبرأ من أَنَّ ضمان العيوب الخفية التي يمكن أن تظهر في الشيء المبيع، فإنه عندئذ لا يكون مسؤولاً تجاه المشتري عما يظهر في المبيع من عيوب، عملاً بالشرط .
ولكن إذا كان البائع عالماً بعيب المبيع وكتمه عن المشتري عند العقد فإن شرط البراءة من ضمان العيب لا يعفيه من هذا الضمان كيلا يتخذ مبدأ احترام الشروط التي يشترطها المتعاقدان ذريعة لأحدهما إلى الغش وسوء النية تحت حماية الشرط.
(۱) طلاق الفرار في اصطلاح المذهب الحنفي (وقد سماه قانون الأحوال الشخصية الجديد لدينا طلاق (التعسف هو أن يطلق الرجل زوجته طلاقاً بائناً دون رضاها في مرض موته، بقصد الفرار من حقها المشروع في الميراث وحرمانها منه. فأوجبوا لها الميراث إذا توفي وهي في العدة سداً لهذا الطريق ولو كان من المحتمل أن الزوج غير قاصد هذا المقصد. Chapter 5: Subsidiary Sources: Istislah. The doer has good intention, so the Quran forbade insulting the idols of polytheists, even though the one who insults them does so out of faith in Allah and victory for Him. The mujtahid jurists, guided by these texts and their like related to the wisdom of legislation and its policy, which lead to the principle of blocking the means (sadd al-dhara'i), resorted to this principle in ijtihad rulings they established on its basis: (1) Among which is what was established by ijtihad in more than one school: that the divorced wife in a fleeing divorce (talaq al-firar) has the right of inheritance from her husband who divorced her, so that he does not take his right to divorce as a means to deprive his wife of her lawful inheritance when he despairs of life, with the intent to harm. This falls under preventing abuse of rights, for it is not in the public interest to enable people to such abuse. Likewise, the jurists ruled that if the seller stipulates in the sale contract that he disclaims liability for hidden defects that may appear in the sold item, then he is not responsible towards the buyer for any defects that appear in the sold item, acting upon the condition. But if the seller knew of the defect and concealed it from the buyer at the time of contract, then the condition of exemption from liability does not exempt him from this liability, so that the principle of respecting conditions agreed upon by contractors is not taken as a means by one of them to cheat and bad faith under the protection of the condition. (Footnote) Fleeing divorce in Hanafi terminology (and the new personal status law calls it 'abusive divorce') is that a man divorces his wife irrevocably without her consent during his terminal illness, intending to flee from her lawful right to inheritance and deprive her thereof. They made inheritance obligatory for her if he dies while she is in the 'idda, to block this path, even if it is possible that the husband did not intend that objective. (See the appendix to this chapter for more detail). Section Two: Sources of Islamic Jurisprudence. Thus ruled 'Uthman ibn 'Affan (may Allah be pleased with him) in an incident that occurred during his caliphate; and the Maliki ijtihad is based upon it. (Footnote) This ruling agrees with what the Syrian Civil Code has stipulated in many instances, including: the seller's stipulation of non-liability for defects or for eviction, or a reduction of this liability, is void if the seller is in bad faith, i.e., concealing a defect or a third-party right while knowing about them. As for the Hanafi ijtihad, they permit the seller's stipulation of non-liability for defects and excuse him by virtue of it even if he is in bad faith, i.e., concealing the defect while knowing it. There is no doubt that the Maliki ijtihad in this is more appropriate and more in line with the wisdom of legislation and the objectives of Sharia.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage introduces the principle of sadd al-dhara'i (blocking the means to evil) as a subsidiary source of Islamic law under the broader concept of istislah (public interest). The author begins by explaining that the doer of an action may have good intention, yet the action may lead to harm, just as the Quran forbade insulting idols to prevent harm to faith. The jurists used this principle to derive two key rulings. First, in the case of 'fleeing divorce' (talaq al-firar), where a husband divorces his wife during terminal illness to deprive her of inheritance, the wife is still entitled to inherit, blocking the use of divorce as a means to harm. Second, regarding exemption clauses in sales: generally, a seller can stipulate exemption from liability for hidden defects, but if the seller knew of the defect and concealed it, the exemption does not protect him, preventing the condition from being a means to fraud. The author then notes that this ruling was applied by Caliph 'Uthman and adopted by the Maliki school. A footnote explains that the Hanafi school differs on the second point: they allow the exemption clause even if the seller is in bad faith, though the author prefers the Maliki view as more in line with Sharia objectives.

Key Terms
سد الذرائع (sadd al-dhara'i): مبدأ أصولي يمنع الأفعال المباحة التي قد تؤدي إلى محرم
طلاق الفرار (talaq al-firar): طلاق الرجل زوجته في مرض موته بقصد حرمانها من الميراث
شرط البراءة (shart al-bara'a): شرط في عقد البيع يتبرأ به البائع من ضمان العيوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) يبطل الطلاق وترث الزوجة
B) يقع الطلاق وترث الزوجة
C) يقع الطلاق ولا ترث
D) لا يقع الطلاق أصلاً

Key Takeaway
Sadd al-dhara'i blocks permissible means that predominantly lead to harm, as seen in talaq al-firar and exemption clauses, though its application varies between schools.

Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications
intermediate

The Hanbali Qa'ida on Maslahah Mursalah: Conditions and Limits
Qa'ida definition with dispute and conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanbali qa'ida that maslahah mursalah is subordinate to qiyas and cannot override definitive texts, and identify the three conditions for its validity.

Core Idea
Maslahah mursalah does not override definitive texts; it operates within qiyas and must serve daruriyyat, hajiyyat, or tahsiniyyat.

This lesson analyzes the Hanbali school's approach to istislah (public interest). Unlike the Maliki school which treats maslahah mursalah as an independent source, the Hanbalis consider it a type of qiyas. The passage also warns against the extreme view of al-Tufi and establishes safeguards to prevent subjective abuse of public interest.

Guided Path
1
Identify the qawida
State the Hanbali rule: maslahah mursalah is a type of qiyas, not an independent source.
This distinguishes the Hanbali view from the Maliki.
2
Understand the conditions
The maslahah must be mursalah (no text), rational, and serve one of the three maqasid categories (daruriyyat, hajiyyat, tahsiniyyat).
These conditions prevent arbitrary rulings.
3
Apply the exception
If a definitive text exists, it takes precedence; this is the istithna'.
The qawida 'النص القطعي مقدم على المصلحة المرسلة' governs.
4
Distinguish from Maliki and al-Tufi's view
Compare with the Maliki independent source and the extreme view that maslahah can override texts.
The majority reject the extreme view.
5
Derive rulings for new cases
Using the qawida, determine permissibility/prohibition of transactions or harmful activities.
E.g., new financial contracts or pollution regulations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٥) المصادر التبعية : الاستصلاح

٥/۱۷ - ثم جاء الاجتهاد الحنبلي فنحا منحى الاجتهاد المالكي في اعتبار المصالح أصلاً يعتمد عليه في تقرير الأحكام.
ففي هذا الاجتهاد يستطيع الفقيه أن يحكم بأن كل عمل فيه مصلحة غالبة يعتبر مطلوباً شرعاً من غير أن يحتاج إلى شاهد خاص من نصوص الشرع لهذا النوع ؛ وكل أمر ضرره وإثمه أكبر من نفعه فهو منهي عنه من غير أن يحتاج إلى نص خاص.
ولكن الحنابلة على التحقيق، لا يعتبرون المصالح المرسلة مصدراً مستقلاً بذاته كما يعتبرها المالكية بل يراها الحنابلة ضَرْباً من ضروب القياس تابعة له وهي في مرتبته اعتباراً (ر)) كتاب «ابن حنبل» للاستاذ أبي زهرة أيضاً ص / ٣٠٣/ الطبعة الأولى).

١٨/٥ - وقد غالى سليمان الطوفي في اعتبار المصلحة، فجعلها مقدمة على النصوص القطعية إذا عارضتها (۱) . وهذا رأي خطر جداً يؤدي إلى تعطيل النصوص التشريعية بنظر اجتهادي محض. ولو جاز أن تتقبل أمة من الأمم هذا الرأي على إطلاقه في تشريعها وتسمح به لرجل الحقوق والقضاء في اجتهادهم السادت الفوضى في العمل بالشريعة والقانون، فمن تراءت له مصلحة في القانون عمل به ومن تصوّر فيه مفسدة نبذه وفي ذلك منتهى الاضطراب والتخبط (۱) .

لذلك وجدنا أئمة فقهاء الشريعة الإسلامية مجمعين على رفض هذا الغلو؛ ورأينا أنَّ الذين أخذوا بقاعدة الاستصلاح قد أقاموا في طريق تمييز من المفسدة، وتحديد حدودهما ضوابط كفيلة بهذا التمييز والتحديد مستمدة من مقاصد الشرع العامة التي تسمى اليوم غرض الشارع، وذلك لمنع الفوضى في الفهم ،والتقدير لأن المصلحة والمفسدة يجب تحديدهما وفقاً لنظر الشرع واعتباره لا للنظر الذاتي أو الفردي كما سلف بيانه در ف ٣/٥ - ٤ ).
(۱) نقل ذلك المحقق الشيخ محمد أبو زهرة في كتابه (مالك) الذي أصدره سنة / ١٣٦٥هـ / وعد فيه الطوفي من الحنابلة . لما أخرج الأستاذ أبو زهرة كتابه ابن حنبل» حقق فيه أن الطوفي شيعي يقول في ذلك بما تقول الشيعة الإمامية. لكن الأستاذ السيد عبد الحسين شرف الدين من شيوخ الشيعة الإمامية في جبل عامل كتب مقالة في مجلة العرفان (عدد) رجب / ١٣٧٣هـ يبرىء فيه مذهب الإمامية من رأي الطوفي، ويعده من الغلاة (ر : المدخل إلى علم أصول الفقه للزميل الأستاذ المحقق معروف الدواليبي ص/ ٢١٦ - ٢١٧ الطبعة الثانية) . وبعد ذلك أخرج الأستاذ الدكتور مصطفى زيد من دار العلوم رسالته القيمة عن المصالح المرسلة، حقق فيها تحقيقاً دقيقاً بالأدلة التاريخية والنقلية أن الطوفي لم يكن شيعياً، وإنما اتهمه بذلك خصومه حسداً، وهو حنبلي المذهب ولكنه حر التفكير

الباب الثاني : مصادر الفقه الإسلامي

وقد رأينا أيضاً أن في طليعة تلك الضوابط التي أقيمت لمنع التخبط من طريق الاستصلاح أن تكون المصلحة المبني عليها الحكم مصلحة مرسلة، أي لا يوجد في الشريعة نص عليها آمر أو ناه، يتناولها بعينها أو نوعها، وأن يكون فيها دعم لإحدى الزمر الثلاث من الأحكام المنظمة للحياة الاجتماعية وهي الضروريات والحاجيات، والتحسينيات.
فإذا عارض المصلحة نص شرعي فالعلماء عندئذ لا يرجحونها على النص ترجيحاً مطلقاً كما يريد (الطوفي)، بل لهم تجاهها موقف تفصيلي سنراه قريباً (ر: ف ٢٠/٥ - ٢٥).
هذا مقام الاستحسان والاستصلاح في هذه الاجتهادات الأربعة (٢). شك أن الأخذ بالمنهاج الذي سلكه فقهاء الحنفية والمالكية والحنابلة يجعل
(۱) ر : المدخل إلى علم أصول الفقه أيضاً ٢١٦ - ٠٢١٩ .۲۱۹
(۲) مما يلحظ في هذا الشأن أنَّ الإمام الشافعي تلميذ الإمام مالك، وأن الإمام أحمد حنبل تلميذ الشافعي .
Chapter (5) Subsidiary Sources: Istislah. 5/17 - Then the Hanbali ijtihad came, following the Maliki ijtihad in considering masalih as a basis relied upon in determining rulings. In this ijtihad, the faqih can rule that every action with a prevailing benefit is considered legally required without needing a specific textual evidence for this type; and every matter whose harm and sin is greater than its benefit is prohibited without needing a specific text. But the Hanbalis, upon verification, do not consider al-masalih al-mursalah as an independent source in itself as the Malikis consider them; rather, the Hanbalis see it as a type of qiyas, subordinate to it and in its rank of consideration (ref. Ibn Hanbal book by Abu Zahra, p. 303). 5/18 - And Sulayman al-Tufi exaggerated in considering maslahah, making it take precedence over definitive texts if they conflict (1). This is a very dangerous opinion that leads to the nullification of legislative texts by pure ijtihadi opinion. And if any nation were to accept this opinion absolutely in its legislation and allow its men of law and judiciary to do so in their ijtihad, chaos would reign in the application of Sharia and law; whoever sees a benefit in a law would act by it, and whoever perceives harm would discard it, and that would be the utmost disorder and confusion (1). Therefore, we found the imams of Islamic jurists unanimously rejecting this extremism; and we saw that those who adopted the principle of istislah established safeguards in the way of distinguishing it from harm and defining their limits, derived from the general maqasid of the Sharia, which today is called the purpose of the Lawgiver, to prevent chaos in understanding and assessment, because benefit and harm must be determined according to the view and consideration of the Sharia, not according to subjective or individual opinion, as previously explained (ref. para 3/5-4). (1) The investigator Sheikh Muhammad Abu Zahra narrated that in his book 'Malik', he counted al-Tufi among the Hanbalis. When Abu Zahra published his book 'Ibn Hanbal', he investigated that al-Tufi was a Shi'i, saying what the Imami Shi'as say. But al-Sayyid Abd al-Husayn Sharaf al-Din, a prominent Shi'i scholar in Jabal Amil, wrote an article in al-Irfan magazine (Rajab 1373 AH) exonerating the Imami school from al-Tufi's opinion, and considered him among the extremists (ref. al-Madkhal ila Ilm Usul al-Fiqh by Ma'ruf al-Dawalibi, p. 216-217). Later, Dr. Mustafa Zaid from Dar al-Ulum published his valuable thesis on al-masalih al-mursalah, verifying precisely with historical and transmitted evidence that al-Tufi was not a Shi'i, but his opponents accused him out of envy; he was Hanbali in madhhab but free-thinking. Part Two: Sources of Islamic Fiqh. And we also saw that among the foremost of those safeguards established to prevent confusion through istislah is that the maslahah on which the ruling is based must be a maslahah mursalah, i.e., there is no text in the Sharia commanding or prohibiting it, addressing it specifically or by type, and that it supports one of the three categories of rulings regulating social life: the necessities (daruriyyat), the needs (hajiyyat), and the improvements (tahsiniyyat). If a maslahah conflicts with a legal text, then scholars do not prefer it absolutely as al-Tufi would have, but they have a detailed stance towards it that we will see soon (ref. para 5/20-25). This is the place of istihsan and istislah in these four ijtihads (2). There is no doubt that adopting the method followed by Hanafi, Maliki, and Hanbali jurists makes... (1) See al-Madkhal ila Ilm Usul al-Fiqh also, pp. 216-219. (2) It may be noted in this regard that Imam al-Shafi'i was a student of Imam Malik, and Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal was a student of al-Shafi'i.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage discusses maslahah mursalah (unrestricted public interest) as a subsidiary source of Islamic law, focusing on the Hanbali approach. The author explains that Hanbali ijtihad follows the Maliki method in considering masalih as a basis for rulings, but with a key difference: the Hanbalis do not treat maslahah mursalah as an independent source; rather, they consider it a type of qiyas (analogy) and subordinate to it. The passage then criticizes the extreme position of Sulayman al-Tufi, who held that maslahah can override definitive texts (nusus qati'ah). The author deems this dangerous because it leads to arbitrary application of law, where judges might follow personal subjective views. The jumhur (majority) of scholars reject this, and those who accept istislah have imposed conditions: the maslahah must be mursalah (not directly addressed by a text), rational, and must serve one of the three maqasid categories: daruriyyat (essentials), hajiyyat (needs), or tahsiniyyat (embellishments). If a maslahah conflicts with a text, scholars do not give it absolute precedence but adopt a detailed approach (to be discussed later). The footnote clarifies that al-Tufi was originally considered Hanbali, but later research suggests he held extreme views; some accused him of Shi'ism, but Dr. Mustafa Zaid argues he was a free-thinking Hanbali.

Key Terms
الاستصلاح (al-istiṣlāḥ): A method of legal reasoning that derives rulings based on consideration of public interest (maslahah) not explicitly regulated by a text.
المصلحة المرسلة (al-maslaḥah al-mursalah): A benefit or interest that is not supported by a specific textual evidence (nass) either commanding or prohibiting, but is compatible with the general objectives of Sharia.
الضروريات والحاجيات والتحسينيات (al-ḍarūriyyāt wa al-ḥājiyyāt wa al-taḥsīniyyāt): The three categories of maqasid al-Shariah: necessities (essential for life and religion), needs (things that remove hardship), and improvements (embellishments and conveniences).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanbali qa'ida presented, which of the following is a valid application of istislah?
A) Prohibiting a new harmful drug based on a hadith that says 'do not harm'.
B) Permitting a new beneficial contract because it benefits the poor, even though a hadith prohibits something similar.
C) Allowing a practice that contradicts a clear Quranic verse because it serves a greater public good.
D) Using analogy to derive a ruling for a new case where no text exists, based on public interest.

Key Takeaway
Maslahah mursalah is a valid tool in Hanbali fiqh but strictly conditioned: it must be mursalah, rational, serve a maqsad, and never override a definitive text.

Takhsis of Non-Definitive Texts by Maslaha: Madhhab Positions
Comparing Hanbali, Maliki, and Hanafi approaches to specifying texts by maslaha.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between the Hanbali and Maliki/Hanafi positions on specifying a non-definitive text by maslaha, and apply the relevant legal maxim to a furu' case.

Core Idea
Maslaha can specify a non-definitive text when conflict arises, but the Hanbali school prioritizes textual generality over maslaha.

This lesson explores two opposing trends among the schools regarding the use of maslaha mursala to specify a text (takhsis al-nass). The Hanbali school refuses to specify even non-definitive texts by maslaha, while the Maliki and Hanafi schools permit it as an interpretative tool. Understanding this difference is crucial for recognizing how each school balances textual literalism with purposive reasoning in ijtihad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two trends
Read the passage and distinguish the Hanbali position (no takhsis by maslaha even for zanni texts) from the second trend (Maliki/Hanafi: maslaha specifies zanni texts when conflict exists).
The passage states: 'first trend: does not accept... second trend: its proponents hold that maslaha specifies the text'.
2
Analyze the Hanbali rationale
The Hanbali position is based on prioritizing the text's generality and restricting maslaha to cases where no text exists. Understand that they see the text as sufficient evidence, leaving no room for maslaha.
3
Analyze the Maliki/Hanafi rationale
They view maslaha as evidence of the Lawgiver's intent, allowing specification of non-definitive texts. Note the Maliki example: a defendant is not required to take an oath unless there is a prior business relationship, to prevent exploitation.
The passage emphasizes that specification is not naskh but interpretative narrowing.
4
Extract the legal maxim
Formulate the maxim from the second trend: 'Maslaha specifies a non-definitive text when there is conflict' (المصلحة تخصص النص غير القطعي عند التعارض). Identify its rank (wusta) and scope.
The qawida_panel in the toolbox provides this maxim.
5
Apply the maxim to a new furu'
Using the Maliki example as a model, consider another scenario where a literal text would cause harm. Apply the maxim to derive the ruling, noting any istithna' (e.g., Hanbali exception).
Practice tasks will provide a structured application.

أما الاجتهادات التي تأخذ بنظرية الاستصلاح، ففيها اتجاهان : الاتجاه الأول : لا يقبل تخصيص النص بمقتضى المصلحة المرسلة ولو كان النص غير قطعي، وهذا هو اتجاه الاجتهاد الحنبلي. فدلالة النص وعمومه مقدَّمان في مذهب أحمد بن حنبل على النظر إلى المصلحة، لأنَّ محل النظر إليها إنما هو عند فقدان النص، ولا مجال للنظر إليها عند وجوده (ر ) كتاب ابن حنبل للأستاذ الشيخ محمد أبي ر) ۱۳۰

والاتجاه الثاني : يرى أصحابه أنَّ المصلحة تخصص النص عند التعارض. أي أنهم يرون إعمال النص في الحالات التي لا يتنافى فيها مع تلك المصلحة المعارضة، إذ يرون في هذه المصلحة الموزونة بالمقاييس الشرعية دليلاً على أنَّ الشارع إنما يريد بنصه أن يطبق فيما لا تقتضي المصلحة خلافه (۱)
ويتجلى هذا الموقف في تخصيص النص غير القطعي بمقتضى المصلحة عند التعارض في الاجتهادين الحنفي والمالكي بصورة خاصة، كما نرى فيما يلي:
٢٣/٥ - في المذهب المالكي :
فالاجتهاد المالكي، وهو المشهور باعتماد المصالح المرسلة أكثر من غيره من المقرر فيه أنَّ المصلحة تخصص النصوص غير القطعية - ومنها النصوص العامة جميعاً في نظر المالكية - عند التعارض. أمثلة ذلك عند المالكية ما لو ادعى أحد على آخر مالاً وعجز عن الإثبات، وطلب تحليف المدعى عليه اليمين، فإن مالكاً لا يوجب تحليف المُدَّعى عليه إلا إذا كان بينه وبين المدَّعي خلطة، نظراً للمصلحة ودرءاً للاستغلال الباطل كي لا يتجرأ السفهاء على الفضلاء فيجروهم إلى المحاكم بدعاوى كاذبة موهومة أمام الناس فيستغلوا كراهيتهم للحلف ليبتزوا أموالهم افتداء من اليمين.
(۱) يجب أن يلحظ هنا أن تخصيص النص التشريعي ليس تغييراً للحكم المقرر فيه، لأن التغيير نسخ لا يملكه إلا من له حق التشريع، وإنما التخصيص عند علماء أصول الفقه هو قصر النص على بعض ما يشمله لفظه على سبيل التفسير والبيان لمراد الشارع، لا على سبيل التغيير.
۱۳۱
As for the ijtihads that adopt the theory of istislah, there are two trends: The first trend: it does not accept specification of a text by virtue of maslaha mursala, even if the text is non-definitive. This is the trend of Hanbali ijtihad. The indication of the text and its generality are given priority in the school of Ahmad ibn Hanbal over considering maslaha, because the place for considering it is only when there is no text, and there is no room for considering it when the text exists. (Ref. Ibn Hanbal's book by Professor Sheikh Muhammad Abu R...) The second trend: its proponents hold that maslaha specifies the text when there is conflict. That is, they see applying the text in cases where it does not conflict with that opposing maslaha, as they consider this maslaha, weighed by Shariah standards, as evidence that the Lawgiver intends His text to apply only where maslaha does not require otherwise. This position is manifested in specifying a non-definitive text by virtue of maslaha in cases of conflict, particularly in Hanafi and Maliki ijtihad, as we see below. In the Maliki school: Maliki ijtihad, which is famous for relying on masalih mursala more than others, establishes that maslaha specifies non-definitive texts – including all general texts in the opinion of the Malikis – when there is conflict. Examples of that according to the Malikis: if someone claims property from another and is unable to prove it, and requests that the defendant be made to take an oath, then Malik does not require the defendant to take an oath unless there is a business relationship (khulta) between him and the claimant, for the sake of maslaha and to prevent false exploitation, so that foolish people do not dare to drag noble people to courts with false illusory claims before people, exploiting their reluctance to take an oath to extort their money to avoid the oath. (1) It should be noted here that specification of a legislative text is not a change of the ruling established in it, because change is abrogation (naskh) which no one has the right to except those with legislative authority; rather specification according to usul al-fiqh scholars is confining the text to part of what its wording includes by way of interpretation and clarification of the Lawgiver's intent, not by way of change.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents two contrasting trends among schools regarding the use of maslaha mursala (unrestricted public interest) to specify a text (takhsis al-nass). The first trend, attributed to the Hanbali school, rejects specification by maslaha even for non-definitive texts (zanni). For them, the text's literal meaning and generality prevail, and maslaha is only considered when there is no text at all. The second trend, exemplified by the Maliki and Hanafi schools, permits specification of non-definitive texts by maslaha when there is conflict. The Maliki example: if someone claims money from another but cannot prove it, the defendant is not required to take an oath unless there is a pre-existing relationship (khulta) between them. This is to prevent frivolous lawsuits and exploitation of people's aversion to oaths. The author also clarifies that such specification is not naskh (abrogation) but merely an interpretative narrowing of the text's scope to align with the Lawgiver's intent as evidenced by maslaha. This lesson illustrates how different schools prioritize textual vs. purposive reasoning.

Key Terms
تخصيص النص (takhsis al-nass): Confining a text to part of its literal scope based on evidence of the Lawgiver's intent, not changing the ruling
المصلحة المرسلة (al-maslaha al-mursala): An interest not explicitly regulated by Shariah text but consistent with its spirit
النص غير القطعي (al-nass ghayr al-qat'i): A text that is not definitively established or whose meaning is not definitive (zanni al-thubut or zanni al-dalala)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Maliki example in the text, when does a defendant not have to take an oath in a property claim?
A) Always, unless the claimant has proof
B) Only if there is a prior business relationship between claimant and defendant
C) Only if the defendant is known to be honest
D) The defendant always has to take an oath

Key Takeaway
Specification by maslaha is an accepted tool for non-definitive texts in the Hanafi and Maliki schools, but rejected by the Hanbali school.

Definition and Evidentiary Value of Custom ('Urf) in Hanafi Fiqh
Defines 'urf and establishes its evidentiary status with conditions and proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi legal maxim 'Custom is only considered if it is consistent or predominant' to determine whether a given practice qualifies as binding custom ('urf).

Core Idea
Custom is a valid secondary source only if it is consistent or predominant — occasional practices lack legal weight.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, custom ('urf) serves as a secondary source when primary texts are silent. But not every common practice becomes law: the jurists set a clear threshold. This lesson unpacks the conditions from the Majalla maxim and shows how custom acquires evidentiary value in Islamic rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its conditions
Identify the Hanafi legal maxim from the Majalla (Article 41): 'Custom is only considered if it is consistent or predominant.' Note that the maxim uses 'adah synonymously with 'urf. The two conditions are ittirād (consistency) and ghalabah (predominance); only one need be fulfilled.
The Arabic: إنما تعتبر العادة إذا اطردت أو غلبت
2
Define and differentiate the two conditions
'Consistency' means the practice is continuous and universal among the community in that locale. 'Predominance' means it is practiced by the overwhelming majority, even if not absolutely universal. A practice that is occasional or confined to a few individuals is not binding custom.
The text emphasizes that 'otherwise it would be an individual action, not a custom.'
3
Apply the maxim to a furū' case
Take the example from the passage: the Syrian dower (mahr) custom where two-thirds is prompt and one-third deferred. Since this practice is predominant in the region, it qualifies as binding custom. In a marriage contract, unless explicitly agreed otherwise, the deferred portion is payable upon death or divorce.
This furū' is from the chapter of Nikah.
4
Identify the istithnā' (exception)
The maxim has a clear limit: custom cannot override a definitive text (nass qat'i) from the Quran or mutawatir Sunnah. In such cases, the text takes precedence, governed by the maxim 'لا اجتهاد مع النص' (No ijtihad in the presence of a text).
This ensures that custom does not contradict fundamental revealed rulings.
5
Understand the proof from the Quran
The passage cites Quran 7:199: 'Take forgiveness, command what is customary ('urf), and turn away from the ignorant.' While the word 'urf here is linguistic, not technical, it provides strong support (isti'nas) for considering the good and familiar practices of people as a source of law.
The Hanafi approach: the verse shows that the Shariah approves of sound customary practices.

Source Text

ومن ثم يسمى أيضاً: «عادة» «تعاملا» .
ويفهم من هذا التعريف أنه لا يتحقق وجود العرف في أمر من الأمور إلا إذا كان مطّرداً بين الناس في المكان الجاري فيه، أو غالباً بحيث يكون معظم أهل هذا العرف كل منهم يرعاه ويجري على وفقه؛ كتعارف الناس مثلاً في بلاد الشام أن المهر الذي يسمى للمرأة في عقد النكاح يكون ثلثاه معجلاً وثلثه مؤجلاً إلى ما بعد الوفاة أو الطلاق. فيجب أن يتحقق في تكوين العرف اعتياد مشترك بين الجمهور وهذا لا يكون إلا في الحالة الاطراد أو الغلبة على الأقل، وإلا كان تصرفاً فردياً لا عرفاً .
وعن هذا وضع الفقهاء القاعدة القائلة : إنما تعتبر العادة إذا اطردت أو غلبت (م/ ٤١) .
٢/٦ - شأن العرف بين مصادر الأحكام.
في الحياة الاجتماعية لدى الأقوام الذين لا شريعة عندهم تكون الأعراف والعادات هي الشريعة التي يحتكم إليها.
ومن ثم نعلم ما للعرف من حاكمية قديمة بين البشر.
وبما أن بعض الأعراف قد تكون في ذاتها حسنة عادلة، أو قبيحة جائرة، كان من جملة أهداف الشرائع التي تسن أن تقر العرف الحسن.
وتنهى عن القبيح
(1)
(۱) يعد علماء القانون مصادر الشرائع الوضعية - أي القوانين التي تضعها الأمم لأنفسها
خمسة، وهي:
۱ - العرف ٢ - الدين ٣ - آراء الفقهاء وشُرّاح القوانين ٤ - اجتهاد القضاء، أي أحكام المحاكم . ه - قواعد العدل والانصاف. فمن هذه المصادر الخمسة تستمد الأحكام الوضعية التي تصاغ قوانين يلزم بها الناس بينهم، كما يرجع إليها القضاء فيما لم تتناولها القوانين بالنص.
ويجري عليها القضاء
هذا ويلحظ أن للأحكام التي تستمد من العرف مزيتين:
إحداهما - أن الناس يكونون على علم سابق في معاملاتهم فيقل اختلافهم. لأن جهل الناس بالأحكام مما يولد المشكلات بينهم.
الفصل (٦) المصادر التبعية : العرف
والعرف والعادات إلى اليوم تعد في نظر الحقوقيين مصدراً من أهم المصادر للقوانين الوضعية ذاتها، فيستمد منه واضعوها كثيراً من الأحكام المتعارفة، ويبرزونها في صورة نصوص قانونية يُزال بها الغموض والإبهام الذي لا يجليه العُزف في بعض الحالات.
والشريعة الإسلامية كذلك جاءت فأقرت كثيراً من التصرفات والحقوق المتعارفة بين العرب والإسلام، وهذبت كثيراً ونهت عن كثير، كما أتت بأحكام جديدة استوعبت بها تنظيم الحقوق والالتزامات بين الناس في حياتهم الاجتماعية على أساس وفاء الحاجة والمصلحة والتوجيه إلى أفضل الحلول والنظم لأنَّ الشرائع الإلهية إنما تبغي بأحكامها المدنية تنظيم مصالح البشر وحقوقهم فتقر من متعارف الناس ما تراه محققاً لغايتها، ملائماً لأسسها وأساليبها .
٣/٦ - الدليل الشرعي على اعتبار العرف:
يذكر بعض العلماء الأدلة الشرعية في الإسلام على اعتبار مكانة من العرف الفقهية في إثبات الأحكام قوله تعالى في الآية السالفة البيان: خُذِ الْعَفْوَ وَأْمُرْ بِالْعُرْفِ وَأَعْرِضْ عَنِ الجهلين (۱۹۹/۷)
ولا يخفى أنَّ العرف في هذه الآية واقع على معناه اللغوي وهو الأمر المستحسن المألوف، لا على معناه الاصطلاحي الفقهي. ولكن توجيه هذا الاستدلال هو أن العرف في الآية، وإن لم يكن مراداً به المعنى
الاصطلاحي، قد يُسْتَأْنَسُ به في تأييد اعتبار العرف بمعناه الاصطلاحي لأنَّ عُرْف الناس في أعمالهم ومعاملاتهم هو مما استحسنوه وألفته عقولهم. والغالب أن عرف القوم دليل على حاجتهم إلى الأمر المتعارف فاعتباره يكون من الأمور المستحسنة.
والثانية - أن تلك الأحكام تكون مألوفة مستساغة ومقبولة، لأنهم قد اعتادوها قبل أن تصبح قانوناً.
Chapter Two: Sources of Islamic Jurisprudence. And it is also called: 'adah, ta'amul. From this definition it is understood that the existence of custom in a matter is not realized unless it is consistent among the people in the place where it is practiced, or predominant, such that most of the people of this custom each observe it and act according to it; like the mutual understanding of people, for example, in the Levant that the dower mentioned for the woman in the marriage contract is two-thirds prompt and one-third deferred until after death or divorce. So it is necessary in the formation of custom that there be a shared habitual practice among the majority, and this is only achieved in the case of consistency or predominance at least, otherwise it would be an individual action, not a custom. And regarding this, the jurists laid down the maxim: 'Custom is only considered if it is consistent or predominant' (Majalla 41). 2/6 - The Status of Custom among the Sources of Rulings. In social life among peoples who have no revealed law, customs and habits are the law to which they refer. Hence we know the ancient authority of custom among humans. Since some customs may be in themselves good and just, or evil and oppressive, it was among the objectives of the laws that are enacted to approve good custom and forbid the evil. (1) (1) Legal scholars count the sources of positive law—i.e., the laws that nations make for themselves—as five: 1. Custom, 2. Religion, 3. Opinions of jurists and commentators of laws, 4. Judicial ijtihad i.e., court rulings, 5. Rules of justice and equity. From these five sources are derived the positive rulings that are formulated into laws binding people among themselves, and the judiciary refers to them in matters not covered by textual provisions. And the judiciary applies them. And it is observed that the rulings derived from custom have two advantages: First - People have prior knowledge of them in their transactions, so their disputes are reduced, because people's ignorance of rulings generates problems among them. Chapter (6): Secondary Sources: Custom. And custom and habits are still considered by jurists as one of the most important sources of positive law itself; the legislators derive from it many common rulings, and they highlight them in the form of legal texts by which ambiguity and obscurity that custom does not clarify in some cases are removed. And Islamic law likewise came and approved many of the common practices and rights among the Arabs and Islam, refined many, forbade many, and brought new rulings by which it encompassed the organization of rights and obligations among people in their social life on the basis of fulfilling need and interest and guiding toward the best solutions and systems, because the divine laws aim by their civil rulings to organize human interests and rights, so they approve from people's common practices what they see as fulfilling their purpose, suitable to their foundations and methods. 3/6 - The Legal Evidence for Considering Custom: Some scholars mention the legal proofs in Islam for considering the status of custom in establishing rulings, the Almighty's statement in the previously mentioned verse: 'Take forgiveness, command what is customary ('urf), and turn away from the ignorant' (7:199). And it is not hidden that 'urf in this verse occurs in its linguistic meaning, which is the good and familiar matter, not in its technical legal meaning. But the direction of this deduction is that 'urf in the verse, even if it is not intended in the technical sense, may be used as a source of support in affirming the consideration of custom in its technical sense, because the 'urf of people in their works and transactions is what they have deemed good and their minds have become familiar with. And generally, the custom of a people is evidence of their need for the familiar matter, so considering it is among the approved matters. And the second - that those rulings are familiar, palatable, and acceptable, because they have become accustomed to them before they become law.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage, from the introduction to the chapter on secondary sources, defines 'urf (custom) as a legal source in Islamic fiqh. The author first clarifies that custom requires a condition of consistency (istirad) or predominance (ghalabah) among the people in a given locality – individual or rare practices do not constitute binding custom. The example of the Syrian marriage dower (mahr) illustrates this. The passage then cites the famous legal maxim from the Majalla: 'Custom is only considered if it is consistent or predominant' (Article 41). The author then discusses the role of custom historically and in positive law, noting that even man-made legal systems recognize custom as a primary source. Islamic law, he explains, affirmed good pre-Islamic customs, refined others, and prohibited the evil ones, all while introducing new regulations. The legal evidence for custom is drawn from Quran 7:199, 'command what is customary'. While the term 'urf in this verse is used linguistically, it provides indirect support because the general good and familiar practices of people carry weight in Islamic law. Finally, the author notes two advantages of ruling by custom: (1) people already know the rules, reducing disputes, and (2) the rules are naturally acceptable because they are already practiced.

Key Terms
العرف (al-‘urf): Custom: a recurring practice that is widely accepted by a community and is consistent or predominant in that place.
العادة (al-‘ādah): Habit: synonymous with ‘urf in this context, used in the legal maxim.
الاطراد (al-ittirād): Consistency: the custom is continuous and not occasional.
الغلبة (al-ghalabah): Predominance: the custom is practiced by the majority of people in that location.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a necessary condition for a practice to be considered binding ‘urf (custom) in Hanafi fiqh?
A) The practice must be recorded in writing.
B) The practice must be consistent (مطرد) or predominant (غالب).
C) The practice must be approved by the ruler.
D) The practice must be followed for at least one hundred years.

Key Takeaway
Custom is a valid secondary source in Hanafi fiqh only if it is consistent or predominant and does not contradict a definitive text.

Custom as a Source: Seven Practical Applications
Listing seven furuat where custom governs the ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list seven fiqh scenarios where custom determines the ruling and explain why custom is binding in each.

Core Idea
Custom fills the gaps where texts are silent — in marriage, sale, hire, and more.

In this passage, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa presents custom as a dynamic source of Islamic law, providing concrete examples from different chapters of fiqh. These examples illustrate how Hanafi jurists relied on custom to decide cases where no explicit textual ruling exists.

Guided Path
1
Identify the role of 'urf
Recognize that custom is a secondary source of Islamic law, used in the absence of explicit text.
Custom is not a primary source; it operates within the framework of Shari'ah.
2
Analyse each example
For each of the seven cases (a-g), identify what detail custom supplies (e.g., amount of mahr, price, defect definition).
The text lists examples from nikah, buyu', ijarah, wadi'ah, and shirkat.
3
Extract the common principle
In all cases, custom operates where the contract leaves a matter unspecified or ambiguous.
Custom acts as a default rule.
4
Note the absolute authority
The text says custom has 'absolute authority in judgment' within its domain, i.e., where no nass exists.
This authority is limited to areas where the Lawgiver has not spoken.

Chapter Two: Sources of Islamic Jurisprudence. From these rules will come an explanation of these rules, along with the rest of the universal rules, in the last section of this introduction. And consider the subject of this last rule what preceded in the discussion of istislah (Chapter 5) and what will come at the end of the theory of custom (F 1/76 - 3/78). And on the basis of these rules, the jurists branched out and decided countless secondary rulings in various jurisprudential chapters and transactions: A - The division of the woman's dowry, for example, into immediate and deferred in marriage, and the amount of each if not specified in the marriage contract, reference is made to custom. B - The division of the price of the sold item and the hire of the hired object if the contractors did not specify it and there was a prevailing custom in the town of the contract, both parties are bound by the custom. C - What is considered a defect in the sold item justifying rescission of the sale or not considered a defect is only judged by custom. D - The lessee's exceeding the permissible limits in using the hired object so that he is considered a transgressor and liable for its value if it perishes, its standard is custom. E - How to safeguard the deposit such that the depositee is considered negligent in its preservation and thus liable if it is lost, or not negligent and not liable, is also considered by custom. F - The dispute between the teacher and the working apprentice in crafts as to who deserves wages from the other is referred to the custom of the town (Majalla 596). G - Likewise in hire, everything that is a corollary of the work and was not stipulated upon the worker is considered by the custom of the town (Majalla 574). And other endless cases with which the chapters of fiqh are filled, and they gave custom absolute authority in judgment. And it is needless to say that rulings built on custom change with the change of custom. Hence the aforementioned maxim was formulated: It cannot be denied that rulings change with the change of time. Likewise, custom is not applied to a matter unless this custom existed and was current at the time of the originating transaction. Custom arising after a transaction does not apply to it. This, and since we will dedicate a research on the theory of custom and its principles and branches in the second section devoted to general jurisprudential theories of this part, we suffice here with this comprehensive idea about custom and its position in the sources of Islamic jurisprudence, referring for details to the research of its theory in its place indicated.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the role of custom ('urf) as a secondary source in Islamic jurisprudence, particularly in the Hanafi school. It begins by referencing earlier discussions on the rules and then provides a list of practical applications (furuat) from various chapters of fiqh, such as marriage (mahr), sales (defects, price), hire (lessee's liability), deposit (negligence), and crafts (wages). All these are governed by custom. The key principle is that rulings based on custom change as custom changes, leading to the well-known Hanafi maxim: 'It cannot be denied that rulings change with the change of time.' The passage also specifies that only customs existing at the time of the transaction are considered; later customs do not retroactively apply. This is a characteristic Hanafi approach, emphasizing the flexibility of law in light of changing societal norms.

Key Terms
العرف (al-'urf): Custom; the prevailing practice in a society that is accepted as a binding source in areas where no explicit text exists.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, if the amount of dower (mahr) is not specified in the marriage contract, how is it determined?
A) It is determined by the bride's family
B) It is determined by the custom of the locality
C) It is determined by the minimum mahr set by the school
D) It is determined by the groom's wealth

Key Takeaway
Custom is a binding secondary source in Hanafi fiqh, filling gaps where no explicit text exists, as demonstrated in seven key transactional scenarios.

The Maxim and Its Temporal Condition: Rulings Change with Time
Presenting the maxim and its temporal condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the maxim 'rulings change with time,' state its temporal condition, and apply it to a new scenario.

Core Idea
Rulings based on custom evolve as custom evolves, but only customs existing at the time of the transaction matter.

In the second part of the passage, Dr. al-Zarqa introduces the foundational maxim that rulings change with changing times, derived from the nature of custom. He then adds a crucial temporal condition: only customs current at the moment of the transaction are considered. This lesson unpacks both the maxim and its limitation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the causal link
Recognize that rulings built on custom change because custom itself changes; the ruling is contingent upon the custom.
2
Memorize the maxim
The maxim is: 'لا ينكر تغير الأحكام بتغير الزمان' (It cannot be denied that rulings change with the change of time).
This is a well-known Hanafi legal maxim.
3
Identify the temporal condition
Custom only applies if it was existing and current at the time of the originating transaction.
Custom arising after the transaction does not apply retroactively.
4
Apply the limitation
In practice, when a contract is formed, only the customs at that moment are relevant; later changes do not affect the contract.
This prevents instability in contractual obligations.

Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the role of custom ('urf) as a secondary source in Islamic jurisprudence, particularly in the Hanafi school. It begins by referencing earlier discussions on the rules and then provides a list of practical applications (furuat) from various chapters of fiqh, such as marriage (mahr), sales (defects, price), hire (lessee's liability), deposit (negligence), and crafts (wages). All these are governed by custom. The key principle is that rulings based on custom change as custom changes, leading to the well-known Hanafi maxim: 'It cannot be denied that rulings change with the change of time.' The passage also specifies that only customs existing at the time of the transaction are considered; later customs do not retroactively apply. This is a characteristic Hanafi approach, emphasizing the flexibility of law in light of changing societal norms.

Key Terms
العرف (al-'urf): Custom; the prevailing practice in a society that is accepted as a binding source in areas where no explicit text exists.
لا ينكر تغير الأحكام بتغير الزمان (la yunkar taghayyur al-ahkam bi taghayyur al-zaman): It cannot be denied that rulings change with the change of time; a Hanafi maxim indicating the dynamic nature of custom-based rules.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is custom considered in a transaction according to the passage?
A) At the time of the fatwa
B) At the time of the transaction
C) At the time of the dispute
D) At any time

Key Takeaway
Rulings based on custom are dynamic and change with custom, but only customs existing at the transaction's time are legally relevant.

Condition for the Validity of Custom (Urf)
Defining a key procedural condition for using custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for the validity of custom (urf) and apply it to distinguish whether a custom governs a given transaction or not.

Core Idea
A custom only governs a transaction if it existed at the time of the transaction; later customs are irrelevant.

In Hanafi jurisprudence, custom (urf) is a recognized subsidiary source of law, but its application is not arbitrary. The most critical condition is temporal: the custom must have been present and established at the moment the legal transaction occurred. This lesson focuses on that key restriction, ensuring that legal stability is maintained and that new norms are not retroactively imposed on past actions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
Read the passage carefully and note the explicit condition: custom is only considered if it was 'موجوداً وقائماً حين التصرف المنشىء' (present and existing at the time of the initiating transaction).
2
Understand the rationale
Consider why this condition exists: to prevent legal instability and to respect the expectations of parties at the time of the transaction. A later custom cannot retroactively alter the rights and obligations that were established earlier.
3
Apply to scenarios
Practice distinguishing: a custom that arose after a contract is signed does not apply to that contract; however, a custom that existed before and at the time of the contract may be used to interpret ambiguous terms or fill gaps.

Source Text

وكذلك لا يحكم العرف في أمر إلا إذا كان هذا العرف موجوداً وقائماً حين التصرف المنشىء . فالعرف الحادث بعد تصرف ما لا يسري عليه .
مستقلاً
هذا، وبما أننا سنفرد لنظرية العرف وما فيها من أصول وفروع بحثاً في القسم الثاني المخصَّص للنظريات الفقهية العامة من هذا الجزء، لذلك نجتزيء هنا بهذه الفكرة الإجمالية عن العُزف وموقعه في مصادر الفقه الإسلامي، محيلين بالتفصيل على بحث نظريته في موطنها المشار إليه .
Chapter (6): Secondary Sources: Custom. Likewise, custom is not considered in a matter unless this custom was present and existing at the time of the initiating transaction. A custom that arises after a transaction does not apply to it. However, since we will dedicate a separate treatment to the theory of custom and its principles and branches in the second section devoted to general jurisprudential theories of this part, we suffice here with this general idea about custom and its place in the sources of Islamic jurisprudence, referring the details to the discussion of its theory in its indicated location.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage presents a fundamental rule in the Hanafi treatment of custom (urf) as a subsidiary source of Islamic law. The author establishes a clear condition: a custom is only legally operative if it was already established and existing at the moment of the relevant legal transaction. Any custom that emerges after the transaction is not retroactively applied to it. This principle prevents the instability that would arise if later norms were imposed on past actions. The author then notes that a full theory of custom will be presented later in the work, indicating that this is a concise introduction. Students should remember this as a key restriction on the use of custom in fatwa and judging.

Key Terms
العرف (al-urf): Custom; a recurring practice accepted by people of sound nature and prevalent in their dealings, which is considered as a subsidiary source of law, provided it exists at the time of the transaction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Custom (urf) is only legally valid if it existed at the exact moment of the transaction; later customs have no effect on that transaction.

The Maxim 'What is Taken Must be Returned'
Hadith citation and authentication for the liability maxim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the hadith 'Ala al-yad ma akhadhat hatta tu'addiyahu', identify its authentication, and derive the Hanafi legal maxim of liability for possession.

Core Idea
Liability for what is taken extends to all possessions, not just stolen goods.

This lesson focuses on the foundational hadith for liability in Hanafi law: 'What is taken must be returned.' The maxim derived from it governs trusts, loans, and torts. Understanding its authentication and application is crucial for issuing fatwas on guarantees and deposits.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the hadith wording
Internalize the Arabic text 'على اليد ما أخذت حتى تؤديه' — it is concise and legally weighty.
Memorize the exact phrase.
2
Verify the hadith's authentication
Note that it is recorded by the four Sunan and graded hasan sahih by al-Tirmidhi. This authentication gives it evidential weight.
Refer to the footnote.
3
Derive the legal maxim
From the text, extract the rule: the person who takes (yad) is liable (ala) for what is taken until it is returned. This is the basis of the qa'ida.
In Hanafi usul, this is a general principle.
4
Apply to a sample case: borrowing
A borrower takes possession of an item. Under this maxim, they must return it. If lost, they are liable for compensation unless the loss was beyond their control.
Distinguish between amanah (trust) and daman (liability).
5
Note Hanafi extension
Hanafis extend this to all forms of possession, including deposits (wadi‘a) if the depositee misuses the item, because the hand becomes transgressive.
This contrasts with other schools on the liability of the depositee.

All from the hadith of al-Hasan from Samura. There is a dispute about al-Hasan hearing from Samura. See: al-Talkhis al-Habir (3/53).
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This lesson presents the hadith 'Ala al-yad ma akhadhat hatta tu'addiyahu' (What is taken must be returned). It is a key principle in the law of guarantees and deposits (wadi‘a). The footnote indicates its authentication by the scholars of hadith. In Hanafi fiqh, this hadith is used to impose liability on anyone who takes possession of something, whether by borrowing, lease, or tort, to return it or compensate for loss. It is a basis for the legal maxim 'al-manfa‘a daman' (benefit entails liability).

Key Terms
على اليد (ala al-yad): Liability upon the hand (the person who takes) for what is taken.

The Maxim: 'Mistake, Forgetfulness, and Coercion are Pardoned'
Presenting a hadith as the primary source for a legal maxim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim that mistake, forgetfulness, and coercion lift liability to construct a fatwa for contracts made under duress.

Core Idea
Allah pardons what is beyond human control — a foundational principle for excusing liability in Hanafi law.

This short hadith carries profound legal weight. It establishes that actions done mistakenly, forgetfully, or under coercion are not binding in many contexts. For a mufti, this maxim serves as a default principle when assessing liability in contracts, oaths, and torts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories pardoned
Read the hadith and note the three conditions: mistake (al-khata'), forgetfulness (al-nisyan), and coercion (al-ikrah). Each removes liability but in different degrees.
Coercion voids consent entirely; mistake and forgetfulness may still require compensation if caused by negligence.
2
Understand the scope of the pardon
The hadith lifts sin and legal liability, but does not cancel financial obligations to others. For example, a mistaken destruction of property still requires compensation if the person was negligent.
The maxim applies to acts of worship, contracts, and torts, but not where a right of another is involved without negligence.
3
Apply to a concrete case
Consider a person forced to sell his house under threat. Using the maxim, the sale is voidable because consent was coerced. The mufti rules that the contract is not binding.
If the coercion is proven, the sale is fasid (voidable) in Hanafi law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنَّ الله تعالى وضع عن أمتي الخطأ والنسيان وما استكرهوا عليه»(٤)
(٤) رواه ابن ماجه (۲۰٤٥) بإسناد جيد من حديث ابن ،عباس ورواه ابن حبان (۷۲۱۹) والحاكم وصححه ۱۹۸/٢ بلفظ : «تجاوز الله ووافقه الذهبي على تصحيحه. وهذا الحديث هو التاسع والثلاثون من الأربعين النووية، وقال النووي عقبه : حديث حسن رواه ابن ماجه والبيهقي ٣٥٦/٧ وغيرهما وأطال الحافظ ابن رجب في تخريجه (ر: جامع العلوم والحكم (٣٦١٢ - (٣٦٥) و (ر: تحفة الطالب بمعرفة أحاديث ابن الحاجب للحافظ ابن كثير ص ٢٧١ - ٢٧٤) .
'Allah has lifted from my nation error, forgetfulness, and what they are forced to do' (4). (4) Narrated by Ibn Majah (2045) with a good chain from Ibn 'Abbas, and by Ibn Hibban (7219) and al-Hakim who authenticated it (2/198) with the wording: 'Allah has overlooked...' and al-Dhahabi agreed. This hadith is the 39th of the Forty Nawawi. Al-Nawawi said afterwards: a hasan hadith narrated by Ibn Majah and al-Bayhaqi (7/356) and others. Ibn Rajab elaborated on its authentication (see: Jami' al-'Ulum wa al-Hikam, pp. 361-365, and Tuhfat al-Talib, pp. 271-274).
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This lesson presents the hadith that exempts from blame mistakes, forgetfulness, and coercion. It is a major principle in Hanafi law for contracts and obligations. For example, a contract made under duress is voidable. The footnote shows its authentication and recognition by al-Nawawi. Hanafi jurists use this hadith to derive the rule that contracts and oaths made in error or under coercion are not binding.

Key Terms
الخطأ والنسيان (al-khata' wa al-nisyan): Mistake and forgetfulness; both lift liability in many contexts.
مستكره (mustakrah): One who is forced; coercion (ikrah) voids consent.
وضع (wada'a): Lifted or removed; indicates the removal of legal effects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, forgetfulness is pardoned.
B) No, because debt is a right of another person and not pardoned.
C) Yes, if he didn't intend to forget.
D) No, unless he was coerced.

Key Takeaway
The hadith that mistake, forgetfulness, and coercion are pardoned is a foundational maxim used by Hanafi muftis to exempt from liability, but it does not remove financial duties owed to others.

The Maxim of Borrowed Goods, Debts, and Guarantors
Hadith with footnote on authenticity and definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the hadith-based maxim on borrowed goods, debts, and guarantors, and apply it to furu' cases in Hanafi mu'amalat.

Core Idea
Three obligations from one hadith: return borrowed items, pay debts, and guarantors are liable.

This hadith is a foundational textual anchor in Hanafi fiqh for three separate areas: loans (ariya), debts (dayn), and suretyship (kafala). It is narrated through multiple chains, giving it strong evidentiary weight. The maxim derived from it governs everyday transactions and fatwa issuance.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the hadith wording
First, commit the exact Arabic phrase to memory: 'العارية مؤداة، والدين مقضي، والزعيم غارم'. This is a core qawida.
Understanding each term aids application.
2
Define the key terms
Identify the legal definitions: 'al-ariya' (borrowed item), 'al-dayn' (debt), 'al-za'im' (guarantor). In Hanafi fiqh, each has specific conditions.
Use the key_terms provided.
3
Identify the legal implication of each phrase
Each phrase is a separate ruling: 'مؤداة' implies obligation to return; 'مقضي' implies obligation to pay; 'غارم' implies liability. These are not just statements but legal commands.
In Hanafi usul, a khabar (news) can indicate talab (demand) when context shows legislation.
4
Apply the maxim to furu' cases
Select examples from Hanafi fiqh where this maxim governs: e.g., borrower must return the item; debtor must pay; guarantor must fulfill if principal defaults.
Think of common transactions like borrowing a book, taking a loan, or acting as a surety.

For example, a borrower must return the item, a debtor must pay, and a guarantor becomes responsible if the principal defaults.

The Meaning of al-Ray in Early Fiqh
Defines 'al-ray' as reasoned opinion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'al-ray' as used by Ibn al-Qayyim and distinguish it from qiyas, identifying its components (qiyas, istihsan, istislah).

Core Idea
Al-ray is reasoned juristic opinion, broader than analogy, encompassing istihsan and istislah.

In this passage, Ibn al-Qayyim clarifies a foundational concept: al-ray. It is not arbitrary opinion but a disciplined reasoning process when textual evidences conflict. Understanding this definition is crucial for grasping Hanafi methodology, where ray serves as a legitimate source of law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source and context
Recognize that Ibn al-Qayyim is explaining the term 'al-ray' as understood by the Companions. This is a historical and conceptual definition.
2
Parse the definition phrase by phrase
Break down the definition: 'what the heart sees' (رأي القلب) 'after thought and reflection' (بعد فكر وتأمل) 'seeking to know the correct aspect' (طلب لمعرفة وجه الصواب) 'when evidences conflict' (تتعارض فيه الأمارات).
Each phrase adds a condition: it must be reasoned, intentional, and situation-bound.
3
Distinguish al-ray from qiyas
Note the author's explicit statement: al-ray is broader (أوسع) than qiyas. Qiyas is only one type of ray. Understand that qiyas involves analogical deduction from an original ruling, while ray includes other methods.
4
Identify the components of al-ray
Al-ray includes qiyas, istihsan, and istislah. Recognize that these are all forms of disciplined reasoning, not independent sources but part of the broader concept.
Istihsan is juristic preference, istislah is public interest; both are subsidiary sources in Hanafi usul.
5
Apply to Hanafi usul framework
Understand that al-ray is used when no explicit text (nass) or consensus (ijma) exists. It produces a probable ruling (zann) and must align with Sharia objectives.
This ties into the Hanafi hierarchy of sources: Qur'an, Sunnah, Ijma, then Ray (including Qiyas, Istihsan, Istislah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢/١١ - معنى الرأي :
وقد أوضح ابن القيم في كتابه «إعلام الموقعين» (٧٦/١) معنى.
«الرأي» الفقهي على حد ما كان يفهمه الصحابة، فأفاد أنّ الرأي هو : ما يراه القلب بعد فكر وتأمل وطلب لمعرفة وجه الصواب مما تتعارض فيه الأمارات .
والرأي بهذا المعنى أوسع من مفهوم القياس الفقهي الخاص، وهو إلحاق أمر غير منصوص على حكمه بأمر منصوص على حكمه لعلة مشتركة جامعة بينهما لأن الرأي بهذا المعنى يشمل القياس المذكور : ويشمل أيضاً الاستحسان والاستصلاح اللذين تقدم بيانهما في بحث المصادر التبعية.
2/11 - The Meaning of al-Ray:
Ibn al-Qayyim clarified in his book 'I'lam al-Muwaqqi'in' (1/76) the meaning of juristic 'ray' as understood by the Companions. He stated that al-ray is: what the heart sees after thought, reflection, and seeking to know the correct aspect when evidences conflict.
Al-ray in this sense is broader than the specific concept of juristic qiyas, which is joining an unregulated matter to a regulated matter due to a common effective cause. Because al-ray in this sense includes the aforementioned qiyas, and also includes istihsan and istislah, which were previously explained in the discussion of subsidiary sources.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'al-ray' (opinion) as it was understood by the Companions, based on Ibn al-Qayyim's explanation. It emphasizes that ray is not arbitrary opinion but a reasoned judgment after careful consideration when evidences conflict. The author then distinguishes this broad concept of ray from the narrower qiyas (analogy), noting that ray encompasses qiyas as well as istihsan (juristic preference) and istislah (public interest). This is important for understanding early Hanafi methodology, where ray was a legitimate source of law when texts were silent.

Key Terms
الرأي (al-ray): Reasoned opinion after thought and reflection when evidences conflict; broader than qiyas.
القياس (al-qiyās): Analogical deduction: applying a ruling from an original case to a new case due to a common effective cause.
الاستحسان (al-istiḥsān): Juristic preference; departing from an apparent analogy in favor of a stronger evidence or custom.
الاستصلاح (al-istiṣlāḥ): Consideration of public interest (maslaha) unrestricted by specific textual evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn al-Qayyim, what is the definition of 'al-ray'?
A) Personal whim or desire.
B) Analogical deduction from an original ruling.
C) What the heart sees after thought and reflection when evidences conflict.
D) Commonly accepted custom among the people.

Key Takeaway
Al-ray is a disciplined reasoning tool, not arbitrary opinion, and is broader than qiyas, including istihsan and istislah.
