intermediate
8 min
The Ruler's Authority to Modify Rulings Based on Maslaha
Examples demonstrating ruler's power to modify primary rulings for public interest

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi qawida on the ruler's authority to modify rulings for maslaha, apply it to historical examples, and identify its limits.

Core Idea
The ruler's order based on public interest can reverse a valid contract's nature, making it void or suspended.

This lesson presents a key principle in Hanafi legal theory: the ruler (wali al-amr) has the authority to temporarily modify primary rulings based on public interest (maslaha). Through four historical examples—from the Prophet's prohibition of storing sacrificial meat to the Ottoman sultan's invalidation of a debtor's waqf—we see how a valid act can become prohibited or void. Understanding this principle is crucial for muftis dealing with modern policy issues where the public interest conflicts with default rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core qawida
Locate the concluding statement that formulates the principle: the sultanic order based on maslaha reverses the character of a valid contract, making it void or suspended in Sharia's view.
This is the legal maxim that the examples support.
2
List the historical furuat (examples)
Identify each of the four cases: (1) Prophet's ban on storing sacrificial meat, (2) Caliph Umar's postponement of zakat, (3) Umar's suspension of the hadd for theft during famine, (4) Ottoman sultan's order invalidating a debtor's waqf.
Each case shows a different area of fiqh: udhiyah, zakat, hudud, waqf.
3
Analyze each example's operation
For each case, determine the original ruling (mubah or valid) and how the ruler's order temporarily changed it. Note the reason (maslaha) and the temporary nature.
The change is always contingent on the cause and ends when it ceases.
4
Recognize the limits (istithna')
Note that the ruler cannot permanently abrogate a clear text from Quran or Sunnah. The authority is administrative and temporary, not legislative.
The principle 'no ijtihad against a text' (la ijtihad ma'a al-nass) governs.
5
Apply the principle to new scenarios
Consider how a modern mufti might use this qawida when a government regulation restricts a normally permissible contract for public health or economic stability.
The mufta bihi position is to uphold the ruler's order as long as it is based on genuine maslaha.

Source Text

الباب الثالث: أدوار تطور الفقه الإسلامي ومميزاته
رحمه الله أنَّ ما حماه رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم من الأرض لم يكن على سبيل التشريع الدائم بل كان تصرفاً منه بالإمامة رئاسة الدولة، فيجوز للخليفة من بعده أن يحمي بعض الأراضي للحاجة ومصلحة الأمة. وهذا الحمي للمصلحة العامة هو من قبيل منع الناس أن ينتفعوا من أراض هي مباحة لهم شرعاً في الأصل، وهو نقطة الشاهد في موضوعنا هذا.
١٥ / ١٠ - ثانياً : النهي عن إدخار لحوم الأضاحي لأمر عارض. للمسلم أن يأكل من لحم أضحيته، ويدَّخر منها، ويهدي ويتصدق. لكن ثبت في السنة الصحيحة فيما روى الإمام مسلم وغيره، أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أمر في إحدى السنين أن لا يأكل أحد من لحم أضحيته بعد ثلاثة أيام. ولما سُئل في العام التالي عما إذا كان عليهم أن يمتنعوا كالعام الفائت أخبرهم أنَّ النهي إنما كان بسبب قدوم أعراب فقراء محتاجين في العام الفائت فأراد لهم أن لا يدَّخروا بعد ثلاث، بل أن يبذلوا للمحتاجين اللحوم المتبقية من أضاحيهم؛ ولا حرج عليهم الآن في أن يأكلوا ويتصدقوا ويدخروا كما يشاؤون الزوال سبب النهي. ووجه الشاهد هنا أنَّ الرسول الله بوصفه ولي أمر المسلمين، نهاهم لسبب طارىء عن أمر مباح في الأصل وهو ادخار لحوم الأضاحي، فوجبت عليهم طاعته.
(1) رواه البخاري في كتاب الأطعمة (٥٤٢٣) ومسلم في كتاب الأضاحي (۱۹۷۱) باب بیان ما كان من النهي عن أكل لحوم الأضاحي بعد ثلاث.
۱۱/۱٥ - ثالثاً : فتاوى سيدنا عمر في عام المجاعة : وقعت مجاعة عامة في جزيرة العرب خلال خلافة عمر رضي الله عنه، فأصدر خلالها حكمين مهمين أولهما أنه أخر جباية زكاة الماشية (من الإبل والغنم ... إلخ) حتى تزول المجاعة، رفقاً بأصحاب المواشي، فلما زالت وهطل المطر وتوافر المراعى أخذ منهم زكاتين، عن العام الفائت والعام الحاضر. كما أمر عمر رضي الله عنه بأن يُدرأ حد السرقة، وهو قطع يد السارق عام المجاعة لشبهة أن يكون قد دفعه إلى السرقة الاضطرار والجوع، لأن الحدود تدرأ بالشبهات كما ورد في الحديث (ر: ف ٤/١١).
ملحق بالفصل (١٥) سلطة ولي الأمر
۱۲/۱٥ - رابعاً وقف المدين جاء في كتاب الوقف من الدر المختار وحاشيته رد المحتار نقلاً عن معروضات المولى أبي السعود (وهو مفتي المملكة العثمانية ثم قاضي القسطنطينية في عهد السلطانين سليمان وسليم ومن كبار رجال المذهب الحنفي المتأخرين المعوّل على فتاويهم وترجيحاتهم) أنه : صدر الأمر السلطاني بعدم نفاذ وقف المدين في القدر الذي يتوقف عليه تسديد الدين من أمواله، قطعاً لما يلجأ إليه بعض الناس من وقف أموالهم لتهريبها من وجه الدائنين. وبناء على الأمر صرّح الفقهاء من بعده بعدم نفاذ مثل هذا الوقف شرعاً، وقد كانت النصوص في أصل المذهب صريحة في نفاذ وقف المدين ولو كان دينه محيطاً بجميع أمواله لأنّ الدين إنما يتعلق بذمته لا بعين ماله. (ر رد المحتار ٣٩٥/٣ - ٣٩٦). فأنت ترى من هذه النصوص الصريحة أنَّ الأمر والمنع السلطاني بحسب المصلحة يقلب صفة العقد الصحيح النافذ في أصل الشرع، فيصبح باطلاً أو موقوفاً في النظر الشرعي نفسه بمقتضى هذا المنع من ولي الأمر. والمهم جداً في هذه النصوص ليس هو الموضوعات التي وردت فيها بل المبدأ الفقهي الذي تتضمنه لما له من تأثير ذي بال في شتى الأحكام.
Chapter Three: Stages of Development of Islamic Jurisprudence and its Characteristics. May Allah have mercy on him (the author?) that what the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) protected of land was not on the basis of permanent legislation, but was an action of his as Imam (head of state). So it is permissible for the caliph after him to protect some lands for need and the interest of the nation. This protection for public interest is of the type of preventing people from benefiting from lands that are originally permissible for them legally, and this is the point of evidence in our subject. 15/10 - Secondly: The prohibition of storing sacrificial meat for a temporary reason. A Muslim may eat from the meat of his sacrifice, store it, give gifts, and give charity. But it is established in the authentic Sunnah, as narrated by Imam Muslim and others, that the Prophet (peace be upon him) ordered in one year that no one should eat from the meat of his sacrifice after three days. When asked the following year whether they should abstain as the previous year, he informed them that the prohibition was only because needy nomadic Arabs had come the previous year, so he wanted them not to store after three, but to give the remaining meat to the needy; and there is no harm now for them to eat, give charity, and store as they wish because the cause of the prohibition had ceased. The point of evidence here is that the Messenger of Allah, as the guardian of the Muslims, prohibited them for a temporary reason from something originally permissible, which is storing sacrificial meat, so obedience to him was obligatory. (1) Narrated by al-Bukhari in the Book of Foods (5423) and Muslim in the Book of Sacrifices (1971), Chapter on the clarification of the prohibition of eating sacrificial meat after three. 11/15 - Thirdly: The fatwas of our master Umar during the year of famine: A general famine occurred in the Arabian Peninsula during the caliphate of Umar (may Allah be pleased with him). During it he issued two important rulings: first, he postponed the collection of zakat on livestock (camels, sheep, etc.) until the famine ended, out of kindness to livestock owners. When the famine ended, rain fell, and pastures became abundant, he took two zakat payments from them: for the past year and the current year. Also, Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) ordered that the hadd punishment for theft, which is cutting off the thief's hand, be suspended during the year of famine due to the doubt that the thief may have been driven to theft by necessity and hunger, because legal penalties are suspended by doubts, as reported in the hadith (see: section 4/11). Appendix to Chapter (15): The Authority of the Ruler. 12/15 - Fourthly: The waqf of a debtor. It is stated in the book on waqf from al-Durr al-Mukhtar and its gloss Rad al-Muhtar, quoting from the submissions of Mulla Abu al-Su'ud (who was the Mufti of the Ottoman Empire and then judge of Constantinople during the reigns of Sultans Sulayman and Salim, and one of the senior later Hanafi scholars whose fatwas and preferences are relied upon) that: The sultanic order was issued that the waqf of a debtor is not valid to the extent necessary for the payment of his debt from his assets, in order to prevent what some people resort to: endowing their wealth to evade creditors. Based on this order, later jurists declared that such a waqf is not valid legally, even though the texts of the original school clearly stated the validity of a debtor's waqf even if his debt encompassed all his wealth, because the debt is only attached to his liability (dhimma) and not to the specific property (see Rad al-Muhtar 3/395-396). So you see from these clear texts that the sultanic order or prohibition based on maslaha (public interest) reverses the nature of a valid and effective contract in the original law, so it becomes void or suspended in the legal view itself by virtue of this prohibition from the ruler. And what is very important in these texts is not the topics they address, but the juristic principle they contain, due to its significant impact on various rulings.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is a collection of historical examples from the early Islamic period and later Hanafi practice, illustrating the principle that the ruler (wali al-amr) has the authority to modify or suspend primary legal rulings based on public interest (maslaha). The passage is structured as a qawida (legal maxim) supported by furuat (applications). The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, presents four cases: (1) The Prophet's prohibition of storing sacrificial meat during a year of need, (2) Caliph Umar's postponement of zakat collection and suspension of the hadd penalty for theft during a famine, (3) The sultan's order invalidating a debtor's waqf to prevent fraud against creditors. Each case demonstrates that an action originally permissible or valid can be temporarily prohibited or rendered void by the ruler's command due to a pressing need. The concluding statement formulates the principle explicitly: the ruler's order or prohibition based on maslaha can reverse the character of a valid contract in the original Sharia, making it void or suspended in Islamic legal perspective. This principle is not limited to these examples; it has broad application in various rulings. The passage is a critical refinement of the understanding that while primary rules are fixed, the ruler has discretion to temporarily override them for the public good, a concept rooted in the Hanafi tradition's recognition of siyasa shar'iyya (Sharia-oriented policy). The authority weight here is from later Hanafi fatawa (like those of Mulla Abu al-Su'ud) and historical practice, not from the core Zahir al-Riwayah texts, but it represents a widely accepted principle in Hanafi legal theory.

Key Terms
سلطة ولي الأمر (sulṭat wālī al-amr): The authority of the ruler (imam or sultan) to issue orders and prohibitions in the public interest, which can modify the legal effect of otherwise valid acts.
المصلحة (al-maṣlaḥa): Public interest or welfare that justifies the ruler's intervention to temporarily alter rulings.
يق لب صفة العقد (yaqlib sifat al-ʿaqd): The ruler's order 'reverses the character' of a contract, meaning it changes its legal classification from valid to void or suspended.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes the effect of a sultanic order based on public interest on a contract that is originally valid in the Sharia, according to the qawida in this lesson?
A) It permanently abolishes the original ruling of the contract.
B) It can reverse the contract's validity, making it void or suspended in Sharia's view.
C) It has no effect because the contract is already valid.
D) It only affects contracts related to land, not other types.

Key Takeaway
The ruler's authority to temporarily suspend or reverse rulings for maslaha is a well-established Hanafi principle, but it cannot override definitive texts.

Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications

The Maxim: Hudud are Averted by Doubts
Explains the maxim and its application to zina proof and retraction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-hudud tudra' bi al-shubuhat' to determine when a hadd punishment is replaced by ta'zir in cases of zina.

Core Idea
Hadd punishments are averted by any doubt; in zina, nearly impossible to prove, so ta'zir is the norm.

This lesson unpacks a key maxim in Hanafi criminal jurisprudence: hudud punishments are averted by the slightest doubt. We explore how this maxim operates in the specific context of zina, where the stringent proof requirements (four witnesses or repeated confession) are almost never met, leading to the substitution of ta'zir. Understanding this maxim is critical for a mufti tasked with applying hudud correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its source
The maxim is: 'al-hudud tudra' bi al-shubuhat'. Note that it is derived from a hadith of the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم). This is the foundational rule.
2
Analyze the proof conditions for zina
Zina requires either four upright witnesses or a confession repeated four times. Both are extremely difficult. Recognize that these conditions create 'shubha' (doubt) when unmet.
The shubha here is in the proof, not in the act itself.
3
Apply the maxim to a failure of proof
When the conditions are not fulfilled, doubt exists, so the hadd is averted. The punishment then becomes ta'zir (discretionary). This is the primary furu' in the passage.
4
Consider retraction and escape
If the confessor retracts or the convicted flees during stoning, this creates further doubt, so hadd is averted and ta'zir applies. This expands the maxim's scope.
Retraction is treated as shubha
5
Draw the conclusion for practical application
The author concludes that stoning is more a deterrent than an applied punishment. As a mufti, recognize that hadd is seldom executed; ta'zir is the default.
This reflects the Hanafi approach of leniency in hudud

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ملحق أول بالفصل (۲۱) عقوبة جريمة الزنى

الثاني : أنه لا يمكن في الواقع إثبات جريمة الزنى إلا بإقرار الزاني على نفسه، لأن إثبات الزنى بالشهادة يتطلب معاينة من أربعة شهود عدول (ثقات)، ورؤية تكاد تكون مستحيلة. وإن إقرار الزاني الموجب للحد أن يتكرر منه يجب أربع مرات في أربعة مجالس. فإذا لم تتوافر هذه الشرائط العسيرة في إثبات الزنى لأجل تطبيق عقوبة الحد (جلداً أو رجماً) تنتقل العقوبة إلى التعزير بالنوع والقدر الذي يراه الحاكم بحسب الأحوال، وذلك لحصول الشبهة في إثبات الجريمة، والحدود تدراً بالشبهات بنص الحديث النبوي.
ويلحظ أيضاً أن إقرار الزاني بالزنى، إذا رجع بعده المقر عن إقراره يسقط الحد برجوعه وتنتقل العقوبة إلى التعزير بما يكفي للزجر. وفي حالة الرجم إذا كان الزاني ثيباً محصناً يعتبر هروبه من مكان الرجم كالرجوع عن الإقرار تمتنع معه متابعة الرجم، وتنتقل عقوبته إلى التعزير.
في ضوء كل ذلك من الأحكام المقررة في السنة والفقه يتبين أن رجم الزاني المحصن هو في الشريعة عقوبة للتخويف أكثر منها للتطبيق لكي يعلم الزاني المحصن الذي له زوجة تغنيه ماذا يستحق من العقاب. ولا يكاد يمكن تطبيق عقوبة الرجم على زان إلا بإرادته حينما يقر على نفسه ويُصِرُّ . وأن الحالات التي وردت في السنة النبوية كلها من هذا القبيل.
First appendix to Chapter (21): Punishment for the crime of zina. Second: It is not possible in reality to prove the crime of zina except by the confession of the adulterer himself, because proving zina by testimony requires the direct witnessing of four upright (trustworthy) witnesses, a sighting that is almost impossible. And the confession of the adulterer that necessitates the hadd must be repeated four times in four sessions. If these difficult conditions for proving zina are not met for applying the hadd punishment (flogging or stoning), the punishment shifts to ta'zir in type and measure as the ruler deems appropriate according to circumstances, because doubt arises in proving the crime, and the hudud are averted by doubts as per the hadith of the Prophet. Also note that if the confessor retracts his confession after confessing, the hadd is dropped by his retraction and the punishment shifts to ta'zir sufficient for deterrence. In the case of stoning, if the adulterer is a muhsan (married), his escape from the stoning place is considered like retraction of confession, so stoning is discontinued and his punishment shifts to ta'zir. In light of all these established rulings in the Sunnah and fiqh, it becomes clear that stoning the muhsan adulterer is in shariah a punishment for intimidation more than for implementation, so that the muhsan adulterer who has a wife that suffices him knows what punishment he deserves. And the punishment of stoning can hardly be applied to an adulterer except by his will when he confesses and persists. And the cases that occurred in the Prophetic Sunnah are all of this kind.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a legal discussion of the punishment for zina under Islamic law. It opens by identifying the practical impossibility of proving zina through testimony, as it requires four upright eyewitnesses to the act itself, which is nearly impossible. Therefore, the only realistic proof is confession by the adulterer himself, and even then the confession must be repeated four times in four separate sessions. When these stringent conditions are not met, the hadd punishment (flogging for unmarried, stoning for married) is not applied; instead, the court imposes ta'zir (discretionary punishment) at the judge's discretion. The principle behind this is the well-known legal maxim 'الحدود تدرأ بالشبهات' (the hudud are averted by doubts), based on a hadith of the Prophet. Furthermore, if the confessor retracts his confession, the hadd is automatically dropped, and the judge moves to ta'zir. In the case of stoning, if the convicted adulterer flees from the execution site, that is treated as a retraction, so stoning is stopped and ta'zir applies. The author concludes that stoning in shariah is more a threat to deter the married person (who has no excuse) than a punishment that is actually carried out; its application effectively requires the offender's willing confession and persistence.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha): Doubt in the evidence that prevents application of hadd punishment.
تدرأ (tudra'): Averted, prevented; used in the maxim 'al-hudud tudra' bi al-shubuhat'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence if a confessor retracts his confession?
A) Hadd is still applied
B) Hadd is dropped and ta'zir applies
C) The confession is repeated
D) The case is dismissed completely

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-hudud tudra' bi al-shubuhat' means that any doubt in proof averts the prescribed hadd punishment, replacing it with ta'zir.

The Qawida of al-Tawallud: Ownership Through Generation
Qawida with furuat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi qawida of al-tawallud and apply it to determine ownership of generated property in cases of sole ownership, joint ownership, and usurpation.

Core Idea
Whatever is generated from owned property belongs to the owner of the original asset.

The Hanafi school establishes a fundamental maxim governing the ownership of produce, offspring, and other results of property. This maxim simplifies countless cases in sales, inheritance, and torts. Let us explore its text and applications as presented by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the qawida text
Find the Arabic statement of the maxim in the passage. It is the key sentence starting with 'من القواعد المقررة'.
The maxim is: ما يتولد أو ينشأ من المملوك مملوك.
2
Understand the rationale
The owner of the original is entitled to its offshoot because ownership is comprehensive and extends to natural and produced increments.
Includes both natural and effort-based generation.
3
Apply to sole ownership
The passage lists examples: fruit of a tree, offspring of an animal, wool and milk of sheep. All belong to the owner of the original.
No need for explicit proof; it is a direct corollary of ownership.
4
Apply to joint ownership
If the original is jointly owned, the generated thing is jointly owned in proportion to shares.
Example: two owners of a sheep share its lamb according to their ownership percentage.
5
Apply to usurped property
Offspring of usurped property belongs to the original owner, not the usurper. However, whether the usurper is liable for the offspring is disputed.
This is an ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.

الباب الخامس: نظرية الملكية في الفقه
وهذا معنى قولهم: إن الإرث جبري فلا يسقط بإسقاط الوارث، كما لا يؤثر فيه قصد المورث إلى الحرمان.
فلو أعلن إنسان وسجّل حرمان أحد ورثته أو تفضيل بعضهم على بعض في النصيب الإرثي، لم يكن لحرمانه أو تفضيله من أثر. وإنما يستطيع المورث أن يتصرف بأمواله كلها بالهبة أو سواها من التصرفات المنجَزَة حال حياته، وأن يوصي بثلث ماله فما دونه بعد موته لمن ليس بوارث، إذ لا وصية لوارث كيلا تُتَّخذ الوصية ذريعة إلى التفضيل في الإرث (۱).
ويمنع
ويتضح مما سلف أن شرط الملكية بالإرث أن لا يكون على المتوفى دین مستغرق للتركة فإن كان فإنه يقدم استيفاؤه من التركة على الإرث تملك الورثة، حتى لو باع هؤلاء شيئاً من التركة لا ينفذ البيع لأنهم باعوا ما لم يملكوا إلا أن يشاء الورثة دفع الدين كله من أموالهم الخاصة. فعندئذ تسلم لهم التركة إذا سلمت للغرماء ديونهم كاملة فلم يبق لهم في التركة علاقة .
وإذا كان مبلغ الدين أكثر من التركة وأراد الورثة أن يدفعوا إلى الغرماء من الدين ما يعادل قيمة التركة فقط ويتملكوها فليس لهم ذلك إلا إذا رضي الغرماء ( ر ) الدر المختار ورد المحتار في كتاب القضاء ٤/ (٣٤٠
ب - التضمين، أو التعويض:
إذا أتلف أحد لآخر شيئاً، أو غَصَبه منه فهلك أو فقد، وكذا إذا ألحق بغيره ضرراً بجناية أو تسبب ففي ذلك وأمثاله يجب عليه ضمان ما أتلفه، وتعويض الضرر الذي باشره أو تسبب به.
وعندئذ يملك المعوّض له ذلك العوض ملكاً مستنداً إلى سبب الخَلَفيَّة، لأنَّ هذا العوض خَلَف عما تضرر فيه من مال أو منفعة أو عضو. ويدخل في ذلك الدية وأرش (۱) الجنايات فكل ذلك يملك بسبب الخَلَفَيَّة.
۹/۲۳ - ( رابعاً) - التولد من المملوك:
من القواعد المقررة أنَّ ما يتولد أو ينشأ من المملوك مملوك.
فمالك الأصل هو أولى بفرعه من سواه، سواء في ذلك ما ينتج بتسبب مالك الأصل وعمله وما يحصل بطبعه دون عمل مالك الأصل أو تسببه .
فثمرة الشجر، وولد الحيوان وصوف الغنم ولبنها، وأمثال ذلك، كلها مملوكة لصاحب الأصل.
وإذا تولد شيء من شيء مشترك فهذا المتولد الحاصل يكون مشتركاً بين المالكين للأصل بنسبة حصصهم فيه . ونتائج المغصوب تجري على هذا الأساس فولد الدابة المغصوبة وثمر الكرم المغصوب ملك للمالك المغصوب منه لا للغاصب. ولكن هل يسري حكم الغصب على الفرع المتولد في يد الغاصب، فيعتبر غاصباً للفرع أيضاً منذ تولده فيضمنه إذا هَلَك، أو لا الغضب عليه إلا بشرائط ؟ هذه قضية اختلفت فيها الاجتهادات، كما هو مفصل في موضعه من (1) الأرش (بفتح فسكون): هو العوض المالي الذي يقدر ويجب على الجاني في غير النفس والأعضاء. أما عوض إتلاف النفس أو العضو فيسمى : دية (بكسر ففتح).
Chapter Five: The Theory of Property in Fiqh. And this is the meaning of their statement: Inheritance is compulsory, so it is not nullified by the heir's waiver, nor does the testator's intent to deprive have any effect on it. If a person announces and records depriving one of his heirs or preferring some over others in the inheritance share, his deprivation or preference has no effect. Rather, the testator can dispose of all his property by gift or other executed dispositions during his life, and he may bequeath up to one-third of his property after his death to someone who is not an heir, because there is no bequest to an heir lest the bequest be used as a means of preference in inheritance (1). And it is prohibited. And it becomes clear from the foregoing that the condition for ownership by inheritance is that there should be no debt covering the entire estate of the deceased; if there is, its satisfaction from the estate takes precedence over the inheritance owned by the heirs, such that if the heirs sell something from the estate, the sale is not valid because they sold what they did not own, unless the heirs wish to pay the entire debt from their own private funds. Then the estate is delivered to them if the creditors' debts are fully paid, so they have no further claim in the estate. And if the amount of the debt is more than the estate and the heirs wish to pay the creditors an amount equal to the value of the estate only and take ownership of it, they may not do so unless the creditors agree (see al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar in the Book of Judgments 4/340).
B – al-Tadmin (Liability), or Compensation: If one person destroys something belonging to another, or usurps it from him and it perishes or is lost, and likewise if he causes harm to another by a crime or by causation, in that and similar cases he must guarantee what he destroyed and compensate for the harm he directly caused or caused by causation. Then the compensated person owns that compensation as ownership based on the cause of substitution (al-khalafiyya), because this compensation is a substitute for what he suffered loss of in wealth, benefit, or limb. This includes diya (blood money) and arsh (compensation for non-fatal injuries) for crimes; all of that is owned by cause of substitution.
9/23 – (Fourth) Generation from Owned Property: One of the established rules is that what is generated or originates from owned property is owned. So the owner of the original is more entitled to its offshoot than anyone else, whether that which is produced by the owner's action and effort or that which occurs naturally without the owner's action or causation. So the fruit of the tree, the offspring of the animal, the wool of the sheep, its milk, and the like, all are owned by the owner of the original. And if something is generated from something that is jointly owned, then this generated thing becomes jointly owned between the owners of the original in proportion to their shares in it. And the results of usurped property follow this basis: the offspring of a usurped animal and the fruit of a usurped vineyard are owned by the owner from whom it was usurped, not by the usurper. But does the ruling of usurpation apply to the generated offshoot in the usurper's hand, so that he is considered a usurper of the offshoot as well from its generation and is liable if it perishes, or is usurpation not established except with conditions? This is an issue regarding which juristic opinions differed, as detailed in its place. (1) Arsh: the financial compensation estimated and required from the criminal for injuries other than life and limbs. As for compensation for taking life or limb, it is called diya.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage covers two main topics: inheritance and the qawida of تولد (generation from owned property). The author first explains that inheritance is compulsory (جبري) – the heir's right cannot be waived, nor can the testator's intention to deprive affect it. However, the testator can dispose of his property during his lifetime or bequeath up to one-third to non-heirs, based on the hadith 'لا وصية لوارث' (no bequest to an heir). The inheritance is also subject to debts: debts must be paid before heirs receive anything; if heirs sell estate property before debt is paid, the sale is invalid unless they pay the debt from their own funds. Then the author briefly mentions tort liability (تضمين) where compensation is owned as a substitute. The main focus is the qawida: 'ما يتولد أو ينشأ من المملوك مملوك' (what is generated from owned property is owned). This means that fruits, offspring, wool, milk, etc., belong to the owner of the original asset. In the case of joint ownership, the offspring is shared proportionally. For usurped property (مغصوب), the offspring belongs to the original owner, not the usurper. However, whether the usurper is also considered a usurper of the offspring is disputed among scholars. This passage is an expansion of the maxim with several furuat (applications).

Key Terms
التولد (al-tawallud): The generation or origination of something from owned property, either naturally or through effort.
الخلفية (al-khalafiyya): Substitution: the legal basis for owning compensation as a substitute for lost property.
الأرش (al-arsh): Financial compensation for injuries to the body except for death or loss of limbs (which is called diya).
الضمان (al-daman): Liability or guarantee to pay compensation for destruction, usurpation, or harm caused.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi qawida of al-tawallud, who owns the offspring of a usurped animal?
A) The usurper
B) The original owner
C) The state
D) It is ownerless

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'ما يتولد من المملوك مملوك' ensures that all natural and produced increments of owned property belong to the owner of the original, with specific applications to joint ownership and usurpation.

The Qawida of Generation from Owned Property
Stating the qawida with furuat and a critique of the Mejelle's omission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the qawida of generation from owned property, identify its furuat, and apply it in a mufti's analysis, including the dispute on usurper liability for offspring.

Core Idea
Whatever generates from owned property is owned by the owner of the original, even if in a usurper's hands.

The Hanafi legal tradition recognizes several causes of ownership. One of the most far-reaching is generation (al-tawallud): any increase, offspring, or produce that arises from an owned object belongs to the owner of that object. This principle governs many practical cases, from fruit on trees to milk from livestock. The passage before you states the rule, applies it to usurped property and shared ownership, and highlights a key dispute about the usurper's liability for offspring. As a mufti, mastering this qawida is essential for correct fatwas on property and damages.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Original Property and Its Generated Thing
Start by clearly defining what the original owned property (al-asl) is and what is claimed as its generation (al-far'). For example, a sheep and its lamb, or land and its crop.
The generation must be a physical or legal product of the original.
2
Determine Ownership of the Original
Ascertain who legally owns the original property. The qawida applies only if the original is owned (mamluk). If the original is jointly owned, the generation is shared proportionally.
This step is critical when the original is in the possession of someone other than the owner, e.g., a usurper.
3
Apply the Qawida: The Generated Thing Belongs to the Original Owner
Once ownership of the original is established, the generated thing follows its owner, regardless of whether the generation occurred naturally or through effort, and regardless of who currently holds it.
Example: fruit of a tree belongs to the tree owner even if the tree is on usurped land.
4
Consider Liability for the Generated Thing When the Original Is Usurped
The original owner owns the generated thing, but does the usurper incur liability (daman) for it? The passage notes a difference of opinion. The preferred Hanafi view is that usurper is not automatically liable for the offspring unless he had actual control or committed a new act of usurpation.
This is a separate inquiry from ownership.
5
Distinguish Natural Offspring from Crop from Own Seed
When a usurper plants seed that he owns, the crop belongs to the planter (because it is growth of his seed), not the landowner. However, the planter must compensate for any damage to the land.
This is an exception: the generated thing (crop) is owned by the seed owner, not the landowner, because the seed is an input belonging to the planter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۹/۲۳ - ( رابعاً) - التولد من المملوك: من القواعد المقررة أنَّ ما يتولد أو ينشأ من المملوك مملوك. فمالك الأصل هو أولى بفرعه من سواه، سواء في ذلك ما ينتج بتسبب مالك الأصل ،وعمله وما يحصل بطبعه دون عمل مالك الأصل أو تسببه . فثمرة الشجر، وولد الحيوان وصوف الغنم ولبنها، وأمثال ذلك، كلها مملوكة لصاحب الأصل. وإذا تولد شيء من شيء مشترك فهذا المتولد الحاصل يكون مشتركاً بين المالكين للأصل بنسبة حصصهم فيه . ونتائج المغصوب تجري على هذا الأساس فولد الدابة المغصوبة وثمر الكرم المغصوب ملك للمالك المغصوب منه لا للغاصب. ولكن هل يسري حكم الغصب على الفرع المتولد في يد الغاصب، فيعتبر غاصباً للفرع أيضاً منذ تولده فيضمنه إذا هَلَك، أو لا الغضب عليه إلا بشرائط ؟ هذا قضية اختلفت فيها الاجتهادات، كما هو مفصل في موضعه من كتاب الغَضب في المؤلّفات الفقهية، إذ ان ثبوت ملكية الفرع لمالك الأصل لا يَسْتَلزم أن يُعتبر غاصب الأصل غاصباً للفرع الذي لم يسلبه من المالك، وإنما حصل في يده حصولاً. وأما إذا زرع الأرض غاصبها فإنّ المحصول له لا لمالك الأرض، لأنه نماء البذر وهو ملكه ولكنه يضمن لمالك الأرض نقصانها بالزرع إن نقصت به (۱).

(1) هذا الحكم الفقهي أصبح اليوم معدّلاً لدينا بالمادة ۸۸٥/ من القانون المدني السوري الجديد، فقد قضت هذه المادة فيما إذا زرع الأرض غير مالكها بأن يكون مالك الأرض مخيراً بين أمرين :
- إما أن يدفع للزارع قيمة البذر ويتملك محصوله بطريقة الإلحاق بالأرض.
وإما أن يترك الغلة للزارع ويأخذ منه أجر مثل الأرض عن سنة كاملة. هذا إذا كان قد فات موسم البذر، وإلا فلصاحب الأرض - إن شاء ـ أن يكلف الزارع نزع بذره من الأرض دون أي تعويض من عمل الحراثة والبذر.

١٠/٢٣ - هذا، وإن المجلة قد حصرت بالمادة / ١٢٤٨ / أسبا في الثلاثة الأول، ولم تتعرض للتولد من المملوك. وكذلك أرادت المجلة بالخَلَفيَّة : الإرث فقط، ففسرتها بخلفية شخص عن آخر متابعة في هذا البيان ما في بعض الكتب الفقهية (٢) دون نظر إلى ما تدل عليه فروع الأحكام من أسباب شرعية للملك . وقد رأينا من تتبع فروع صور الملكية أن من الضروري إضافة سبب رابع هو التولد من المملوك كما فعلنا لأن الشيء المتولد من المملوك لا تستند ملكية صاحب الأصل فيه إلى إحراز، إذ قد يكون في يد غيره كنتاج المغصوب ولا تستند أيضاً إلى عقد أو خَلَفيَّة كما هو ظاهر فوجب التولد سبباً مستقلاً.

(۲) ذكر ذلك ابن نجيم في الصيد والذبائح من كتابه الأشباه والنظائر وتابعته المجلة فيه (ر: مرآة المجلة م/ ١٢٤٨).
9/23 - Fourth: Generation from owned property: It is established rules that whatever is generated or originates from owned property is owned. So the owner of the origin has priority over its offshoot over others, whether that results from the cause of the owner and his action or what occurs naturally without the action of the owner or his cause. So fruit of trees, young of animals, wool of sheep, and their milk, and the like, all are owned by the owner of the origin. And if something is generated from shared property, then this generated thing is shared between the owners of the origin according to their shares in it. And the results of usurped property proceed on this basis, so the offspring of a usurped she-animal and the fruit of a usurped vineyard are owned by the owner from whom it was usurped, not by the usurper. But does the ruling of usurpation extend to the offshoot generated in the hands of the usurper, so that he is considered a usurper of the offshoot as well from its generation and thus liable if it perishes, or is he not considered a usurper of it except with conditions? This is an issue differing in ijtihad, as detailed in its place in books of fiqh on usurpation, since establishing ownership of the offshoot for the owner of the origin does not necessitate that the usurper of the origin be considered a usurper of the offshoot which he did not seize from the owner, but rather came into his possession naturally. As for if the usurper plants the land, then the crop is his, not the owner of the land, because it is growth of the seed which is his property, but he guarantees to the owner of the land any decrease (of the land) due to planting if it diminishes (by it). (1)

(1) This fiqh ruling today is modified for us by Article 885 of the new Syrian Civil Code, which ruled that if someone plants the land of another, the owner of the land has two options: either to pay the planter the value of the seed and own the crop by annexation to the land, or to leave the produce to the planter and take from him the rent of the land for a full year. This applies if the planting season has passed; otherwise, the owner may require the planter to remove his seed from the land without any compensation for plowing and planting.

10/23 - And the Mejelle has restricted the causes by Article 1248 to the first three, and did not address generation from owned property. Likewise the Mejelle intended by al-khalafiyya: only inheritance, interpreting it as substitution of one person for another following what is in some fiqh books (2) without regard to what the branches of rulings indicate regarding shar'i causes of ownership. And we have seen from tracking the branches of ownership images that it is necessary to add a fourth cause, which is generation from owned property as we did, because the thing generated from owned property does not base the ownership of the owner of the origin on acquisition (ihraz), as it may be in the hand of another like the offspring of a usurped animal, nor does it base on contract or substitution as is apparent, so generation must be an independent cause.

(2) Ibn Nujaym mentioned that in his book al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir in the chapter of hunting and slaughtering, and the Mejelle followed him in that (see: Mir'at al-Majalla, Article 1248).
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth cause of ownership: generation (al-tawallud) from owned property. It states the qawida: whatever is generated from owned property belongs to the owner of the original. This applies to natural increases (like fruit, offspring, wool, milk) and those produced by the owner's labor. If the original is shared, the generated thing is shared proportionally. The passage then addresses a specific furu: when property is usurped, the offspring still belongs to the original owner, but the usurper's liability for the offspring is debated. For planting on usurped land, the crop belongs to the usurper (since it grows from his seed) but he must compensate for land damage. The author then criticizes the Mejelle for limiting causes to three (including inheritance as the only substitution) and argues that generation is an independent fourth cause.

Key Terms
التولد (al-tawallud): The process by which property originates or is generated from an existing owned object, either naturally or through effort.
الغصب (al-ghasb): Unlawful seizure of another's property; its rules apply to the original property but not necessarily to its offspring.
المجلة (al-Majalla): The Ottoman Mejelle-i Ahkâm-ı Adliyye, a codification of Hanafi fiqh; it listed only three causes of ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The owner of the land
B) The usurper (planter) because the seed is his
C) Both in equal shares
D) It must be divided three ways

Key Takeaway
The qawida 'what is generated from owned property is owned' is a fundamental principle of Hanafi ownership, distinct from acquisition and contract.

Hanafi Maxim: Ownership Broader Than Wealth
Defining the Hanafi concept that usufructs and debts are owned but not wealth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the Hanafi terms milk and mal, distinguish them, and apply the distinction to classify usufructs and debts as owned but not wealth.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, ownership (milk) includes non-tangible items; wealth (mal) is only tangible and valuable.

This passage establishes a key Hanafi legal maxim: milk (ownership) is broader than mal (wealth). Understanding this distinction is essential for classifying rights, obligations, and contracts in Hanafi law. The author contrasts the Hanafi view with the Shafi'i/Maliki position, which counts usufructs as wealth.

Guided Path
1
State the Maxim
Identify the core claim: 'Usufructs and debts are owned (milk) but not wealth (mal).' This is the Hanafi distinction.
The maxim is a sub-principle governing the scope of property.
2
Define the Key Terms
Distinguish milk from mal. Milk is exclusive entitlement to anything of value, tangible or intangible. Mal is limited to physical, valuable objects.
Milk is the genus; mal is a species.
3
Identify the Scope
List what falls under each category. Milk includes corporeal objects (a'yan), usufructs (manafi'), and debts (duyun). Mal includes only corporeal objects.
Usufructs and debts are mamluk (owned) but not mal.
4
Note the Cross-Madhhab Difference
Recognize that the Shafi'i and Maliki schools consider usufructs as mal, thus equating milk and mal more closely.
This difference affects rulings on zakat, bequests, and contract validity.
5
Apply the Maxim
Classify a sample: e.g., the right to use a house for a year (usufruct) is milk but not mal; a debt owed to you is milk but not mal; a physical house is both milk and mal.
This application is crucial for fatwa purposes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢٤/ ٥ ـ وفقهاء الحنفية قد اعتبروا المنافع والديون أملاكاً ولم يعتبروها أموالاً. وبذلك فرَّقوا بين مفهوم «الملك» ومفهوم «المال» كما تقدم. فالملك بمعنى المملوك هو أعم مفهوماً من المال، لأن المال عند فقهاء الحنفية يقتصر على الموجودات المادية ذات القيمة بين الناس، أما المملوك فهو كل ما تتعلق به الملكية أي الاختصاص، فيشمل الأعيان المالية والمنافع والديون، لأن هذه المنافع والديون يجري فيها الاختصاص كما يجري في الأعيان. على أن الاجتهادين المالكي والشافعي يعتبران المنافع أموالاً كالأعيان بلا فرق. وهذا هو الأوجه والموافق للأنظار القانونية العامة الحديثة.
24/5 - And the Hanafi jurists have considered usufructs and debts as owned things (amlak) and did not consider them as wealth (amwal). And by this they distinguished between the concept of 'ownership' (milk) and the concept of 'wealth' (mal) as previously mentioned. So 'milk' in the sense of the owned thing is broader in concept than 'mal', because 'mal' according to Hanafi jurists is limited to material existents of value among people, while 'the owned' is everything to which ownership, i.e., exclusive entitlement, attaches, including financial corporeal objects, usufructs, and debts, because these usufructs and debts admit of exclusive entitlement just as corporeal objects do. However, the Maliki and Shafi'i schools consider usufructs as wealth like corporeal objects without distinction. And this is the more appropriate and consistent with modern general legal perspectives.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage presents a foundational Hanafi legal maxim (qawida) about the scope of 'mal' (wealth). The Hanafi school holds that ownership (milk) is broader than wealth (mal): everything that can be owned is 'mamluk', but only tangible, valuable physical items qualify as 'mal'. Usufructs (manafi') and debts (duyun) are owned—they can be the subject of contracts and bequests—but they are not considered 'mal' because they are non-corporeal. The author notes that the Maliki and Shafi'i schools include usufructs in the definition of wealth, and he considers that view more aligned with modern legal thought.

Key Terms
الملك (al-milk): Ownership; the exclusive relationship between a person and an object/usufruct/debt; broader than mal.
المال (al-mal): Wealth; limited to physical, valuable items in Hanafi; excludes usufructs and debts.
المنافع (al-manafi'): Usufructs or benefits derived from assets; owned but not mal in Hanafi.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The right to use a house for a year
B) A debt owed to you by a friend
C) A plot of land
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, ownership (milk) is broader than wealth (mal); usufructs and debts are owned but not wealth.

Distinct and Shared Ownership in Hanafi Fiqh
Defining and illustrating distinct and shared ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between distinct ownership (milk mumayyaz) and shared ownership (milk sha'i') and apply the definition of the common share (hissah shai'ah) in Hanafi property law.

Core Idea
Shared ownership means every atom belongs to all co-owners proportionally — the share permeates the whole.

Ownership (milk) can be classified by its form: either a specific, physically separated object or an undivided fraction of a whole. This distinction is foundational for many property transactions, inheritance partitions, and pre-emption cases. As a mufti, you must correctly identify the type to apply the relevant legal maxims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the object
First, determine whether the owned thing is a specific, bounded entity (e.g., a particular book or a distinct floor) or a fraction of an undivided whole (e.g., half a house).
2
Classify the ownership type
If the object is physically distinct and separable, it is milk mumayyaz. If the ownership is a relative share of an indivisible whole, it is milk sha'i'.
3
Apply the definition of the common share
For shared ownership, remember that the share (hissah shai'ah) permeates every part: every atom belongs to all co-owners proportionally.
This prevents a co-owner from claiming any specific physical portion exclusively.
4
Consider the practical consequence
Recognize that in shared ownership, any use, disposal, or partition must respect that all owners have rights in every part. The maxim 'al-ghurm bil-ghunm' (loss follows gain) applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثاني: من حيث الصورة:
ومن حيث الصورة ينقسم الملك أيضاً إلى نوعين: ملك متميز وملك شائع.
أولاً: فالملك المتميز هو ما يتعلق بشيء معين ذي حدود تفصله عما سواه. وذلك كما يملك إنسان رأساً من الغنم أو كتاباً، أو داراً بكاملها، أو طبقة معينة من دار ذات طبقات، ونحو ذلك.
ثانياً: والملك الشائع أو المشاع هو الملك المتعلق بجزء نسبي غير معين من مجموع الشيء مهما كان ذلك الجزء كبيراً أو صغيراً. وذلك كما يملك إنسان نصف دار، أو ربع فرس، أو جزءاً من مائة فأكثر من أرض، ونحو ذلك. وهذا ما يسمونه «الحصة الشائعة في الشيء المشترك». وبمقتضى نظرية الشيوع يكون كل جزء أو ذرّة من المال المشترك غير مخصوص بأحد من أصحاب الحصص، بل تتعلق به ملكيات جميعهم. وعن هذا يعرفون الحصّة الشائعة بأنها: السّهم الساري إلى كل جزء.
Sub-section Two: From the Aspect of Form:
And from the aspect of form, ownership is also divided into two types: distinct ownership and shared ownership.
First: Distinct ownership is that which pertains to a specific thing with boundaries that separate it from others. For example, a person owns a head of sheep, a book, an entire house, a particular floor of a multi-story house, and the like.
Second: Shared or undivided ownership is ownership pertaining to a relative, unspecified part of the total thing, whether that part is large or small. For example, a person owns half of a house, a quarter of a horse, or a hundredth part or more of land, and the like. This is called 'the common share in a joint thing.' And according to the theory of undivided ownership, each part or atom of the common property is not exclusively assigned to any one of the share holders; rather, the ownership of all of them attaches to it. And from this they define the common share as: the share that permeates every part.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of ownership (milk) based on its form (surah). The author identifies two categories: 1) Distinct ownership (milk mumayyaz) – ownership of a specific, physically separable object such as a particular book or a whole floor of a building. 2) Shared undivided ownership (milk sha'i' or musha') – ownership of a proportional, unspecified fraction of a whole, like half a house or a quarter of a horse. In shared ownership, the defining feature is that every atom of the property is owned jointly by all co-owners according to their shares. The common share (hissa shai'ah) is described as 'the share that permeates every part' – meaning it is not localized but spreads throughout the entire property.

Key Terms
الملك المتميز (al-milk al-mumayyaz): Ownership of a specific, physically distinct object.
الملك الشائع (al-milk al-sha'i'): Ownership of an undivided share in a whole, where ownership permeates every part.
الحصة الشائعة (al-hissah al-shai'ah): The common share; a fractional ownership that extends to every part of the joint property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of shared ownership (milk sha'i')?
A) A person owns a specific floor of a building
B) A person owns a quarter of a horse
C) A person owns a particular book
D) A person owns a head of sheep

Key Takeaway
Shared ownership in Hanafi law means each co-owner holds a proportional share that permeates every part of the property — it is not localized.

The Concept of Muhaya'ah and Its Two Types in Hanafi Law
Definition and classification of muhaya'ah as a Hanafi legal concept

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define muhaya'ah, distinguish its two types (temporal and spatial), and apply them to scenarios involving common property.

Core Idea
Muhaya'ah allows co-owners to use common property alternately or in separate portions without physical division, preserving benefits for all.

The Hanafi school recognizes that joint ownership often leads to practical difficulties, especially when benefits must be shared. One solution is muhaya'ah, a contractual arrangement regulating use. This lesson explores its definition, the two types, and how it interacts with the principle of istihsan in modern legal reforms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal context of the passage
The author begins with a 1947 Syrian law that extended compulsory division of common property to movables. This sets the stage for why muhaya'ah becomes necessary as an alternative.
The law is an example of modern legislation aligning with Hanafi istihsan.
2
Extract the essential features of muhaya'ah
From the description, muhaya'ah is an arrangement among co-owners to enjoy the common property without physically dividing it. It requires mutual agreement and is not a sale.
Use the legal definition dissector to capture hukm, sabab, shart, mani, athar.
3
Distinguish temporal and spatial muhaya'ah
Temporal muhāyā'ah (زمانية) involves alternating use of the entire property for fixed periods. Spatial muhāyā'ah (مكانية) involves allocating specific portions for simultaneous use. Use the examples: land vs. house.
For indivisible items like a single animal, only temporal muhaya'ah is possible.
4
Apply the types to practical scenarios
Given a co-owned property, decide which type fits based on divisibility and the nature of the asset. Consider the need for agreement among partners.
The passage implies that muhaya'ah is particularly useful when leasing a common share.
5
Connect muhaya'ah to the principle of istihsan
The author states that the benefit of muhaya'ah is not rejected by istihsan even if strict qiyas (analogy) might oppose it. This illustrates the Hanafi use of juristic preference to facilitate practical solutions.
Istihsan allows departure from analogy for necessity or public interest.

وتظهر فائدة المهايأة في حق إيجار الحصّة الشائعة من عقار أو مال منقول، فإن إيجارها ممكن شرعاً وقانوناً ولا سبيل إلى تنفيذ عقد الإيجار في حق الحصّة المأجورة إلا بطريق التهايؤ مع الشريك غير المؤجر مهايأة زمانية أو مكانية:

والمكانية هي أن يخصص لكل من الشركاء بعض من المال المشترك بنسبة حصته فينتفعون معاً في وقت واحد، كالدار يسكن كل من الشريكين فيها قسماً منها يعادل حصته وكحانوتين مشتركين بين شخصين يستعمل كل منهما واحداً.
ومن الواضح أنه في نحو الحيوان الواحد مما لا يتجزأ لا تمكن المهايأة إلا في الزمان.

Finally, in the year 1366 AH = 1947 CE, Article 3 of the Law of Conciliation Judges was amended by Law No. 353 issued on June 3, 1947. This amended article generalized the ruling of compulsory judicial division of common property to movable property as well, by the method followed legally regarding common immovable property, namely by dividing it if it is divisible, and selling it if it is not divisible. In this there is an apparent benefit by removing the problems of joint ownership whenever they arise from natural causes such as inheritance and others. Such a benefit is not rejected by juristic preference (istihsan) even if analogy (qiyas) rejects it.

The benefit of muhaya'ah appears regarding the leasing of a common share in immovable or movable property. Its lease is permissible legally and jurisprudentially, and there is no way to execute the lease contract regarding the leased share except through alternating use (tahayu') with the non-lessor partner, either temporal or spatial muhaya'ah.

Temporal muhaya'ah is that each of the two partners benefits alternately from the entire common thing for a specified period according to his share, as in land held in common with one-third shares between two persons if they agree that one will cultivate it for one year and the other for two years.

Spatial muhaya'ah is that each of the partners is allocated a portion of the common property according to his share, and they benefit together at the same time, like a house where each partner lives in a section equivalent to his share, or two shops held in common between two persons, each using one.

It is obvious that for something indivisible like a single animal, muhaya'ah is only possible temporally.

Hence, the jurists made muhaya'ah two types: temporal and spatial, so that benefits are divided in everything according to its suitability.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the two types of muhaya'ah (alternating use) in Hanafi fiqh. The author begins by noting a modern Syrian legal amendment that extended compulsory judicial division of common property to movable property, highlighting that such a practical benefit is in line with istihsan (juristic preference) even if opposed by strict qiyas. Then he introduces muhaya'ah as a necessary mechanism when leasing a common share, because without the cooperation of the co-owner, the lease cannot be executed. He defines temporal muhaya'ah (zamanīyah) as alternating use of the entire property for periods proportional to each partner's share, giving the example of land held in thirds where one partner cultivates for one year and the other for two years. Spatial muhaya'ah (makānīyah) is the allocation of separate portions of the property for simultaneous use, like rooms in a house or separate shops. He notes that for indivisible items like a single animal, only temporal muhaya'ah is possible. Finally, he states that jurists categorize muhaya'ah into these two types to appropriately divide benefits according to the nature of the property.

Key Terms
المهايأة (al-muhāyā'ah): A contractual arrangement among co-owners to alternately use the common property or allocate portions for simultaneous use, without physically dividing the property.
إزالة الشيوع (izālat al-shuyū'): The legal process of terminating joint ownership by physical division (qisma) or sale of the property and distribution of proceeds.
الاستحسان (al-istihsān): Juristic preference to depart from strict analogy (qiyas) for a more suitable ruling based on custom, necessity, or public interest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) In land held in thirds
B) In a single animal
C) In two shops
D) In a house with multiple rooms

Key Takeaway
Muhaya'ah is a legitimate Hanafi mechanism to regulate use of common property, divisible temporally or spatially, based on agreement and istihsan.

Muhāyāʾ: Types and Limitations of Temporary Division of Usufruct
Legal definition and classification of muhāyāʾ with a talil-based prohibition rationale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define muhāyāʾ, distinguish between temporal and spatial muhāyāʾ, and identify the prohibition on muhāyāʾ in agricultural land with its rationale.

Core Idea
Muhāyāʾ is a practical solution for co-owners, but it is prohibited in agricultural land to prevent soil degradation.

Co-ownership often requires creative solutions to use shared property without formally dividing it. Muhāyāʾ, a temporary division of usufruct, allows co-owners to enjoy benefits proportionally. This lesson unpacks the two forms of muhāyāʾ—temporal and spatial—and explains a critical limitation: its prohibition in agricultural land, rooted in preserving land productivity.

Guided Path
1
Define muhāyāʾ and its purpose
Identify muhāyāʾ as a mechanism for co-owners to divide usufruct without dividing ownership, particularly for leasing undivided shares.
Not to be confused with formal division (qisma).
2
Distinguish the two types
Temporal muhāyāʾ involves sequential use; spatial muhāyāʾ involves simultaneous use of allocated portions. Note the examples given for each.
Indivisible assets (e.g., a single animal) only allow temporal division.
3
Analyze the prohibition rationale
The passage explains that muhāyāʾ in agricultural land is prohibited because each partner overexploits and neglects maintenance during their turn, harming the land.
This is a talil (rationale) based on public interest (maslaha) and preventing harm.
4
Identify the scope of the prohibition
The prohibition is limited to agricultural land; muhāyāʾ in other properties (houses, shops, movables) remains valid. This corrects a common misunderstanding.

Chapter (24): Division of Ownership and Division of its Causes. The benefit of muhāyāʾ appears in relation to leasing an undivided share of real estate or movable property, for leasing it is possible legally and legally [sic], and there is no way to execute the lease contract with respect to the leased share except through a temporary division (tahāyuʾ) with the non-lessor partner, either temporal or spatial muhāyāʾ. Temporary muhāyāʾ is that each of the two partners benefits from all the common thing successively for a specified period according to the proportion of his share, as in land shared in thirds between two persons if they divide it temporally such that one cultivates it one year and the other two years. Spatial muhāyāʾ is that a part of the common property is allocated to each partner according to the proportion of his share, and they benefit together at the same time, like a house where each partner lives in a portion equal to his share, or two jointly-owned shops where each uses one (al-Majalla articles 1176-1177). It is obvious that in something like a single animal which is indivisible, muhāyāʾ is only possible temporally. Hence the jurists made muhāyāʾ two types: temporal and spatial, so that benefits are divided in everything according to its nature. ... which is without documents is prohibited and remains prohibited). The periodic division intended in this material is muhāyāʾ. The viewpoint for the prohibition is that muhāyāʾ weakens agricultural land because each partner strives during his turn to extract the maximum possible fruits from the land, and he is also stingy in his turn with plowing and fertilizing because the beneficial residues will go to the partner who follows him in the turn of division. It is apparent that this prohibition is confined to agricultural properties and is not an abolition of muhāyāʾ in general as is thought. That which indicates this also is what came in its reasons that the intent is to improve agriculture.

Commentary
This passage is a legal exposition on the concept of muhāyāʾ (temporary division of usufruct) in Hanafi fiqh. It begins by stating the utility of muhāyāʾ in the context of leasing an undivided share of property: since a co-owner cannot unilaterally lease the entire property, muhāyāʾ with the other co-owner allows the lease to be executed by temporally or spatially dividing the benefits. The author then defines the two types: temporal (zamāniyya) where co-owners take turns using the entire property for periods proportional to their shares, and spatial (makāniyya) where each co-owner is allocated a specific portion of the property to use simultaneously. An example of temporal is a jointly owned field where one partner cultivates one year and the other two years; spatial is a shared house where each occupies a portion. The text notes that indivisible things like an animal can only have temporal division. The passage then addresses a prohibition: muhāyāʾ is prohibited in agricultural land because each partner overexploits the land in their turn and neglects maintenance, harming the land. Importantly, this prohibition is limited to agricultural land; muhāyāʾ elsewhere remains valid. This clarifies a common misunderstanding that muhāyāʾ is generally prohibited.

Key Terms
مهايأة (muhāyāʾ): Temporary division of benefits among co-owners without dividing the property itself.
مهايأة زمانية (muhāyāʾ zamāniyya): Co-owners take turns using the entire shared property for periods proportional to their shares.
مهايأة مكانية (muhāyāʾ makāniyya): Co-owners are allocated specific spatial portions of the shared property for simultaneous use.

The Maxim of Complete First Ownership
States a characteristic that initial ownership is always complete.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim that the first ownership of a previously unowned thing is always complete, and distinguish original causes from transfer causes in Hanafi jurisprudence.

Core Idea
The first ownership of any previously unowned thing is always complete—covering both corpus and usufruct.

Ownership is a central concept in Hanafi fiqh. But how does it begin? When a person acquires something that had no previous owner, what kind of ownership do they obtain? This lesson explores a foundational maxim of Hanafi legal philosophy: the first ownership is always complete. Understanding this will sharpen your ability to analyze ownership disputes and correctly classify modes of acquisition.

Guided Path
1
Read the maxim
Memorize the Arabic formulation: 'إن أول ملكية تثبت على الشيء الذي لم يكن مملوكاً قبلها إنما تكون دائماً ملكية تامة'. Understand its literal meaning.
This is a hifz anchor; repetition helps internalize the rule.
2
Understand 'complete ownership'
Complete ownership (ملكية تامة) includes both the corpus (رقبة) and the benefits (منافع). The first owner cannot own only one aspect; both are conferred together.
3
Identify original causes
The text mentions two original causes (سبب منشيء): acquisition of ownerless property (إحراز المباحات) and natural increase from owned property (التولد من المملوك). These create first ownership.
Original causes are creative—they bring property into an ownership relation for the first time.
4
Distinguish transfer causes
After first ownership, any subsequent ownership on the same thing can only occur through a transfer cause (سبب ناقل) such as sale or gift. The owner changes; the property does not restart as unowned.
Transfer causes do not create new ownership; they shift existing ownership.
5
Note the exception
A bequest of usufruct (وصية بالمنفعة) gives only a temporary right to benefits, not first ownership. The underlying corpus was already owned, so it does not violate the maxim.
This is a derivative right, not an original acquisition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

إن أول ملكية تثبت على الشيء الذي لم يكن مملوكاً قبلها إنما تكون
دائماً ملكية تامة . أي إنها تكون شاملة لرقبة الشيء ومنافعه معاً. فلا يمكن أن تثبت الملكية الأولى على الشيء ناقصة مقصورة على رقبته دون منافعه أو على منافعه دون رقبته.
والملكية الأولى التي تثبت على الشيء بسبب منشيء، أي بطريق
هي

ومتى ثبتت تلك الملكية الأولى على شيء بسببها المنشيء لا يتصور بعدها أن يتعلق بذلك الشيء ملك جديد إلا بالسبب الناقل للملكية دون المنشيء، لأن كل ملك يثبت على شيء مملوك قبلاً إنما يكون من قبيل
تبدل المالك، وهذا هو معنى نقل الملكية.
٣٦١
3/25 - Second Characteristic: Every first ownership that is established on a thing that was not owned before it is always complete ownership. That is, it includes both the corpus of the thing and its benefits together. So the first ownership cannot be established on a thing as incomplete, restricted to its corpus without its benefits or to its benefits without its corpus. The first ownership that is established on a thing by an original cause, i.e., by the way of acquisition of ownerless things (ibahat) or by generation from owned property. Once that first ownership is established on a thing by its original cause, it is not conceivable that thereafter a new ownership attaches to that thing except by a cause that transfers ownership, not by an original cause, because every ownership that is established on a thing previously owned is only a case of change of owner, and that is the meaning of transfer of ownership. 361.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage presents a qawida (legal maxim) regarding the characteristic of ownership. It states that when ownership first arises on an object that was previously unowned (e.g., through appropriation of mubah or through natural increase from owned property), it is always 'tam' (complete) – meaning it encompasses both the corpus (raqaba) and the usufruct (manafi'). It cannot be incomplete (naqisa), such as owning only the corpus without the benefits, or only benefits without the corpus. This initial acquisition is termed 'sabab munshi' (original cause). After this first ownership, any subsequent ownership on the same object can only occur through a 'sabab naqil' (transfer cause) – like sale, gift, inheritance – which results in a change of owner (tabaddul al-malik), not a new original acquisition. This maxim is crucial in distinguishing original from derivative modes of acquiring property in Hanafi jurisprudence.

Key Terms
ملكية تامة (milkiyya tāmma): Full ownership that includes both corpus (raqaba) and usufruct (manfa'a).
سبب منشيء (sabab munshi'): An original cause of ownership, such as taking possession of ownerless property (ihraz al-mubahat) or natural increase from owned objects.
سبب ناقل (sabab nāqil): A transfer cause of ownership, such as sale, gift, inheritance, which moves ownership from one person to another without creating new ownership.
تبدل المالك (tabaddul al-mālik): Change of owner, the essential effect of a transfer cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Complete ownership of the fish, including its corpus and benefits.
B) Only the corpus, not the benefits, until he sells it.
C) Only the benefits, the corpus remains ownerless.
D) Incomplete ownership, since the fish had no prior owner.

Key Takeaway
The first ownership of an unowned thing is always complete; any subsequent ownership is by transfer.

The Third Characteristic of Ownership: Perpetuity of Corpus and Time-Bound Usufruct
Expands a key Hanafi ownership rule with furu' cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi legal maxim 'Ownership of corpus is perpetual; ownership of usufruct is inherently time-bound' to classify and rule on property contracts (sale, ijarah, i'arah, wasiyya).

Core Idea
The corpus cannot be time-bound; the usufruct cannot be perpetual.

This lesson unpacks a fundamental Hanafi distinction: owning a physical asset is permanent, while owning its benefit is temporary. This principle shapes the validity of sales, leases, loans, and bequests. Through the hadith on 'umra, we see that even a lifetime gift of the corpus becomes perpetual, affirming that time limits apply only to usufruct.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim
Recite the Arabic maxim: ملكية العين لا تقبل التوقيت، أما ملكية المنفعة فالأصل فيها التوقيت. Note its English equivalent.
This is a sughra (minor) maxim.
2
Identify the scope
Recognize that this maxim applies primarily to contracts transferring ownership or usufruct: sale (buyu'), lease (ijarah), loan (i'arah), and bequest (wasiyya).
3
Work through the furuat
For each case in the text (sale with time limit → invalid; ijarah with term → binding; i'arah with term → revocable; wasiyya of usufruct→ binding on heirs), trace how the maxim yields the ruling.
Pay attention to whether the contract is commutative or gratuitous.
4
Note the istithna'
The text does not mention exceptions to this maxim. However, the 'umra hadith is not an exception but a confirmation: even if a corpus gift is phrased as time-bound, it becomes perpetual.
No istithna' in this passage.
5
Apply the fatwa hierarchy
Realize that this principle is from the fatwa of later Hanafi works (e.g., Radd al-Muhtar) and is settled (mufta bihi). Note that the binding nature differs between commutative (lazim) and gratuitous (ja'iz) contracts.
This determines how a mufti issues rulings.

الخاصة الثالثة : إن ملكية العين لا تقبل التوقيت أما ملكية المنفعة فالأصل فيها التوقيت .. فملكية العين متى ثبتت بأحد أسبابها تثبت مؤيَّدة، وإنما يتصور أن يطرأ عليها بعد ذلك انتقال بسبب جديد ناقل فإن لم يطرأ عليها ناقل من عقد جديد أو خلفية لا تنتهي الملكية التي سبق ثبوتها. وعلى ذلك أثبت الرسول الله في العُمرى (1) الملك الدائم بقوله : أيَّما رجلٍ أُعمِرَ عُمْرى فهي له ، ولعقبه (۲) . (1) العُمْرى (بضم العين والألف المقصورة وزان صغرى :) هي أن يهب إنسان الآخر شيئاً مدى عمره، أي على أنه إذا مات الموهوب له عاد الشيء إلى الواهب. ويكون ذلك بقوله : أعمرتك إياه أو أعطيتكه عمري، ونحو ذلك من العبارات . (۲) رواه مسلم عن جابر في الهبات (١٦٢٥)، والترمذي في الأحكام (۱۳۵۰) وقال : حسن صحيح، وأحمد في المسند ٣٩٩/٣ . وقد فرع الفقهاء على هذا المبدأ أن من شرائط صحة البيع عدم التوقيت، فإذا وُقت فسد (ر: رد المحتار ٦/٤). - أما ملكية المنافع دون الأعيان فالأصل فيها التوقيت، كما في الإعارة، والإجارة، والوصية بمنفعة شيء لشخص مدة محدودة. فمتى انقضت تلك المدة المحدودة انقطع حق الانتفاع. وهذا التوقيت في تمليك المنفعة إذا كان في عقد معاوضة كالإجارة والصلح كان ملزماً للمملك ليس له أن يرجع عنه، لأنه بمثابة بيع مدة معينة، فقد صرّح الفقهاء أن الإجارة هي في الحقيقة نوع من البيع من حيث أنها بيع المنافع، في مقابل الأعيان، كما تقدم (ر: ف ٣/٢٤ بيع ثانياً، ورد المحتار ٢/٥ و (۲۹). وأما إذا كان التوقيت لملك المنفعة واقعاً في عقد التبرع وهو الإعارة فإنه ليس بملزم للمُمَلك، بل يكون للمعير أن يرجع عن الوقت الذي حدده للمستعير إلى وقت أقصر منه كما يجوز له أن يرجع عن أصل الإعارة ويسترد العارية متى شاء(۱) غير أنَّ فقهاءنا لم يهملوا النظر إلى ما يمكن أن تسببه هذه القاعدة (۱) يلحظ هنا أن الوصبة بالمنفعة وإن كانت تبرعاً لا يحق فيها لورثة الموصي بعد موته يرجعوا عن المدة التي حددها مورثهم لانتفاع الموصى له، لأن حق الرجوع إنما كان لمورثهم المالك الموصي، وقد مات فأصبحت الوصية بهذا العارض مبرمة لازمة في أصل المنفعة الموصى بها وفي وقتها المحدد لها، وإن كانت تبرعاً، لأن الورثة إنما ينتقل إليهم في هذه الحال ملك الرقبة فقط مدة انتفاع الموصى له، كما تقدم، وإلا لما استقرت وصيّة أبداً لو أعطي ورثة الموصي حق الرجوع. ولهذا يصرّح الفقهاء بأن انتفاع الموصى له بالمنفعة بعد الموصي إنما يعتبر شرعاً على حساب ملك الموصي لا على حساب ورثته فيعتبر المال الموصى بمنفعته محبوساً على ملك المورّث المتوفى في حق المنفعة الموصى بها وهذا معنى كون الورثة إنما يملكون في هذه الحال رقبة المال فقط، فلا يرثون المنفعة أيضاً إلا بعد انقضاء مدة انتفاع الموصى له أو موته كما تقدم ( ر ) ف ٤/٢٤ ، والدر المختار ورد المحتار ٥/ (٤٤٢).
The third characteristic: Ownership of the corpus does not accept time-bounding, while ownership of usufruct is by nature time-bound. So ownership of the corpus, whenever it is established by one of its causes, is established perpetually; it is only conceivable that thereafter transfer occurs due to a new conveying cause. If no conveyor from a new contract or succession occurs, the previously established ownership does not end. And upon this, the Messenger of Allah (ﷺ) established perpetual ownership in 'umra (1) by his saying: 'Any man who is given a 'umra, it is for him and his descendants' (2). (1) 'Umra (with damm on 'ayn and alif maqsura, like sughra) is that a person gives another something for the duration of his life, meaning that if the donee dies, the thing returns to the donor. It is expressed by saying: 'I give you 'umra of it' or 'I give it to you as 'umra,' and similar expressions. (2) Narrated by Muslim from Jabir in al-Hibat (1625), al-Tirmidhi in al-Ahkam (1350) saying hasan sahih, and Ahmad in al-Musnad 3/399. And the jurists derived from this principle that one of the conditions for the validity of a sale is the absence of time-bounding; if it is time-bound, it is invalid (cf. Radd al-Muhtar 6/4). As for ownership of usufruct without the corpus, it is by nature time-bound, as in i'arah (loan), ijarah (lease), and bequest of the usufruct of something to a person for a limited period. When that limited period ends, the right of usufruct ceases. This time-bounding in the transfer of usufruct, if it is in a commutative contract like ijarah and sulh, is binding upon the transferor; he cannot revoke it, because it is like a sale of a specific period. Indeed, the jurists have stated that ijarah is in reality a type of sale in that it is a sale of usufruct in exchange for consideration, as previously mentioned (cf. Fi 3/24, sale secondly, and Radd al-Muhtar 2/5 and 29). As for when the time-bounding of the usufruct occurs in a gratuitous contract, namely i'arah, it is not binding upon the transferor; rather, the lender may revert from the time he set for the borrower to a shorter time, just as he may revoke the original loan and reclaim the borrowed item whenever he wishes. However, our jurists did not neglect to consider what this rule might cause. (1) It is noted here that the bequest of usufruct, even though it is gratuitous, the heirs of the testator after his death have no right to revert from the period set by their testator for the beneficiary's usufruct, because the right of revocation belonged to their testator, the owner who made the bequest, and he has died, so the bequest becomes final and binding in the very usufruct bequeathed and its specified time, even though it is gratuitous, because the heirs in this case only acquire ownership of the corpus for the duration of the beneficiary's usufruct, as previously mentioned; otherwise, a bequest would never be settled if the testator's heirs were given the right to revoke. For this reason, the jurists explicitly state that the beneficiary's usufruct after the testator is legally considered to be from the estate of the testator, not from the heirs; thus, the property whose usufruct is bequeathed is considered detained under the ownership of the deceased testator with respect to the bequeathed usufruct, and this means that the heirs in this case only own the corpus of the property, and they do not inherit the usufruct until after the beneficiary's period ends or he dies, as previously mentioned (cf. Fi 4/24, al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar 5/442).
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage is a legal principle from the Hanafi school regarding the nature of ownership. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, presents the third characteristic of ownership: ownership of the physical asset (raqaba) is perpetual and cannot be limited by time, while ownership of usufruct (manfa'a) is inherently time-bound. He supports this with the hadith on 'umra, where a gift for life becomes perpetual ownership, illustrating that even a seemingly time-limited grant of the corpus becomes permanent. The jurists derive that a sale contract, which transfers full ownership, cannot have a time limit; if it does, it is invalid. For usufruct, time-bounding is natural. In a commutative contract like ijarah (lease), the time period is binding on both parties because it is essentially a sale of usufruct. In a gratuitous contract like i'arah (loan), the lender can revoke the period or the loan itself at any time, because it is a favor. However, for a bequest (wasiyya) of usufruct, once the testator dies, the bequest becomes binding on the heirs; they cannot shorten the period because the right of revocation died with the testator. The heirs only inherit the corpus during the beneficiary's usufruct period. This is a nuanced application of the principle, ensuring that bequests are upheld.

Key Terms
ملكية العين (milk al-‘ayn): Ownership of the physical substance or corpus of a property.
ملكية المنفعة (milk al-manfa‘a): Ownership of the usufruct or beneficial use of a property.
التوقيت (al-tawqit): Attaching a specific time limit to ownership or a contract.
العمرى (al-‘umra): A gift of property for the donee's lifetime, which by hadith becomes perpetual ownership.
الرقبة (al-raqaba): The corpus or physical substance of a property; synonymous with 'ayn in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle presented, what is the ruling on a sale contract that includes a condition that ownership will revert after one year?
A) Valid, because the buyer owns the corpus for that period.
B) Invalid, because ownership of corpus cannot be time-bound.
C) Valid, if both parties agree.
D) Makruh, but not invalid.

Key Takeaway
Ownership of corpus is perpetual; ownership of usufruct is inherently time-bound, and this distinction governs the validity and binding nature of property contracts.

The Principle of Timing in Ownership of Usufruct vs Corpus
Explains the rule that sale must be unconditional in time while usufruct ownership is inherently timed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi qawida regarding timing in ownership transfers and apply it to distinguish between sale, ijarah, i'arah, and bequest of usufruct.

Core Idea
Sale must be timeless; usufruct ownership is inherently timed — timing binds in commutative contracts but not in gratuitous ones.

This lesson introduces a core Hanafi legal principle: the role of time in ownership transfers. While the sale of a corpus (ayn) must be unconditional regarding time, the transfer of usufruct (manfa'ah) is intrinsically temporary. This distinction underlies different rulings across contracts like ijarah, i'arah, and bequests. By mastering this qawida, you will be able to predict and apply the correct rulings in various property transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the qawida
Read the passage and locate the key sentence: 'أما ملكية المنافع دون الأعيان فالأصل فيها التوقيت'. This is the central legal maxim governing timing in usufruct ownership.
The opposite rule for sale is also stated: 'من شرائط صحة البيع عدم التوقيت'.
2
Understand the scope
The qawida applies to all usufruct transfers, whether by ijarah (lease), i'arah (gratuitous loan), or wasiyyah (bequest). Timing is essential for validity in usufruct ownership.
Sale is excluded: timing invalidates the sale.
3
Distinguish commutative vs gratuitous contracts
In commutative contracts (ijarah, sulh), timing is binding on the transferor; he cannot revoke early. In gratuitous contracts (i'arah), timing is not binding; the lender may revoke at will.
The passage describes ijarah as 'a type of sale of usufruct'.
4
Identify the exception for bequest
A bequest of usufruct is gratuitous, but after the testator's death, the heirs cannot revoke the term. This is because the right to revoke died with the testator.
This exception is explained in the footnote of the passage.
