الفصل (٢٥) خصائص الملكية
وقد فرع الفقهاء على هذا المبدأ أن من شرائط صحة البيع عدم التوقيت، فإذا وُقت فسد (ر: رد المحتار ٦/٤).
- أما ملكية المنافع دون الأعيان فالأصل فيها التوقيت، كما في الإعارة، والإجارة، والوصية بمنفعة شيء لشخص مدة محدودة. فمتى انقضت تلك المدة المحدودة انقطع حق الانتفاع.
وهذا التوقيت في تمليك المنفعة إذا كان في عقد معاوضة كالإجارة والصلح كان ملزماً للمملك ليس له أن يرجع عنه، لأنه بمثابة بيع مدة معينة، فقد صرّح الفقهاء أن الإجارة هي في الحقيقة نوع من البيع من حيث أنها بيع المنافع، في مقابل الأعيان، كما تقدم (ر: ف ٣/٢٤ بيع ثانياً، ورد المحتار ٢/٥ و (۲۹).
وأما إذا كان التوقيت لملك المنفعة واقعاً في عقد التبرع وهو الإعارة فإنه ليس بملزم للمُمَلك، بل يكون للمعير أن يرجع عن الوقت الذي حدده للمستعير إلى وقت أقصر منه كما يجوز له أن يرجع عن أصل الإعارة ويسترد العارية متى شاء(۱)
غير أنَّ فقهاءنا لم يهملوا النظر إلى ما يمكن أن يسببه الرجوع عن توقيت العارية من ضرر للمستعير، كما لو كانت العارية أرضاً أعارها صاحبها إلى غيره للزرع أو الغرس أو البناء مدة معينة، فَزَرَع فيها المستعير أو غرس أو بنى، ثم رجع المعير وطلب استرداد الأرض قبل انقضاء المدة المضروبة .
فقد قرَّرَ الفقهاء في ذلك تفصيلاً:
أولاً : ففي صورة الزرع لا يحق للمعير استرداد الأرض قبل استحصاد الزرع، ولا فرق في صورة إعارة الأرض لأجل الزرع بين أن تكون هذه الإعارة موقتة أو مطلقة بل يبقى الزرع إلى أن يستحصد على الأرض بأجر مثلها عن المدة التي بين تاريخ الرجوع والاستحصاد. وفي ذلك حفظ المعير ومنع الضرر عن المستعير .
ثانياً : وفي صورتي الغرس والبناء يحق للمعير أن يرجع في الأرض. المعارة للغرس والبناء مطلقاً في أي وقت شاء، سواء أكانت الإعارة مطلقة أو موقوتة. وعندئذ يكلف المستعير قلع الغراس أو البناء، وتسليم الأرض إلى المعير خالية عنه وذلك لأنَّ الغرس أو البناء ليس لهما أمد ينتهيان إليه :
كأمد الحصاد للزرع . ولكنها إذا كانت موقوتة فرجع المعير قبل الأجل الذي ضربه للمستعير الغارس أو الباني، فإنَّ المعير يكون قد غَرَّر بالمستعير تغريراً أضر به، فيحق للمستعير تضمين المعير مقدار تفاوت قيمة البناء أو الغراس بين وقت القلع وانتهاء المدة التي كانت مضروبة للإعارة.
فلو كان البناء أو الشجر المقلوع قيمته حين الرجوع والقلع اثني عشر ديناراً، وقيمته في ذلك الحين على أساس بقائه إلى انتهاء الأجل المضروب للإعارة عشرون ديناراً يضمن المعير للمستعير ثمانية دنانير (ر:
المجلة / ۸۳۱ - ۸۳٢ والدر المختار ٥٠٥/٤) .

(۱) يلحظ هنا أن الوصبة بالمنفعة وإن كانت تبرعاً لا يحق فيها لورثة الموصي بعد موته يرجعوا عن المدة التي حددها مورثهم لانتفاع الموصى له، لأن حق الرجوع إنما كان لمورثهم المالك الموصي، وقد مات فأصبحت الوصية بهذا العارض مبرمة لازمة في أصل المنفعة الموصى بها وفي وقتها المحدد لها، وإن كانت تبرعاً، لأن الورثة إنما ينتقل إليهم في هذه الحال ملك الرقبة فقط مدة انتفاع الموصى له، كما تقدم، وإلا لما استقرت وصيّة أبداً لو أعطي ورثة الموصي حق الرجوع.
ولهذا يصرّح الفقهاء بأن انتفاع الموصى له بالمنفعة بعد الموصي إنما يعتبر شرعاً على حساب ملك الموصي لا على حساب ورثته فيعتبر المال الموصى بمنفعته محبوساً على ملك المورّث المتوفى في حق المنفعة الموصى بها وهذا معنى كون الورثة إنما يملكون في هذه الحال رقبة المال فقط، فلا يرثون المنفعة أيضاً إلا بعد انقضاء مدة انتفاع الموصى له أو موته كما تقدم ( ر ) ف ٤/٢٤ ، والدر المختار ورد المحتار ٥/٣٦٣ .
Chapter (25): Characteristics of Ownership
The jurists have derived from this principle that one of the conditions for the validity of a sale is the absence of timing; if it is timed, it is void (see: Radd al-Muhtar 6/4).
- As for ownership of usufruct without the corpus, the principle in it is timing, as in i'arah (gratuitous loan), ijarah (lease), and bequest of the usufruct of something to a person for a limited period. When that limited period ends, the right of enjoyment ceases.
This timing in the transfer of usufruct, if it is in a commutative contract like ijarah and sulh (compromise), is binding on the transferor; he cannot revoke it, because it is like a sale of a specific period. The jurists have stated that ijarah is in reality a type of sale in that it is a sale of usufruct, as opposed to the corpus, as previously mentioned (see: vol. 3/24, sale secondly, and Radd al-Muhtar 2/5 and (29)).
As for when the timing of the ownership of usufruct occurs in a gratuitous contract, which is i'arah, it is not binding on the transferor; rather, the lender may revoke the period he set for the borrower to a shorter period, and he may also revoke the i'arah itself and reclaim the borrowed item whenever he wishes. (1)
However, our jurists did not neglect to consider the harm that revoking the timing of i'arah could cause to the borrower, such as if the borrowed item is land that its owner lent to another for planting, planting trees, or building for a specific period, and the borrower planted, planted trees, or built, and then the lender revoked and demanded the return of the land before the end of the set period.
They detailed the ruling as follows:
First: In the case of planting crops, the lender does not have the right to reclaim the land before the harvest. There is no difference in the case of lending land for planting whether the loan is timed or absolute; rather, the crop remains on the land until it is harvested, with the payment of similar rent for the period between the revocation and harvest. This preserves the lender's right and prevents harm to the borrower.
Second: In the cases of planting trees and construction, the lender has the right to revoke the land lent for planting trees and construction absolutely at any time he wishes, whether the loan is absolute or timed. In that case, the borrower is required to uproot the trees or demolish the building and surrender the land free of them, because planting trees and construction do not have a natural end like the harvest season of crops. However, if the loan was timed and the lender revoked before the term he set for the borrower who planted trees or built, then the lender has deceived the borrower, causing him harm. The borrower is entitled to claim compensation from the lender for the difference in the value of the building or trees between the time of uprooting and the time of the end of the set loan period.
For example, if the value of the building or uprooted trees at the time of revocation and uprooting is twelve dinars, and its value at that time based on its remaining until the end of the loan period is twenty dinars, the lender compensates the borrower eight dinars (see: Majalla articles 831-832, and al-Durr al-Mukhtar 4/505).

(1) It is noted here that a bequest of usufruct, although gratuitous, the heirs of the testator after his death do not have the right to revoke the period set by their decedent for the beneficiary's enjoyment, because the right of revocation was only for their decedent, the owner testator, and he has died. Thus, the bequest becomes binding and obligatory regarding the usufruct and its specified time, even though it is gratuitous, because the heirs only inherit the ownership of the corpus during the period of the beneficiary's enjoyment, as previously mentioned. Otherwise, no bequest would ever be settled if the testator's heirs were given the right to revoke. Therefore, the jurists state that the beneficiary's enjoyment of the usufruct after the testator's death is considered shar'i from the account of the testator's property, not from his heirs' account. Thus, the property whose usufruct is bequeathed is considered detained in the ownership of the deceased testator regarding the bequeathed usufruct. This is the meaning that the heirs only own the corpus in this case; they do not inherit the usufruct until after the term of the beneficiary's enjoyment expires or he dies, as previously mentioned (see: vol. 4/24, and al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar 5/363).
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage discusses a key principle in Hanafi property law: the role of timing in different types of ownership transfers. The author begins by stating the general rule for sale: a sale must be unconditional regarding time; if it is timed, it is void. This is because sale transfers ownership of the corpus (عين) permanently. Then, the passage turns to ownership of usufruct (منفعة), which is inherently temporary. In commutative contracts like ijarah (lease) and sulh (compromise), the timing is binding on the transferor because it is effectively a sale of usufruct for a period. However, in gratuitous contracts like i'arah (loan for use), the timing is not binding; the lender may revoke the loan or shorten the period at will. But the Hanafi jurists did not ignore the potential harm to the borrower. They provided detailed rules: if the borrowed land was used for crops, the lender cannot reclaim it until harvest, regardless of whether the loan was timed or absolute. If the land was used for trees or buildings, the lender can demand immediate removal, but if the loan was timed and he revokes early, he must compensate the borrower for the loss in value. A footnote explains that a bequest of usufruct is an exception: after the testator's death, the heirs cannot revoke the bequest because the right to revoke died with the testator. This passage exemplifies the Hanafi methodology of balancing legal principles with practical justice.

Key Terms
التوقيت (al-tawqit): Specifying a time limit for the validity or duration of an ownership transfer.
الإعارة (al-i'arah): A contract granting gratuitous use of an object for a period.
المنفعة (al-manfa'ah): The usufruct or benefit derived from an object without consuming its substance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi law, if a person lends land to another for planting crops for a specified period, and then the lender revokes before the harvest, what is the initial ruling?
A) The lender can immediately take back the land, and the borrower must remove the crops at his own expense.
B) The lender must wait until the harvest, but the borrower must pay rent for the remaining period after revocation.
C) The lender can take back the land, but must compensate the borrower for the full value of the crops.
D) The lender cannot revoke at all because the loan was timed.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi qawida that usufruct ownership is inherently timed distinguishes binding commutative contracts from non-binding gratuitous ones, with a binding exception for bequests after death.

Harm Prevention in Voluntary Usufruct Transfer: I'arah and its Detailed Rulings
Details the harm prevention rulings for i'arah when the lender revokes, covering crops, trees, and buildings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi detailed rulings for i'arah when the lender revokes, distinguishing between crops, trees, and buildings, and calculate compensation for early revocation.

Core Idea
In i'arah, the lender may revoke, but harm to the borrower is prevented by allowing crops to harvest and compensating for trees/buildings.

While i'arah (gratuitous loan) is non-binding in Hanafi law, the jurists developed detailed protections for the borrower to prevent injustice. This lesson examines the specific rulings when the borrowed land is used for crops, planting trees, or construction. You will learn when the lender must wait, when the borrower must remove improvements, and how compensation is calculated for early revocation of a timed loan.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general rule for i'arah
Recall that i'arah is non-binding; the lender can revoke anytime. However, this can cause harm to the borrower who has invested effort.
The passage provides three scenarios based on the type of use.
2
Analyze the first scenario: crops
If the borrowed land is used for crops, the lender cannot reclaim it before harvest, regardless of whether the loan was timed or absolute. The crop remains until harvest, and the borrower pays rent for the period after revocation.
This prevents total loss of the crop.
3
Analyze the second scenario: trees/buildings (general rule)
If the land is used for planting trees or building, the lender can demand immediate removal at any time, even if the loan was timed. The borrower must uproot/demolish and surrender the land clear.
Unlike crops, trees and buildings have no natural end point.
4
Analyze the third scenario: timed loan for trees/buildings with early revocation
If the loan was timed and the lender revokes before the term ends, the borrower is entitled to compensation for the decrease in value of the trees/buildings due to early removal.
The compensation equals the difference between value at revocation and value if left until the end of the term.
5
Calculate compensation using the example
The passage gives an example: if value at revocation is 12 dinars, and value if left until end of term is 20 dinars, the lender pays 8 dinars compensation.
This compensates for the loss of the remaining benefit.

الفصل (٢٥) خصائص الملكية
وقد فرع الفقهاء على هذا المبدأ أن من شرائط صحة البيع عدم التوقيت، فإذا وُقت فسد (ر: رد المحتار ٦/٤).
- أما ملكية المنافع دون الأعيان فالأصل فيها التوقيت، كما في الإعارة، والإجارة، والوصية بمنفعة شيء لشخص مدة محدودة. فمتى انقضت تلك المدة المحدودة انقطع حق الانتفاع.
وهذا التوقيت في تمليك المنفعة إذا كان في عقد معاوضة كالإجارة والصلح كان ملزماً للمملك ليس له أن يرجع عنه، لأنه بمثابة بيع مدة معينة، فقد صرّح الفقهاء أن الإجارة هي في الحقيقة نوع من البيع من حيث أنها بيع المنافع، في مقابل الأعيان، كما تقدم (ر: ف ٣/٢٤ بيع ثانياً، ورد المحتار ٢/٥ و (۲۹).
وأما إذا كان التوقيت لملك المنفعة واقعاً في عقد التبرع وهو الإعارة فإنه ليس بملزم للمُمَلك، بل يكون للمعير أن يرجع عن الوقت الذي حدده للمستعير إلى وقت أقصر منه كما يجوز له أن يرجع عن أصل الإعارة ويسترد العارية متى شاء(۱)
غير أنَّ فقهاءنا لم يهملوا النظر إلى ما يمكن أن يسببه الرجوع عن توقيت العارية من ضرر للمستعير، كما لو كانت العارية أرضاً أعارها صاحبها إلى غيره للزرع أو الغرس أو البناء مدة معينة، فَزَرَع فيها المستعير أو غرس أو بنى، ثم رجع المعير وطلب استرداد الأرض قبل انقضاء المدة المضروبة .
فقد قرَّرَ الفقهاء في ذلك تفصيلاً:
أولاً : ففي صورة الزرع لا يحق للمعير استرداد الأرض قبل استحصاد الزرع، ولا فرق في صورة إعارة الأرض لأجل الزرع بين أن تكون هذه الإعارة موقتة أو مطلقة بل يبقى الزرع إلى أن يستحصد على الأرض بأجر مثلها عن المدة التي بين تاريخ الرجوع والاستحصاد. وفي ذلك حفظ المعير ومنع الضرر عن المستعير .
ثانياً : وفي صورتي الغرس والبناء يحق للمعير أن يرجع في الأرض. المعارة للغرس والبناء مطلقاً في أي وقت شاء، سواء أكانت الإعارة مطلقة أو موقوتة. وعندئذ يكلف المستعير قلع الغراس أو البناء، وتسليم الأرض إلى المعير خالية عنه وذلك لأنَّ الغرس أو البناء ليس لهما أمد ينتهيان إليه :
كأمد الحصاد للزرع . ولكنها إذا كانت موقوتة فرجع المعير قبل الأجل الذي ضربه للمستعير الغارس أو الباني، فإنَّ المعير يكون قد غَرَّر بالمستعير تغريراً أضر به، فيحق للمستعير تضمين المعير مقدار تفاوت قيمة البناء أو الغراس بين وقت القلع وانتهاء المدة التي كانت مضروبة للإعارة.
فلو كان البناء أو الشجر المقلوع قيمته حين الرجوع والقلع اثني عشر ديناراً، وقيمته في ذلك الحين على أساس بقائه إلى انتهاء الأجل المضروب للإعارة عشرون ديناراً يضمن المعير للمستعير ثمانية دنانير (ر:
المجلة / ۸۳۱ - ۸۳٢ والدر المختار ٥٠٥/٤) .

(۱) يلحظ هنا أن الوصبة بالمنفعة وإن كانت تبرعاً لا يحق فيها لورثة الموصي بعد موته يرجعوا عن المدة التي حددها مورثهم لانتفاع الموصى له، لأن حق الرجوع إنما كان لمورثهم المالك الموصي، وقد مات فأصبحت الوصية بهذا العارض مبرمة لازمة في أصل المنفعة الموصى بها وفي وقتها المحدد لها، وإن كانت تبرعاً، لأن الورثة إنما ينتقل إليهم في هذه الحال ملك الرقبة فقط مدة انتفاع الموصى له، كما تقدم، وإلا لما استقرت وصيّة أبداً لو أعطي ورثة الموصي حق الرجوع.
ولهذا يصرّح الفقهاء بأن انتفاع الموصى له بالمنفعة بعد الموصي إنما يعتبر شرعاً على حساب ملك الموصي لا على حساب ورثته فيعتبر المال الموصى بمنفعته محبوساً على ملك المورّث المتوفى في حق المنفعة الموصى بها وهذا معنى كون الورثة إنما يملكون في هذه الحال رقبة المال فقط، فلا يرثون المنفعة أيضاً إلا بعد انقضاء مدة انتفاع الموصى له أو موته كما تقدم ( ر ) ف ٤/٢٤ ، والدر المختار ورد المحتار ٥/٣٦٣ .
Chapter (25): Characteristics of Ownership
The jurists have derived from this principle that one of the conditions for the validity of a sale is the absence of timing; if it is timed, it is void (see: Radd al-Muhtar 6/4).
- As for ownership of usufruct without the corpus, the principle in it is timing, as in i'arah (gratuitous loan), ijarah (lease), and bequest of the usufruct of something to a person for a limited period. When that limited period ends, the right of enjoyment ceases.
This timing in the transfer of usufruct, if it is in a commutative contract like ijarah and sulh (compromise), is binding on the transferor; he cannot revoke it, because it is like a sale of a specific period. The jurists have stated that ijarah is in reality a type of sale in that it is a sale of usufruct, as opposed to the corpus, as previously mentioned (see: vol. 3/24, sale secondly, and Radd al-Muhtar 2/5 and (29)).
As for when the timing of the ownership of usufruct occurs in a gratuitous contract, which is i'arah, it is not binding on the transferor; rather, the lender may revoke the period he set for the borrower to a shorter period, and he may also revoke the i'arah itself and reclaim the borrowed item whenever he wishes. (1)
However, our jurists did not neglect to consider the harm that revoking the timing of i'arah could cause to the borrower, such as if the borrowed item is land that its owner lent to another for planting, planting trees, or building for a specific period, and the borrower planted, planted trees, or built, and then the lender revoked and demanded the return of the land before the end of the set period.
They detailed the ruling as follows:
First: In the case of planting crops, the lender does not have the right to reclaim the land before the harvest. There is no difference in the case of lending land for planting whether the loan is timed or absolute; rather, the crop remains on the land until it is harvested, with the payment of similar rent for the period between the revocation and harvest. This preserves the lender's right and prevents harm to the borrower.
Second: In the cases of planting trees and construction, the lender has the right to revoke the land lent for planting trees and construction absolutely at any time he wishes, whether the loan is absolute or timed. In that case, the borrower is required to uproot the trees or demolish the building and surrender the land free of them, because planting trees and construction do not have a natural end like the harvest season of crops. However, if the loan was timed and the lender revoked before the term he set for the borrower who planted trees or built, then the lender has deceived the borrower, causing him harm. The borrower is entitled to claim compensation from the lender for the difference in the value of the building or trees between the time of uprooting and the time of the end of the set loan period.
For example, if the value of the building or uprooted trees at the time of revocation and uprooting is twelve dinars, and its value at that time based on its remaining until the end of the loan period is twenty dinars, the lender compensates the borrower eight dinars (see: Majalla articles 831-832, and al-Durr al-Mukhtar 4/505).

(1) It is noted here that a bequest of usufruct, although gratuitous, the heirs of the testator after his death do not have the right to revoke the period set by their decedent for the beneficiary's enjoyment, because the right of revocation was only for their decedent, the owner testator, and he has died. Thus, the bequest becomes binding and obligatory regarding the usufruct and its specified time, even though it is gratuitous, because the heirs only inherit the ownership of the corpus during the period of the beneficiary's enjoyment, as previously mentioned. Otherwise, no bequest would ever be settled if the testator's heirs were given the right to revoke. Therefore, the jurists state that the beneficiary's enjoyment of the usufruct after the testator's death is considered shar'i from the account of the testator's property, not from his heirs' account. Thus, the property whose usufruct is bequeathed is considered detained in the ownership of the deceased testator regarding the bequeathed usufruct. This is the meaning that the heirs only own the corpus in this case; they do not inherit the usufruct until after the term of the beneficiary's enjoyment expires or he dies, as previously mentioned (see: vol. 4/24, and al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar 5/363).
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage discusses a key principle in Hanafi property law: the role of timing in different types of ownership transfers. The author begins by stating the general rule for sale: a sale must be unconditional regarding time; if it is timed, it is void. This is because sale transfers ownership of the corpus (عين) permanently. Then, the passage turns to ownership of usufruct (منفعة), which is inherently temporary. In commutative contracts like ijarah (lease) and sulh (compromise), the timing is binding on the transferor because it is effectively a sale of usufruct for a period. However, in gratuitous contracts like i'arah (loan for use), the timing is not binding; the lender may revoke the loan or shorten the period at will. But the Hanafi jurists did not ignore the potential harm to the borrower. They provided detailed rules: if the borrowed land was used for crops, the lender cannot reclaim it until harvest, regardless of whether the loan was timed or absolute. If the land was used for trees or buildings, the lender can demand immediate removal, but if the loan was timed and he revokes early, he must compensate the borrower for the loss in value. A footnote explains that a bequest of usufruct is an exception: after the testator's death, the heirs cannot revoke the bequest because the right to revoke died with the testator. This passage exemplifies the Hanafi methodology of balancing legal principles with practical justice.

Key Terms
التوقيت (al-tawqit): Specifying a time limit for the duration of the loan.
الزرع (al-zar'): Crops planted in the land.
الغرس والبناء (al-ghars wa al-bina'): Planting trees or constructing buildings.
تضمين (tadmeen): Compensation for loss or damage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi detailed rulings for i'arah, if a lender lends land for planting trees for a fixed period and then revokes early, what is the borrower entitled to?
A) The borrower can keep the trees until they naturally die.
B) The borrower must remove the trees at his own expense with no compensation.
C) The borrower must remove the trees, but the lender must compensate for the decrease in value caused by early removal.
D) The lender cannot revoke early under any circumstances.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi law protects the borrower in i'arah by requiring the lender to wait for crops to harvest and compensating for early revocation of timed loans involving trees or buildings.

Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications

Ownership of Physical Objects Cannot Be Abandoned – A Hanafi Qawida with Applications
Statement of a qawida with furuat and istithna'at

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qawida that ownership of physical objects (a'yan) does not accept isqat (abandonment) but only naql (transfer), identify the furuat (applications) and istithna'at (exceptions), and construct a fatwa on related property questions.

Core Idea
Ownership of physical objects can only be transferred, never dropped; exceptions like waqf and itq prove the rule.

This passage from Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm presents a fundamental principle of Hanafi property law: milkiyyat al-a'yan (ownership of physical objects) does not accept isqat (unilateral abandonment) but only accepts naql (transfer). The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, explains the rationale through the prohibition of sa'ibah (pre-Islamic release of camels) and the rules of ibra' (discharge) for physical objects vs. debts. Two well-known exceptions—waqf (endowment) and itq (manumission)—are discussed, demonstrating that even these transfers involve a form of transfer or dedication, not mere abandonment.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the Qawida
Internalize the wording: 'Inna milkiyyat al-a'yan la taqbal al-isqat, wa innama taqbal al-naql.' This is the foundational principle.
Note: The qawida is a sughra (minor) universal rule.
2
Understand the Underlying Rationale
Ownership is a legal relationship that requires a subject and an object. Abandonment (isqat) leaves the object without an owner, leading to chaos. Islam prohibits practices like sa'ibah where property is released without transfer.
3
Apply to the First Furu': Ibra' for Physical Objects
Examine that ibra' (discharge) from a physical object (e.g., returning a borrowed item) is invalid because it involves isqat of ownership. Contrast with ibra' from a debt (right in dhimma) which is valid.
Key distinction: rights in the dhimma can be dropped; rights in ayn cannot.
4
Apply to Sa'ibah and Hunting
The Quranic prohibition of sa'ibah and the rule that a released hunted animal remains the property of the hunter illustrate that unilateral abandonment does not extinguish ownership.
Even if the animal is physically free, ownership persists.
5
Recognize the Exceptions: Waqf and Itq
Waqf and itq appear to be isqat, but they serve higher purposes. In waqf, ownership is dropped to create a perpetual charity; in itq, the slave becomes free. These are allowed by shari'ah and are not considered sa'ibah because they involve a transfer of benefit or freedom.
The istithna'at are specific and should not be generalized.

الفصل (٢٥) خصائص الملكية
٦/٢٥ - الخاصة الرابعة :
إن ملكية الأعيان لا تقبل الإسقاط، وإنما تقبل النقل.
فلو أسقط أحد ملكيته عن شيء مملوك له لا تسقط ويبقى ملكاً له .
،
وعلى هذا الأساس منع الإسلام السائبة وهي الشيء يخرج عن ملك مالكه لا إلى مالك جديد وقد كانت العرب قبل الإسلام تعتاد التسييب في الإبل في حالات خاصة فأبطله القرآن بالنص الصريح. حتى لو صيد الحيوان ثم أُطلق يبقى على ملك صائده الأول. فلو وقع في يد شخص آخر وعرف فللأول استرداده منه .
وقد بنى الفقهاء على ذلك عدم صحة الإبراء عن الأعيان، لما في الإبراء من معنى الإسقاط مشوباً بمعنى التمليك. فلو كان لأحد عند آخر شيء مغصوب أو مودَع فأبرأه عنه لا يصح الإبراء، ويبقى الشيء ملكاً لصاحبه .
لو تلف المغصوب في يد الغاصب حتى وجب عليه ضمان قيمته، فأبرأه المالك عنها يصح إبراؤه لانتقال الحق إلى الذمة، والحقوق في الذمم تقبل الإسقاط.
٧/٢٥ - استثناء الوقف والعتق :
ويستثنى من عدم قابلية ملكية العين للإسقاط أمران :
۱ - الوقف في رأي من يرى الوقف إسقاطاً لملكية الواقف لا تبرعاً ؛ فقد خرجت به عين الموقوف عن ملكية الواقف لا إلى مالك جديد .
لكن هذا لا يعد من السائبة، لأنَّ فيه إلى جانب إسقاط الملكية تبرعاً دائماً بالمنفعة لجهة خيرية يتوقف على خروج العين الموقوفة عن ملكية الأفراد. ولذا يعرف الفقهاء الوقف بأنه :
حبس العين على حكم ملك الله تعالى، والتصدق بالمنفعة».
Chapter (25) Characteristics of Ownership. 6/25 - The Fourth Characteristic: Ownership of physical objects (a'yan) does not accept abandonment (isqat); rather, it accepts transfer. If one were to drop his ownership of a thing owned by him, it is not dropped and remains his property. On this basis, Islam prohibited the sa'ibah, which is something that goes out of the ownership of its owner not to a new owner. The Arabs before Islam used to practice releasing camels (tasyeeb) in special cases, so the Quran abolished it with explicit text. Even if an animal is hunted and then released, it remains the property of its original hunter. If it falls into the hand of another and he knows it, the first owner may reclaim it. The jurists based on this the invalidity of discharging (ibra') regarding physical objects, because ibra' contains the meaning of dropping mixed with the meaning of transferring ownership. If someone has a thing belonging to another that is usurped or deposited, and he discharges (yubri'u) him from it, the discharge is invalid, and the thing remains the property of its owner. If the usurped object perishes in the hands of the usurper so that compensation of its value becomes obligatory, then the owner's discharge from that compensation is valid because the right has transferred to the liability (dhimma), and rights in liabilities accept dropping. 7/25 - Exception of Waqf and Itq: Two exceptions are made from the non-susceptibility of ownership of physical objects to dropping: 1. Waqf according to the view that considers waqf to be a dropping of the founder's ownership, not a donation; for by it the waqf object leaves the ownership of the founder to no new owner. But this is not considered sa'ibah, because alongside the dropping of ownership there is a permanent donation of usufruct to a charitable cause, which depends on the object leaving individual ownership. Hence the jurists define waqf as: 'detention of the object under the rule of Allah's ownership, and giving in charity the usufruct.'
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage from Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm discusses the fourth characteristic of ownership: that ownership of physical objects (a'yan) cannot be unilaterally abandoned (isqat); it can only be transferred to another owner. The author provides several applications: the Islamic prohibition of sa'ibah (releasing ownership with no new owner), the rule that a released hunted animal remains the property of the hunter, and the invalidity of ibra' (discharge) regarding physical objects. However, rights in the dhimma (liability) can be dropped, e.g., when a usurped item is destroyed and compensation becomes a debt. Two exceptions are mentioned: waqf (endowment) according to the view that it is an isqat of the founder's ownership, and itq (manumission) of a slave. In waqf, the ownership is dropped but the usufruct is given perpetually to charity, so it is not like sa'ibah. This principle is foundational in Hanafi property law and has implications for transactions and legal stratagems.

Key Terms
ملكية الأعيان (milkiyyat al-aʿyān): Ownership of specific physical objects, as opposed to debts or usufructs.
إسقاط (isqāṭ): A unilateral abandonment or dropping of a right without transferring to another.
السائبة (al-sāʾibah): An animal (especially camel) that was released by its owner without transferring ownership to anyone, a pre-Islamic practice.
الإبراء (al-ibraʾ): A legal discharge from a right, often used for debts.
الذمة (al-dhimmah): A person's legal liability or capacity to owe obligations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle discussed, if a person's property is stolen and then the owner says to the thief, 'I discharge you from the stolen item,' what is the ruling?
A) The discharge is valid and the thief becomes the owner.
B) The discharge is invalid; the property remains the owner's.
C) The discharge is valid only if the item has been destroyed.
D) The discharge is invalid unless the thief returns the item.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle that ownership of physical objects cannot be abandoned but only transferred is a cornerstone of property law, with clearly defined exceptions for waqf and itq.

Common Ownership: General Rule and Mortgage Exception
This passage presents a legal maxim (qawida) on common ownership and its exception for mortgage, with a modern adaptation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim on common ownership, distinguish actual from verbal dispositions, and apply the exception for mortgage, including the modern adaptation via registration.

Core Idea
Common ownership permits disposition like distinct ownership, except where a legal obstacle (mani') exists—mortgage requires continuous possession, so it's invalid on a common share unless registration overcomes the obstacle.

This lesson explores a key principle in Hanafi property law: the capacity to dispose of a common share. While most verbal contracts on a common share are valid, three contracts—mortgage, gift, and lease—are exceptions due to their unique requirements. We will focus on the mortgage exception, understanding its rationale and how modern registration systems have adapted the ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim
Identify the general rule: common ownership is like distinct ownership in capacity for disposition, except for an obstacle.
2
Distinguish actual vs. verbal dispositions
Classify dispositions into actual (physical acts harming partners) and verbal (contracts). Note that actual acts are restricted, while verbal acts are generally permissible.
3
Identify the three excepted contracts
List the contracts that are exceptions: mortgage (rahn), gift (hiba), and lease (ijara). Understand that the passage elaborates only on mortgage.
4
Explain the mortgage exception
Understand why mortgage is invalid on a common share: mortgage requires continuous possession (istimrar al-habs) of the entire property, which is impossible without harming the co-owner or losing security.
5
Apply the modern adaptation
See how modern land registration systems remove the obstacle, making a mortgage on a common share valid, as cited by the author with reference to civil codes.

٢٥/ ٨ - الخاصة الخامسة :
إنَّ الملكية الشائعة في الأعيان المادية هي في الأصل، كالملكية المتميّزة المعيَّنة في قابلية التصرف، إلا لمانع.
فوجود المانع هو حالة استثنائية من المبدأ وهذا المانع يتصور بالنسبة إلى التصرف الفعلي وبالنسبة إلى التصرف القولي :
أ - ففي التصرُّف الفعلي، يمتنع على صاحب الحصة الشائعة كل ما يمس حقوق الشريك الآخر فليس له إتلاف حصته لأنه يستتبع إتلاف حصة شريكه لعدم التميز ؛ وإنما له أن يتبادل الانتفاع بالشيء المشترك على سبيل المهايأة بنسبة حصته.
ب - وأما التصرُّفات القولية فتجري في الحصة الشائعة كما في الحصة المتميزة، فيصحُ بيع الحصة الشائعة من شيء والصلح عنها، ووقفها، والوصية بها، سواء أكان البائع أو العاقد مالكاً لتلك الحصة المعقود عليها فقط، أو مالكاً للشيء كاملاً، لأنَّ التصرُّف في بعض الشيء المملوك جائز كالتصرف فيه كله.
غير أنه يستثنى من ذلك ثلاثة عقود اعتبر الفقهاء في طبيعتها وشرائطها ما يمنع إجراءها على الحصة الشائعة إما منعاً مطلقاً، أو في حال دون حال.
وهذه العقود المستثناة هي الرهن، والهبة، والإجارة.
٢٥/ ٩ - وهذا المنع بالنسبة إلى الرَّهن وجيه فإن الرهن يتنافى مع شيوع المرهون في نظر الفقهاء؛ لأنه - بحسب غايته وهي توثيق استيفاء الدين - يشترط فيه قبض المرهون ثم يقتضي ذلك استمرار حبسه في يد المرتهن. وهذا مانع من صحة الرهن في الجزء الشائع، إذ لو رهن الإنسان حصة شائعة كربع فرس أو نصف دار فإذا احتبس المرتهن المال كله دائماً كان متجاوزاً على حق الشريك غير الراهن لحبس حصته من المال عنه بسبب الشيوع؛ وإذا تناوب المرتهن مع مالك الحصة غير المرهونة في الحبس والاستعمال بحيث يحتبسه بحكم الرهن مدة، ويسلمه إليه مدة للاستعمال، فإنه يفوت عندئذ استمرار احتباس المرهون فيفقد الرهن قوته التوثيقية ويعدم غايته.
وهذا المعنى يقتضي أن يصح الرهن العقاري اليوم على الحصة الشائعة في البلاد التي أسست فيها سجلات ونظم عقارية توجب من تسجيل كل عقد عقاري في تلك السجلات. وهذا ما قد قرره القانون المدني لدينا إذ نَصَّت المادة / ١٠٥٨ منه على أن كل عقار جاز بیعه جاز
25/8 - The fifth characteristic: Common ownership in material things is originally like distinct, specified ownership in capacity for disposition, except due to an obstacle. The presence of an obstacle is an exceptional case from the principle, and this obstacle is conceivable regarding actual disposition and verbal disposition: A) In actual disposition, the owner of a common share is prohibited from anything that affects the rights of the other partner; he may not destroy his share because it entails destroying the partner's share due to lack of distinction. He may alternate use of the common thing by way of apportionment in proportion to his share. B) As for verbal dispositions, they apply to the common share as they do to the distinct share. So the sale of a common share of something, settlement regarding it, its endowment, and bequest by it are valid, whether the seller or party is owner of only that contracted share or of the whole thing, because disposition in part of owned property is permissible like disposition in all of it. However, three contracts are excepted from this, the jurists having considered that their nature and conditions prevent their execution on a common share, either absolutely or in some cases. These excepted contracts are: mortgage, gift, and lease. 25/9 - This prohibition regarding mortgage is sound, for mortgage conflicts with the commonness of the mortgaged property in the view of the jurists; because - according to its purpose being securing repayment of a debt - it requires taking possession of the mortgaged property and then necessitates its continuous detention in the hand of the mortgagee. This prevents the validity of a mortgage of a common part, for if a person mortgages a common share like a quarter of a horse or half a house, then if the mortgagee detains the entire property permanently, he transgresses upon the right of the non-mortgaging partner by detaining his share of the property due to commonness; and if the mortgagee alternates with the owner of the non-mortgaged share in detention and use such that he detains it by virtue of the mortgage for a period and delivers it to him for use for a period, then the continuous detention of the mortgaged property is lost, weakening the mortgage's security and defeating its purpose. This meaning entails that today a real estate mortgage on a common share is valid in countries where land registries and systems have been established that require the registration of every real estate contract in those registries. This is what our civil code has decided, as Article 1058 thereof stipulates that every property that may be sold may be mortgaged.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage from Chapter 25 of al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm presents the fifth characteristic of ownership, which is a legal maxim (qawida) concerning common ownership (al-milkiyya al-sha'i'a). The author states the general rule: common ownership is originally identical to distinct ownership in its capacity for legal disposition (qabiliyyat al-tasarruf), except where an obstacle (mani') exists. He distinguishes two types of disposition: actual (fi'li) and verbal (qawli). In actual disposition, the co-owner cannot unilaterally destroy or consume the common property because it would harm the other co-owner; he may only use it through mutual access (muhaya'a). In verbal dispositions (contracts), common shares are treated like distinct shares: sale, settlement, waqf, and bequest are valid. However, three contracts are excepted: mortgage (rahn), gift (hiba), and lease (ijara). The passage then elaborates on mortgage: the prohibition is justified because the nature of a mortgage requires continuous possession (qabd) and detention (habs) of the entire mortgaged property to secure the debt. On a common share, this is impossible without injustice to the other co-owner. Either the mortgagee detains the whole thing and oppresses the partner, or he alternates detention, losing the continuous security. Thus a mortgage of a common share is invalid in traditional fiqh. However, al-Zarqa notes that modern real estate registration systems overcome this obstacle, so a mortgage on a common share becomes valid if registration records the interest and allows the mortgage to be effective without physical detention. This shows the dynamic nature of Hanafi fiqh, adapting to changing circumstances while preserving underlying principles.

Key Terms
ملكية شائعة (milkiyya shā'i'a): Ownership of an undivided share in a thing, where the owner's right extends to every part of the thing proportionally but not to a specific part.
تصرف فعلي (taṣarruf fi‘lī): Physical act of dealing with the property, such as consuming, destroying, or using.
تصرف قولي (taṣarruf qawlī): Legal disposition through words, such as sale, gift, or lease.
مانع (māni‘): A legal hindrance that prevents the application of the general rule of permissibility of disposition.
رهن (rahn): A contract where property is given as security for a debt, requiring possession by the creditor and continuous detention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a mortgage (rahn) on a common share traditionally invalid in Hanafi fiqh?
A) Because the co-owner cannot sell his share without the partner's permission
B) Because mortgage requires continuous possession of the entire property, which is impossible without harming the other co-owner or losing security
C) Because mortgage is only valid for movable property
D) Because common ownership is not recognized in Hanafi fiqh

Key Takeaway
Common ownership generally allows contracts on the share, but mortgage is invalid unless modern registration overcomes the obstacle of continuous possession.

Exceptions to the General Rule of Transactions in Common Share
Explains the general rule of verbal transactions in common share and three exceptions with rationales.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim on verbal transactions in a common share and apply its three exceptions (rahn, hiba, ijara) by identifying the underlying rationale (illah) for each.

Core Idea
Verbal transactions are valid on a common share, except for rahn, hiba (if the property is divisible), and ijara—each blocked by a distinct illah.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi legal maxim: verbal transactions on an undivided share (ḥiṣṣa shā'iʿa) are valid just as on a distinct share. However, three contracts are excepted due to their inherent requirements: mortgage (rahn) requires continuous possession, gift (hiba) requires complete delivery, and lease (ijara) requires exclusive use. We will parse each exception and its rationale, enabling you to apply the maxim correctly in contemporary fiqh and fatwa contexts.

Guided Path
1
State the general maxim and its scope
From the passage, identify the maxim that verbal transactions (al-tasarrufat al-qawliyya) are executed in the common share as in the distinct share. Note the examples given: sale, reconciliation, endowment, bequest.
This maxim is grounded in the principle that dealing with part of owned property is permissible.
2
Identify the three excepted contracts
The passage lists rahn (mortgage), hiba (gift), and ijara (lease) as contracts that are not valid on a common share under certain conditions. List them and note that they are exceptions to the general permissibility.
The exceptions are not absolute; they depend on the circumstances (e.g., divisibility for hiba, presence of land registry for rahn).
3
For each exception, extract the illah (reason) from the text
Read the passage carefully to find the reasoning for each exception: - Rahn: requires continuous detention (istimrar al-habs) of the mortgaged property, which is impossible with a common share without violating co-owner rights or losing the security purpose. - Hiba: requires complete delivery (qabd kamil); for divisible property, partial delivery is insufficient. - Ijara: requires exclusive possession (taslim al-ayn lil-musta'jir li-l-intifa') which cannot be achieved if the property is commonly owned at the time of contract.
The illah determines the scope and modern applicability (e.g., land registration solves the rahn issue).
4
Apply the exception to specific cases
Work through the examples in the passage: - For hiba, distinguish between divisible property (e.g., half of a large house) – invalid; and indivisible property (e.g., a pearl) – valid. - For rahn, consider the modern context with land registration – valid. - For ijara, if commonness exists at contract time – invalid.
Note that if the property becomes distinct later (e.g., after partition), the contract may become valid.
5
Synthesize: when does the maxim apply and when does an exception govern?
Consolidate: The maxim applies to all verbal transactions except rahn, hiba (if property is divisible), and ijara (if commonness at contract time). For rahn, the exception can be overridden by a land registry system. The illah of each exception is key to determining its applicability.
This synthesis enables you to handle novel cases by analogy (qiyas) with the established illah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - وأما التصرُّفات القولية فتجري في الحصة الشائعة كما في الحصة المتميزة، فيصحُ بيع الحصة الشائعة من شيء والصلح عنها، ووقفها، والوصية بها، سواء أكان البائع أو العاقد مالكاً لتلك الحصة المعقود عليها فقط، أو مالكاً للشيء كاملاً، لأنَّ التصرُّف في بعض الشيء المملوك جائز كالتصرف فيه كله. غير أنه يستثنى من ذلك ثلاثة عقود اعتبر الفقهاء في طبيعتها وشرائطها يمنع إجراءها على الحصة الشائعة إما منعاً مطلقاً، أو في حال دون حال. وهذه العقود المستثناة هي الرهن، والهبة، والإجارة.
٢٥/ ٩ - وهذا المنع بالنسبة إلى الرَّهن وجيه فإن الرهن يتنافى مع شيوع المرهون في نظر الفقهاء؛ لأنه - بحسب غايته وهي توثيق استيفاء الدين - يشترط فيه قبض المرهون ثم يقتضي ذلك استمرار حبسه في يد المرتهن. وهذا مانع من صحة الرهن في الجزء الشائع، إذ لو رهن الإنسان حصة شائعة كربع فرس أو نصف دار فإذا احتبس المرتهن المال كله دائماً كان متجاوزاً على حق الشريك غير الراهن لحبس حصته من المال عنه بسبب الشيوع؛ وإذا تناوب المرتهن مع مالك الحصة غير المرهونة في الحبس والاستعمال بحيث يحتبسه بحكم الرهن مدة، ويسلمه إليه مدة للاستعمال، فإنه يفوت عندئذ استمرار احتباس المرهون فيفقد الرهن قوته التوثيقية ويعدم غايته. وهذا المعنى يقتضي أن يصح الرهن العقاري اليوم على الحصة الشائعة من العقار في البلاد التي أسست فيها سجلات ونظم عقارية توجب من تسجيل كل عقد عقاري في تلك السجلات. وهذا ما قد قرره القانون المدني لدينا إذ نَصَّت المادة / ١٠٥٨ منه على أن كل عقار جاز بيعه جاز رهنه ولم تستثن الحصة الشائعة في العقار من هذا الجواز (١). وذلك لأن نظام السجل العقاري أغنى عن قبض العقار المرهون بمجرد وضع إشارة الرهن عليه في صحيفته من السجل العقاري لمنع الراهن من التصرف فيه ببيع ونحوه مع بقاء العقار المرهون في يد مالكه الراهن، كما في سورية (٢). هذا ما يتعلق برهن الحصة الشائعة في نظر علماء الشريعة، أما الشيوع في محل عقد الهبة والإجارة فلفقهاء الحنفية تفصيل نلخصه فيما يلي:
أ - في الهبة :
إذا وردت الهبة على حصة شائعة من مال قابل للقسمة كهبة نصف دار كبيرة تحتمل أن تقسم إلى دارين وهبة ربع قطيع من الغنم من الغنم أو نصف صبرة من القمح ونحو ذلك مما لا تضره القسمة، فإن الهبة لا تصح في هذه الحصص الشائعة لأنه يشترط في عقد التبرع: التسليم والقبض، وإن كمال القبض يكون بتمييز المقبوض من غيره. والحصة الشائعة لا يمكن فيها إلا التسليم الضمني تبعاً لتسليم جميع المال الذي هي جزء شائع فيه فتسليمها على هذا الوجه يكون ناقصاً غير كامل وما دام المال قابلاً لأن يقسم وتسلم منه الحصة الموهوبة متميزة تسليماً كاملاً لا يكتفي بالتسليم الناقص. أما إذا وردت الهبة على حصة شائعة من مال غير قابل للقسمة وهو ما تضره القسمة كاللؤلؤة، والعقد من الحلي، والكتاب الواحد والفرس والحمام، والدار الصغيرة التي لا تحتمل أن تقسم منها الحصة الموهوبة ويبقى كل قسم داراً صالحة للسكنى ففي كل ذلك وأمثاله تصح هبة الحصة الشائعة لأنه عندما لا يمكن القبض الكامل في الهبة يكتفى بالقبض الناقص فيتسلم الموهوب له الحصة الموهوبة في ضمن تسلمه للكل، ويصبح شريكاً في ملكية العين صاحب الحصة الأخرى.
ب - في الإجارة : وأما الإجارة فقد منعها فقهاء الحنفية في الحصة الشائعة إذا كان الشيوع مقارناً لعقدها كما لو آجر أحد نصفاً فقط من عقار أو من دابة أو من ثوب، أو نحو ذلك.
B - As for verbal transactions, they are executed in the common share as in the distinct share, so the sale of a common share of something is valid, as is reconciliation regarding it, its endowment, and bequest of it, whether the seller or contracting party is the owner of that contracted share only, or the owner of the entire thing, because dealing with part of owned property is permissible like dealing with all of it. However, three contracts are excepted from this; the jurists considered in their nature and conditions that they are prevented from being executed on the common share, either absolutely or in some cases. These excepted contracts are mortgage, gift, and lease. 25/9 - This prohibition regarding mortgage is sound, for mortgage conflicts with the commonness of the mortgaged property in the view of jurists; because - according to its purpose, which is securing debt repayment - possession (qabd) of the mortgaged property is required, and then that necessitates continuous detention in the hand of the mortgagee. This prevents the validity of mortgage in the common share, for if a person mortgages a common share like a quarter of a horse or half a house, then if the mortgagee detains the entire property permanently, he transgresses against the right of the non-mortgaging partner by detaining his share due to commonness; and if the mortgagee alternates with the owner of the non-mortgaged share in detention and use, such that he detains it by virtue of the mortgage for a period and surrenders it to him for use for a period, then the continuous detention of the mortgaged property is lost, and the mortgage loses its security strength and its purpose is nullified. This meaning requires that today real estate mortgage is valid on the common share of real estate in countries where land registries and systems have been established that require registration of every real estate contract in those registries. This is what our civil law has decided, as its Article 1058 states that every real estate that may be sold may be mortgaged, and the common share in real estate was not excepted from this permission (1). This is because the real estate registry system sufficed for possessing the mortgaged real estate simply by placing a mortgage notation on its page in the real estate registry to prevent the mortgagor from disposing of it by sale or otherwise while the mortgaged real estate remains in the hand of its owner the mortgagor, as in Syria (2). This concerns the mortgage of a common share in the view of Sharia scholars. As for commonness in the subject of gift and lease, the Hanafi jurists have a detail that we summarize as follows: A - Gift: If the gift is made on a common share of property that is divisible, such as gifting half of a large house that can be divided into two houses, or gifting a quarter of a flock of sheep, or half a heap of wheat, and the like that division does not harm, then the gift is not valid in these common shares because in a donation contract, delivery and taking possession are required, and complete taking possession is achieved by distinguishing what is taken from others. In a common share, only implicit delivery is possible following the delivery of all the property of which it is a common part; thus delivery in this manner is incomplete and insufficient as long as the property is divisible and the gifted share can be delivered distinct with complete delivery; incomplete delivery is not sufficient. If the gift is made on a common share of indivisible property, meaning that division harms it, such as a pearl, a piece of jewelry, a single book, a horse, a pigeon, and a small house that cannot be divided such that the gifted share is taken from it and each part remains a house suitable for habitation, then in all these and their like, the gift of the common share is valid because when complete taking possession is not possible in a gift, incomplete taking possession is sufficient; so the donee receives the gifted share within his receipt of the whole and becomes a co-owner of the property with the other share owner. B - Lease: As for lease, the Hanafi jurists have prohibited it in the common share if the commonness is contemporaneous with the contract, such as if someone leases only half of a property, or an animal, or a garment, or the like.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage is a detailed legal discussion from a Hanafi perspective on the permissibility of transactions regarding a common share (حصة شائعة). It begins by establishing the general rule: verbal transactions such as sale, reconciliation, endowment, and bequest are valid on a common share just as on a distinct share, because dealing with part of owned property is permissible. Then it introduces three exceptions: mortgage (رهن), gift (هبة), and lease (إجارة). For mortgage, the prohibition is reasoned: mortgage requires continuous detention of the mortgaged property to secure the debt, but if the property is commonly owned, either the mortgagee would wrongfully detain the entire property or alternating possession would break the continuous detention, defeating the purpose. However, modern real estate registration systems allow mortgage on a common share because registration substitutes for physical possession. For gift, the distinction depends on divisibility: if the property is divisible (e.g., a large house), the gift of a common share is invalid because complete delivery (قبض كامل) is required and impossible without division; if the property is indivisible (e.g., a pearl), incomplete delivery suffices and the gift is valid. For lease, Hanafi jurists prohibit it when the commonness exists at the time of the contract, as in leasing half a property, because the lessee cannot exclusively possess the subject matter. The author follows the Hanafi madhhab throughout, providing the underlying rationale (علة) for each exception.

Key Terms
حصة شائعة (ḥiṣṣa shā'iʿa): An undivided share in a jointly owned property that has not been physically partitioned.
الرهن (al-rahn): A contract securing a debt by detaining the mortgaged property until the debt is paid.
الهبة (al-hiba): A donation contract transferring ownership without consideration.
الإجارة (al-ijāra): A lease contract transferring the right of use for a known period and rent.
مهايأة (muhāyā'a): An arrangement between co-owners to use the common property alternately according to shares.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is mortgage (رهن) on a common share generally prohibited in Hanafi fiqh?
A) Because the share is not clearly identified.
B) Because it conflicts with the condition of continuous detention (قبض) required for mortgage.
C) Because the mortgagee cannot use the property.
D) Because the mortgagor loses ownership.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim on common share transactions has three exceptions determined by the illah of each contract: continuous possession for rahn, complete delivery for hiba, and exclusive use for ijara.

The Maxim 'Forgiven in Continuance' Applied to Lease of Undivided Shares
Application of a legal maxim to lease of undivided shares

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'يغتفر في البقاء ما لا يغتفر في الابتداء' to determine the validity of a lease of an undivided share when common ownership arises after the contract.

Core Idea
A lease that is defective at inception can become valid if the defect occurs later.

The Hanafi maxim 'What is not forgiven at inception is forgiven in continuance' allows ongoing obligations to survive initial defects. This lesson focuses on its application to the lease of an undivided share by one co-owner without the other's consent, where the other later refuses to ratify. Understanding this maxim is crucial for fatwa practice in modern tenancy disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibition
Hanafi jurists prohibit leasing an undivided share if common ownership exists at the time of the contract. This is because the lessee cannot take exclusive possession of a specific part.
2
Distinguish later-arising commonality
If common ownership arises after the lease (e.g., one co-owner leases the whole property, then the other refuses), the rule changes.
3
Apply the maxim
The lease remains valid for the contracting co-owner's share because defects occurring after inception are forgiven.
The maxim 'يغتفر في البقاء ما لا يغتفر في الابتداء' is the legal basis.
4
Explore limits
The maxim does not apply to essential conditions like consent at inception.

إذا وردت الهبة على حصة شائعة من مال قابل للقسمة كهبة نصف دار كبيرة تحتمل أن تقسم إلى دارين وهبة ربع قطيع من الغنم من الغنم أو نصف صبرة من القمح ونحو ذلك مما لا تضره القسمة، فإن الهبة لا تصح في هذه الحصص الشائعة لأنه يشترط التمام عقد التبرع : التسليم والقبض، وإن كمال القبض يكون بتمييز المقبوض من غيره. والحصة الشائعة لا يمكن فيها إلا التسليم الضمني تبعاً لتسليم جميع المال الذي هي جزء شائع فيه فتسليمها على هذا الوجه يكون ناقصاً غير كامل وما دام المال قابلاً لأن يقسم وتسلم منه الحصة الموهوبة متميزة تسليماً كاملاً لا يكتفي بالتسليم الناقص أما إذا وردت الهبة على حصة شائعة من مال غير قابل للقسمة وهو ما تضره القسمة كاللؤلؤة، والعقد من الحلي، والكتاب الواحد والفرس والحمام، والدار الصغيرة التي لا تحتمل أن تقسم منها الحصة الموهوبة ويبقى كل قسم داراً صالحة للسكنى ففي كل ذلك وأمثاله تصح هبة الحصة الشائعة لأنه عندما لا يمكن القبض الكامل في الهبة يكتفى بالقبض الناقص فيتسلم الموهوب له الحصة الموهوبة في ضمن تسلمه للكل، ويصبح شريكاً في ملكية العين صاحب الحصة الأخرى.
ج
ب - في الإجارة : وأما الإجارة فقد منعها فقهاء الحنفية في الحصة الشائعة إذا كان الشيوع مقارناً لعقدها كما لو آجر أحد نصفاً فقط من عقار أو من دابة أو من ثوب، أو نحو ذلك.

الفصل (٢٥) خصائص الملكية
يتضح مما تقدم أن المانع من قابلية الحصة الشائعة لبعض التصرفات قد يكون حق الغير (وهو الشريك) كما في التصرُّف الفعلي بالإتلاف ونحوه، وقد يكون طبيعة التصرف كما في الرهن.

٢٥/ ١٠
الخاصة السادسة :
إنَّ الملكية الشائعة في الديون المشتركة وهي متعلقة بالذمم، لا تقبل
أ - فإذا قبضت تلك الديون المشتركة تعلقت الملكية عندئذ بعين المقبوض فأصبحت ملكية عين لا دين ؛ فيقسم المقبوض بين أصحاب ،
الحصص الشائعة فيه كما تقسم سائر الأعيان المشتركة القابلة للقسمة. وعلى هذا لو استوفى أحد شركاء الدين المشترك ما يعادل حصته من المدين لا يختص هو بالمقبوض ، بل يكون لشركاته في الدين أن يقاسموه ما ،قبض، فيأخذ كل منهم حصته منه ؛ إذ لو ساغ أن يستقل الواحد منهم بقبض حصته لوجب أن يعتبر الدين قد اقتسم وهو في الذمة، وتميزت فيه حصص الدائنين فلا يكون عندئذ مشتركاً، بينما هو مشترك.
فلو لم يكن الشيوع مقارناً لعقدها بل طرأ طروءاً، كما لو آجر أحد الشريكين جميع العقار المشترك أصالة عن نفسه وفضولاً عن شريكه فرفض الشريك العقد ولم يجزه في حصته، فإن الإجارة تبقى صحيحة في حصة الشريك العاقد؛ إذ من المقرر أنه
يغتفر في البقاء ما لا يغتفر في الابتداء» .
هذا ما يقرره فقهاء الحنفية في هذا الشأن. لكن لا يخفى على المتأمل أن هذه الاستثناءات الفقهية المانعة من صحة التصرف في
الحصة الشائعة بالرهن والهبة والإجارة ليست وجيهة متينة العلة في غير الرهن. فإن الهبة والإجارة لا يظهر مانع من صحتهما في المشاع وحلول الموهوب له أو المستأجر محل الواهب أو المؤجر في ملك الحصة الشائعة أو منفعتها، بدليل أن فقهاءنا أنفسهم قد تسامحوا بذلك فيما لا يحتمل القسمة بالنسبة إلى الهبة، وفي الشيوع الطارىء بالنسبة إلى الإجارة. على أن الأحكام القانونية النافذة لدينا اليوم تقتضي صحة هبة الحصة الشائعة وإجارتها في العقار وفي المنقول مطلقاً بلا تفصيل؛ كما أن قوانين الإيجار المتعاقبة لدينا تسوّغ
إجارة الحصة الشائعة من العقار.
٣٦٩
Chapter Five: The Theory of Property in Jurisprudence. He mortgaged it and did not exclude the undivided share in real estate from this permissibility. This is because the real estate registry system sufficed for taking possession of the mortgaged real estate by merely placing a mortgage mark on its page in the real estate registry to prevent the mortgagor from disposing of it by sale or otherwise while the mortgaged property remains in the hands of its owner-mortgagor, as in Syria. This relates to the mortgage of an undivided share in the view of Sharia scholars, civil law. Previously, our property law No. 3339 excluded the undivided share and explicitly stated in articles 23 and 104 the impermissibility of mortgaging it. Recently, the opinion of the Syrian Court of Cassation and its ijtihad has settled that registering the real estate contract in the real estate registry is considered a legal delivery that takes the place of actual delivery in its results. As for common ownership in the subject of a gift and lease contract, the Hanafi jurists have a detail that we summarize as follows: A - In gift: If a gift is made of an undivided share of divisible property, like gifting half of a large house that can be divided into two houses, or a quarter of a flock of sheep, or half a pile of wheat, etc., which are not harmed by division, then the gift is not valid for these undivided shares because a gratuitous contract requires complete delivery and possession, and complete possession is achieved by distinguishing the received property from others. In an undivided share, only implicit delivery is possible by delivering all the property of which it is a part; thus this delivery is incomplete and imperfect. As long as the property is divisible and the gifted share can be handed over separately with complete delivery, incomplete delivery is insufficient. But if the gift is of an undivided share of indivisible property, i.e., property harmed by division, such as a pearl, a piece of jewelry, a single book, a horse, a pigeon, a small house that cannot be divided such that each part remains habitable, then in all such cases and the like, the gift of an undivided share is valid, because when complete delivery is impossible in a gift, incomplete delivery suffices, so the donee receives the gifted share within his receipt of the whole and becomes a partner in ownership of the object with the other share owner. B - In lease: As for lease, the Hanafi jurists prohibit it for an undivided share if common ownership is concurrent with the contract, as if someone leases only half of a property, or of an animal, or of a garment, etc. [Page 346] Chapter 25: Characteristics of Property. It becomes clear from the above that the impediment to the susceptibility of an undivided share to certain dispositions may be the right of another (i.e., the partner) as in actual disposition by destruction etc., or it may be the nature of the disposition as in mortgage. Division. The sixth characteristic: Common ownership in joint debts, which are related to liabilities, does not accept... A - If those joint debts are collected, ownership then attaches to the collected thing, becoming ownership of a thing, not a debt; then the collected thing is divided among the owners of undivided shares as other common divisible things are divided. Thus, if one of the co-creditors collects from the debtor an amount equivalent to his share, he does not exclusively own what he collected; rather, his co-creditors in the debt may share with him what he collected, each taking his share. For if it were permissible for one of them to independently collect his share, then the debt would be considered divided while still in the liability, and the shares of the creditors would be distinguished, so it would not be common, whereas it is common. If common ownership is not concurrent with the contract but arises later, as if one of the two co-owners leases the entire common property personally and as an agent for his partner, and the partner rejects the contract and does not ratify it for his share, then the lease remains valid for the share of the contracting partner, because it is established that "what is not forgiven at inception is forgiven in continuance." This is what the Hanafi jurists affirm in this matter. But it is not hidden from the ponderer that these juristic exceptions preventing the validity of disposing of an undivided share in mortgage, gift, and lease are not soundly grounded in reasoning, except for mortgage. For gift and lease, there appears no impediment to their validity in co-owned property and the substitution of the donee or lessee for the donor or lessor in ownership of the undivided share or its usufruct, as evidenced by the fact that our own jurists have tolerated it for indivisible property in the case of gift, and for subsequent common ownership in the case of lease. Moreover, the current legal provisions in our country dictate the validity of gifting and leasing an undivided share in real estate and movable property absolutely without distinction; just as our successive rental laws have permitted leasing an undivided share of real estate.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage is a detailed fiqhi analysis from a modern legal theory text, discussing the permissibility of transactions involving undivided shares (hissa sha'i'a). The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, presents the classical Hanafi rulings on mortgage (rahn), gift (hibah), and lease (ijarah) regarding undivided shares, then critiques those rulings from a contemporary legal perspective. He begins with mortgage: the classical Hanafi rule prohibits mortgaging an undivided share because possession (qabd) of a specific portion is impossible. However, modern Syrian law, with its real estate registry system, considers registration as constructive possession, thus permitting mortgage of undivided shares. For gift, Hanafi jurists distinguish between divisible and indivisible property. If the property is divisible (e.g., a large house, a flock), a gift of an undivided share is invalid because complete delivery is required and possible. If the property is indivisible (e.g., a pearl, a small house), the gift is valid because incomplete delivery suffices since complete delivery is impossible. For lease, Hanafi jurists prohibit leasing an undivided share if the common ownership exists at the time of the contract. However, if common ownership arises later (e.g., one co-owner rents the whole property, the other refuses to ratify), the lease remains valid for the contracting co-owner's share under the maxim 'what is not forgiven at inception is forgiven in continuance.' The passage also discusses joint debts: an undivided share in a debt cannot be divided until the debt is collected; if one creditor collects his share, the others have a right to share in what he collected because the debt remains common until actual division. Finally, al-Zarqa critiques these classical exceptions as not being well-founded for gift and lease, noting that contemporary laws permit them unconditionally. This illustrates the interaction between classical fiqh and modern positive law.

Key Terms
الحصة الشائعة (al-hissah al-sha'i'ah): An undivided share in jointly owned property; a fractional interest that is not physically separated.
القبض (al-qabd): Possession or taking delivery; necessary for validity of certain contracts like gift and mortgage.
يغتفر في البقاء ما لا يغتفر في الابتداء (yughtafar fi al-baqa'i ma la yughtafar fi al-ibtida'): A Hanafi legal maxim meaning 'what is not forgiven at inception is forgiven in continuance'; used to allow ongoing contracts to remain valid despite defects that arise after formation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for the maxim 'يغتفر في البقاء ما لا يغتفر في الابتداء' to apply to a lease of an undivided share?
A) Common ownership must exist at the inception of the lease.
B) Common ownership must arise after the lease contract.
C) The lease must be registered in the real estate registry.
D) The lessee must be a relative of the lessor.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'يغتفر في البقاء ما لا يغتفر في الابتداء' permits a lease of an undivided share to remain valid for the lessor's share when common ownership arises after the contract.

Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications
advanced
10 min
Hanafi Rulings on Gift and Mortgage of Undivided Shares: Classical Positions and Modern Critique
Detailed fiqhi analysis of undivided shares in gift, mortgage, and critique

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions for the validity of gifting and mortgaging undivided shares, and evaluate the modern critique of these positions.

Core Idea
Gifts of undivided shares are valid only if the property is indivisible; mortgages are generally invalid without modern legal modifications.

The classical Hanafi school distinguishes between divisible and indivisible property for gifts of undivided shares, and generally prohibits mortgaging undivided shares due to possession requirements. However, modern jurists and laws have relaxed these restrictions. This lesson presents the original rulings and then examines the critique by contemporary scholar Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa.

Guided Path
1
State the gift ruling
Gift of undivided share is invalid if property is divisible (because complete delivery is possible), valid if indivisible (because only incomplete delivery possible).
2
State the mortgage ruling
Classic Hanafi: mortgage of undivided share is invalid because possession of a specific part is impossible.
3
Note modern modification
Modern registry systems consider registration as constructive possession, allowing mortgage of undivided shares.
4
Understand the critique
Al-Zarqa argues the restrictions for gift and lease (not mortgage) are not well-founded and that contemporary laws permit them unconditionally.
The critique is that the classical Hanafi exceptions are not firmly grounded in reasoning except for mortgage.
5
Identify the lesson for fatwa
The mufta bihi today may follow the modern law, but the classical view must be known for historical and comparative understanding.

Chapter Five: The Theory of Property in Jurisprudence. He mortgaged it and did not exclude the undivided share in real estate from this permissibility. This is because the real estate registry system sufficed for taking possession of the mortgaged real estate by merely placing a mortgage mark on its page in the real estate registry to prevent the mortgagor from disposing of it by sale or otherwise while the mortgaged property remains in the hands of its owner-mortgagor, as in Syria. This relates to the mortgage of an undivided share in the view of Sharia scholars, civil law. Previously, our property law No. 3339 excluded the undivided share and explicitly stated in articles 23 and 104 the impermissibility of mortgaging it. Recently, the opinion of the Syrian Court of Cassation and its ijtihad has settled that registering the real estate contract in the real estate registry is considered a legal delivery that takes the place of actual delivery in its results. As for common ownership in the subject of a gift and lease contract, the Hanafi jurists have a detail that we summarize as follows: A - In gift: If a gift is made of an undivided share of divisible property, like gifting half of a large house that can be divided into two houses, or a quarter of a flock of sheep, or half a pile of wheat, etc., which are not harmed by division, then the gift is not valid for these undivided shares because a gratuitous contract requires complete delivery and possession, and complete possession is achieved by distinguishing the received property from others. In an undivided share, only implicit delivery is possible by delivering all the property of which it is a part; thus this delivery is incomplete and imperfect. As long as the property is divisible and the gifted share can be handed over separately with complete delivery, incomplete delivery is insufficient. But if the gift is of an undivided share of indivisible property, i.e., property harmed by division, such as a pearl, a piece of jewelry, a single book, a horse, a pigeon, a small house that cannot be divided such that each part remains habitable, then in all such cases and the like, the gift of an undivided share is valid, because when complete delivery is impossible in a gift, incomplete delivery suffices, so the donee receives the gifted share within his receipt of the whole and becomes a partner in ownership of the object with the other share owner. B - In lease: As for lease, the Hanafi jurists prohibit it for an undivided share if common ownership is concurrent with the contract, as if someone leases only half of a property, or of an animal, or of a garment, etc. [Page 346] Chapter 25: Characteristics of Property. It becomes clear from the above that the impediment to the susceptibility of an undivided share to certain dispositions may be the right of another (i.e., the partner) as in actual disposition by destruction etc., or it may be the nature of the disposition as in mortgage. Division. The sixth characteristic: Common ownership in joint debts, which are related to liabilities, does not accept... A - If those joint debts are collected, ownership then attaches to the collected thing, becoming ownership of a thing, not a debt; then the collected thing is divided among the owners of undivided shares as other common divisible things are divided. Thus, if one of the co-creditors collects from the debtor an amount equivalent to his share, he does not exclusively own what he collected; rather, his co-creditors in the debt may share with him what he collected, each taking his share. For if it were permissible for one of them to independently collect his share, then the debt would be considered divided while still in the liability, and the shares of the creditors would be distinguished, so it would not be common, whereas it is common. If common ownership is not concurrent with the contract but arises later, as if one of the two co-owners leases the entire common property personally and as an agent for his partner, and the partner rejects the contract and does not ratify it for his share, then the lease remains valid for the share of the contracting partner, because it is established that "what is not forgiven at inception is forgiven in continuance." This is what the Hanafi jurists affirm in this matter. But it is not hidden from the ponderer that these juristic exceptions preventing the validity of disposing of an undivided share in mortgage, gift, and lease are not soundly grounded in reasoning, except for mortgage. For gift and lease, there appears no impediment to their validity in co-owned property and the substitution of the donee or lessee for the donor or lessor in ownership of the undivided share or its usufruct, as evidenced by the fact that our own jurists have tolerated it for indivisible property in the case of gift, and for subsequent common ownership in the case of lease. Moreover, the current legal provisions in our country dictate the validity of gifting and leasing an undivided share in real estate and movable property absolutely without distinction; just as our successive rental laws have permitted leasing an undivided share of real estate.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage is a detailed fiqhi analysis from a modern legal theory text, discussing the permissibility of transactions involving undivided shares (hissa sha'i'a). The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, presents the classical Hanafi rulings on mortgage (rahn), gift (hibah), and lease (ijarah) regarding undivided shares, then critiques those rulings from a contemporary legal perspective. He begins with mortgage: the classical Hanafi rule prohibits mortgaging an undivided share because possession (qabd) of a specific portion is impossible. However, modern Syrian law, with its real estate registry system, considers registration as constructive possession, thus permitting mortgage of undivided shares. For gift, Hanafi jurists distinguish between divisible and indivisible property. If the property is divisible (e.g., a large house, a flock), a gift of an undivided share is invalid because complete delivery is required and possible. If the property is indivisible (e.g., a pearl, a small house), the gift is valid because incomplete delivery suffices since complete delivery is impossible. For lease, Hanafi jurists prohibit leasing an undivided share if the common ownership exists at the time of the contract. However, if common ownership arises later (e.g., one co-owner rents the whole property, the other refuses to ratify), the lease remains valid for the contracting co-owner's share under the maxim 'what is not forgiven at inception is forgiven in continuance.' The passage also discusses joint debts: an undivided share in a debt cannot be divided until the debt is collected; if one creditor collects his share, the others have a right to share in what he collected because the debt remains common until actual division. Finally, al-Zarqa critiques these classical exceptions as not being well-founded for gift and lease, noting that contemporary laws permit them unconditionally. This illustrates the interaction between classical fiqh and modern positive law.

Key Terms
الحصة الشائعة (al-hissah al-sha'i'ah): An undivided share in jointly owned property; a fractional interest that is not physically separated.
القبض (al-qabd): Possession or taking delivery; necessary for validity of certain contracts like gift and mortgage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the classical Hanafi view presented in the passage, when is a gift of an undivided share valid?
A) When the property is divisible.
B) When the property is indivisible.
C) Always, regardless of divisibility.
D) Only if the donor is the sole owner.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi law permits gifts of undivided shares in indivisible property; modern laws have extended this to all property, and mortgages are now allowed through constructive possession.
