The Rule on Common Debts: Indivisibility Before Collection
States the rule on common debts with talil and exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule on common debts to determine when division is permissible and identify the exception for subsequent co-ownership.

Core Idea
A common debt is indivisible while a debt; only after collection does it become divisible like any shared asset.

This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal presents a rasm rule: the sixth characteristic of ownership regarding common debts. It clarifies that shared debts are not divisible until collected, and provides an exception for contracts where co-ownership arises subsequently. We will parse this rule and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act? Co-ownership in common debts. The ruling: it does not accept division before collection.
The debt is attached to the liability (dhimmah) and is a single obligation.
2
Analyze the talil (rational proof)
Why is division not allowed? Because allowing one creditor to collect his share would imply the debt was already divided, which contradicts its common nature.
The passage states: 'if it were permissible... that would require that the debt be considered as divided while in the liability'.
3
Identify the exception for subsequent co-ownership
When co-ownership arises after the contract (e.g., ijarah), the contract is valid for the acting partner's share due to the rule 'what is not excused at inception is excused in continuance'.
This is a key Hanafi principle: yughtafar fi al-baqā' mā lā yughtafar fi al-ibtidā'.
4
Apply the rule to a hypothetical scenario
Given a scenario: two creditors with a joint debt of 1000 from Zayd. One creditor cannot claim his 500 separately; if he collects the whole, he must share with his partner.
After collection, the money is a specific asset (ayn) and is divided like any common property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٢٥) خصائص الملكية
يتضح مما تقدم أن المانع من قابلية الحصة الشائعة لبعض التصرفات قد يكون حق الغير (وهو الشريك) كما في التصرُّف الفعلي بالإتلاف ونحوه، وقد يكون طبيعة التصرف كما في الرهن.
القسمة .
٢٥/ ١٠
الخاصة السادسة :
إنَّ الملكية الشائعة في الديون المشتركة وهي متعلقة بالذمم، لا تقبل القسمة.
أ - فإذا قبضت تلك الديون المشتركة تعلقت الملكية عندئذ بعين المقبوض فأصبحت ملكية عين لا دين؛ فيقسم المقبوض بين أصحاب الحصص الشائعة فيه كما تقسم سائر الأعيان المشتركة القابلة للقسمة. وعلى هذا لو استوفى أحد شركاء الدين المشترك ما يعادل حصته من المدين لا يختص هو بالمقبوض، بل يكون لشركاته في الدين أن يقاسموه ما قبض، فيأخذ كل منهم حصته منه؛ إذ لو ساغ أن يستقل الواحد منهم بقبض حصته لوجب أن يعتبر الدين قد اقتسم وهو في الذمة، وتميزت فيه حصص الدائنين فلا يكون عندئذ مشتركاً، بينما هو مشترك.
فلو لم يكن الشيوع مقارناً لعقدها بل طرأ طروءاً، كما لو آجر أحد الشريكين جميع العقار المشترك أصالة عن نفسه وفضولاً عن شريكه فرفض الشريك العقد ولم يجزه في حصته، فإن الإجارة تبقى صحيحة في حصة الشريك العاقد؛ إذ من المقرر أنه يغتفر في البقاء ما لا يغتفر في الابتداء» .
Chapter (25) Characteristics of Ownership. It is clear from the foregoing that the impediment to the capacity of a common share for some dispositions may be the right of another (the partner), as in physical disposition by destruction and the like, and may be the nature of the disposition, as in pledge. Division 25/10. The sixth characteristic: Common ownership in shared debts, which are attached to the liabilities (dhimmah), does not accept division. A - If those shared debts are collected, ownership then attaches to the specific thing collected, becoming ownership of a specific asset, not a debt; so the collected amount is divided among the holders of common shares in it, just as all other common assets that are divisible are divided. Accordingly, if one of the partners in the common debt recovers from the debtor the equivalent of his share, he does not exclusively own what is recovered; rather, his partners in the debt have the right to share with him in what he recovered, each taking his share from it. For if it were permissible for one of them to independently recover his share, that would require that the debt be considered as divided while in the liability, and the creditors' shares distinguished, so it would not be common, whereas it is common. If the co-ownership was not contemporaneous with the contract but arose subsequently, such as if one of the two partners leases the entire common property on his own behalf and as a volunteer on behalf of his partner, but the partner rejects the contract and does not ratify it regarding his share, then the lease remains valid in the share of the contracting partner, because it is established that what is not excused at the inception is excused in continuance.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm presents a rasm rule: the sixth characteristic of ownership regarding common debts. The rule states that a debt shared among multiple creditors (dīn mushtarak) is not divisible while it remains a liability on the debtor (fi al-dhimmah). Division only takes place after collection, because until collection, the debt is a single, indivisible obligation. After collection, the collected items become a specific asset (māl ayn), which can be divided like any other common property. The author provides a talil (rational proof): if one creditor could independently collect his share, it would imply that the debt was already divided in the liability, which is not the case. The passage then gives an exception for contracts where co-ownership arises subsequently (turu' al-shuyū'), specifically ijarah (lease): if one partner leases the entire common property without the other's permission, the lease is valid for his own share because what is not allowed initially (when the co-ownership is present) is allowed in continuance (yughtafar fi al-baqā' ma lā yughtafar fi al-ibtidā'). This is a well-known Hanafi rule.

Key Terms
ملكية شائعة (milkiyya shā'i‘a): Joint ownership where the share of each owner is undifferentiated and common in the property.
دين مشترك (dayn mushtarak): A debt owed to multiple creditors jointly, with each having an undivided share in the debt.
قسمة (qisma): Division of common property among co-owners by allotment of shares.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi rule on common debts, when does a common debt become divisible?
A) As soon as the debt is established in the liability.
B) Only after the debt is collected and becomes a specific asset (ayn).
C) When all creditors agree to divide it before collection.
D) When one creditor recovers his portion from the debtor.

Key Takeaway
Common debts are indivisible while in the liability; division occurs only after collection, except when co-ownership arises after the contract, where the contract is valid for the acting partner's share.

Options of Joint Creditor When Partner Purchases from Debtor
Ruling on options of joint creditor when co-purchases from debtor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when one joint creditor purchases from the debtor using his share of the debt, and distinguish the rule from the case of settlement (sulh).

Core Idea
A joint creditor who purchases from the debtor gives his partner an option to take his share of the price, not the item itself.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's work presents a detailed Hanafi rule on joint debts. It addresses a common scenario: one of two joint creditors purchases an item from their common debtor using his share of the debt. The text clarifies the legal consequences and the options available to the other creditor, and then contrasts this with the distinct rule for settlement (sulh). Understanding this distinction is crucial for a mufti when advising parties in such financial arrangements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fundamental transaction
Recognize that one joint creditor buys an item from the debtor, using his share of the joint debt as the price. Legally, this is treated as if that creditor received his share of the debt from the debtor.
The purchase is valid and considered a form of receipt (qabd) of that portion of the debt.
2
State the option (khiyar) of the other joint creditor
The non-purchasing creditor has two choices: (1) take from his partner the share of the price that corresponds to his portion of the debt, or (2) leave the item to the purchasing partner and pursue the debtor for his remaining share.
This option is not a right to share in the actual item unless the purchasing partner agrees.
3
Define the consequence if the debtor becomes insolvent
If the debtor becomes bankrupt or otherwise unable to pay the remaining debt, the non-purchasing creditor retains the right to recourse against his partner for his share of the price. This right is not lost by prior silence or non-approval.
The purchasing partner bears the risk of the debtor's insolvency to the extent of the price.
4
Contrast with the rule for settlement (sulh)
If instead of buying, one creditor settles with the debtor for something not of the same type as the debt (sulh), the other creditor has a different option: he may share in the item settled upon or demand his share of the debt amount. This is because sulh is built on compromise and usually involves a reduction.
The different treatment protects the settling partner from loss, as the settled item is typically of lesser value.
5
Apply the underlying principle: equity and custom
The distinction reflects the Hanafi methodology of balancing legal theory with custom and equity. In purchase, bargaining and equivalence are assumed; in sulh, leniency and reduction are intended. The mufti must appreciate these nuances.
This demonstrates the weight given to 'urf (custom) and maslahah (public interest) in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حتى لو ابتاع أحد الدائنين في الدين المشترك شيئاً من المدين بحصته من الدين فجعلها ثمناً للمبيع، يعتبر كما لو قبض من المدين المشترك ذلك القدر الذي اعتبر ثمناً. فالشريك الآخر في الدين عندئذ مخيّر: فإن شاء استوفى من رفيقه حصته من جزء الدين الذي اعتبر ثمناً للمبيع؛ وإن شاء ترك المبيع لرفيقه المشتري، ويلاحق المدين بحصته وهي الباقي من أصل الدين. ولو تعذر عليه تحصيل باقي الدين من المدين بإفلاس ونحوه يبقى له حق الرجوع على رفيقه بحصته من ثمن المبيع ولا يكون عدم إجازته قبلاً بمانع له من هذا الرجوع. وليس له أن يختار مشاركة رفيقه في عين المبيع الذي اشتراه إلا برضاه بخلاف ما لو قبض رفيقه جانباً من نفس الدين وجنسه فإنَّ للآخر أن يشاركه في المقبوض (ر : المجلة / ١٠٣/ وما بعدها) (۱).
* (۱) في هذا الموضوع يفترق حكم الشراء عن الصلح وإن كان من المقرر فقهاً أن الصلح عن مال بمال يعتبر بمثابة البيع والشراء. فلو صالح أحد الشريكين المدين عن حصته من الدين المشترك على شيء من غير جنس الدين وقبضه منه صلحاً لا شراء، فإن الخيار له عندئذ في أن يشترك معه رفيقه في عين الشيء المصالح عليه، أو أن يدفع إليه حصته من مبلغ الدين المصالح عنه، إلا أن يشاء رفيقه ترك الشيء لشريكه المصالح وملاحقة المدين بحصته من الدين: وذلك لأن الصلح مبني على التسامح والحط من أصل الحق، بخلاف البيع فإنه مبني على المماكسة والتعادل.. فالعين المصالح عليها تكون في العادة أقل قيمة من الحق المصالح عنه، فلو ألزم الشريك المصالح بأن يدفع إلى رفيقه حصته من مبلغ الدين المصالح عنه لتضرر الشريك المصالح (ر : المجلة / ١١٠٥ - ١١٠٦ / وشروجها للأستاذ الباز وغيره). وهذا كما ترى من الفروق التي تتجلى فيها دقائق الأنظار الفقهية في رعاية العرف والعادة والمبادىء النظرية معاً، ثم وزن فروع الأحكام على أساس ذلك بقسطاس العدل والإنصاف وفكرة التوازن التي تتراءى في سائر مباني الفقه الإسلامي.
Even if one of the joint creditors purchases something from the debtor with his share of the debt, making it the price of the item purchased, it is considered as if he had received from the joint debtor that amount which was considered the price. Then the other partner in the debt is given a choice: if he wishes, he may take from his partner his share of the part of the debt that was considered the price of the item; and if he wishes, he may leave the item to his purchasing partner and pursue the debtor for his share, which is the remainder of the original debt. If collecting the remaining debt from the debtor becomes impossible due to bankruptcy or the like, he retains the right to recourse against his partner for his share of the price of the item, and his prior non-approval does not prevent him from this recourse. And he may not choose to share with his partner in the actual item purchased except with his consent, unlike if his partner had received a portion of the debt itself of its type, for then the other may share in what was received (ref: al-Majalla, 103/et seq.) (1). (1) On this topic, the ruling of purchase differs from settlement (sulh), even though it is established in fiqh that settlement of property for property is considered equivalent to sale and purchase. So if one of the two partners settles with the debtor for his share of the joint debt for something not of the type of debt, and receives it from him as a settlement not a purchase, then he has the option that his partner may share with him in the actual item settled upon, or that he pay him his share of the amount of the debt settled for, unless his partner chooses to leave the item to his settling partner and pursue the debtor for his share of the debt. This is because settlement is based on compromise and reduction from the original right, unlike sale which is based on bargaining and equivalence. The item settled upon is usually of lesser value than the right settled for; so if the settling partner were forced to pay his partner his share of the amount of the debt settled for, he would be harmed (ref: al-Majalla, 1105-1106 and its commentaries by al-Baz and others). This, as you see, is one of the distinctions that manifest the subtleties of juristic considerations in observing custom, habit, and theoretical principles together, then weighing the branches of rulings on that basis with the scales of justice, equity, and the idea of balance that appears in all foundations of Islamic jurisprudence.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on the scenario where one of two joint creditors purchases an item from the debtor using his share of the joint debt as the price. The ruling clarifies that such a purchase is treated as if the creditor had received that portion of the debt. The other creditor is given an option: either to take his share of the debt from the purchasing partner (essentially recovering his portion of the price) or to leave the item to his partner and pursue the debtor for his remaining share. If the debtor becomes insolvent, the non-purchasing creditor still has recourse against his partner for his share of the price, and his prior silence or non-approval does not bar this right. However, the non-purchasing creditor cannot force a partnership in the actual item purchased; he can only claim his share of the price, unless the purchasing partner agrees to share the item. This contrasts with the case where the partner received a portion of the same type of debt (cash), where the other can share in that cash. The text then distinguishes purchase from settlement (sulh), noting that although settlement for property is legally like a sale, the option differs: in settlement, the other partner can choose to share in the item settled upon or demand his share of the debt amount, because settlement is based on leniency and reduction, unlike sale which is based on bargaining. The purpose is to protect the settling partner from loss, as the settled item is usually less valuable. This reflects the Hanafi method of balancing custom, equity, and legal theory.

Key Terms
دين مشترك (dayn mushtarak): A debt owned jointly by two or more creditors against a single debtor, where each has a share.
خيار (khiyar): The option given to the non-purchasing joint creditor to either take his share from the purchasing partner or to leave the item and pursue the debtor.
صلح (sulh): A settlement where the creditor accepts something other than the debt in full or partial satisfaction, based on compromise.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He must share in the item purchased with the purchasing partner.
B) He may pursue the debtor for his entire share, but cannot recourse against his partner.
C) He has right of recourse against his partner for his share of the price of the item.
D) He loses his claim entirely because of the purchase.

Key Takeaway
When one joint creditor purchases from the debtor, the other can only claim his share of the price, not the item itself; in sulh, the partner can share in the item.

Definition of Rukn in Hanafi Usul
Defining 'rukn' as an essential internal component

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'rukn' in Hanafi usul and distinguish it from 'shart' as an external condition.

Core Idea
The rukn is the integral part of a thing's essence; without it, the thing ceases to exist.

Understanding the five key terms in contract theory begins with the rukn (pillar). This lesson defines rukn both linguistically and technically, and illustrates its application in qiyas and contracts. Mastering this concept is essential for distinguishing between essential components and external conditions in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic meaning of rukn
The Arabic root implies strength and support, like the pillars of a house that hold the structure.
This helps grasp why rukn is considered a foundational element.
2
State the technical definition in usul al-fiqh
Rukn is that by which the existence (qiwam) of a thing is constituted, and it is an integral part of its essence (mahiyyah).
Contrast with shart, which is external.
3
Analyze the example of rukn in qiyas
The common illah between the original and branch cases is the rukn of qiyas; without it, the analogy collapses.
This highlights that rukn is indispensable for the act or concept.
4
Apply the definition to contracts
The rukn of a contract is the offer and acceptance (ijab wa qabul), which express the agreement of wills.
Offer and acceptance are internal; witnesses or written form are external conditions.
5
Differentiate rukn from shart (condition)
Rukn is part of the thing itself; shart is an external requirement for validity or obligation.
Missing rukn renders the act non-existent; missing shart may render it invalid but not necessarily non-existent.

Source Text

الفصل الثامن والعشرون
خمسة ألفاظ اصطلاحية في نظرية العقد

The twenty-eighth chapter: Five terminological words in the theory of contract: the pillar, the cause, the effective cause, the condition, the preventer. 1/28 - In order to understand the formation of a contract precisely, distinguishing the effect of each element upon which its formation and creation depend, it is good to preface by clarifying some terminological words in usul al-fiqh that one needs to know and distinguish their scientific concepts here and in many future investigations. These words are: the pillar (rukn), the cause (sabab), the effective cause (illah), the condition (shart), and the preventer (mani). 2/28 - a - The pillar (rukn): Linguistically, the rukn of something is the strong side that holds it, like the pillars of a house, which are its corners that hold its structure. The rukn in the terminology of the jurists and usul scholars is: that by which the thing's subsistence and existence are constituted, such that it is considered an integral part of its essence. (See al-Misbah al-Munir and Definitions of al-Sayyid al-Jurjani). For example, the rukn of analogy (qiyas) upon which it is built is the unity or similarity in the effective cause of the ruling between the original case and the branch case, because qiyas stands by this similarity just as the house stands by its four pillars. If this similarity is lost, the matter is no longer analogical; rather it is an establishment of a new ruling for the new incident. (See al-Kashf Sharh Usul al-Bazdawi, beginning of the chapter on rukn al-qiyas, 3/344). And the rukn of the contract is that which expresses the agreement of the two wills from offer and acceptance or...
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage introduces five key terms used in contract theory: rukn, sabab, illah, shart, and mani. It then focuses on defining 'rukn.' Linguistically, it means the strong side that supports something, like house pillars. Technically, it is the integral part of a thing's essence without which the thing cannot exist. Two examples are given: first, in qiyas (analogy), the rukn is the common illah between the original and branch cases; without it, qiyas fails. Second, in contracts, the rukn is the expression of mutual agreement through offer and acceptance. This distinction is crucial for understanding how contracts are formed and analyzed in Hanafi jurisprudence.

Key Terms
الركن (al-rukn): The essential component without which a thing cannot exist; an integral part of its essence.
السبب (al-sabab): The cause that leads to the existence of a thing, not part of its essence.
العلة (al-illah): The effective cause that motivates a ruling or action.
الشرط (al-shart): An external condition required for validity or obligation, not part of the essence.
المانع (al-mani): A preventer that blocks the effect of a cause or condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the rukn of a contract in Hanafi usul?
A) The subject matter of the contract.
B) The offer and acceptance.
C) The witnesses.
D) The written documentation.

Key Takeaway
The rukn is the integral part of a thing's essence; for contracts, it is offer and acceptance.

Types of Conditions: Legal and Posited
Defining two types of conditions (shart) in contract theory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a legal condition (al-shart al-shar'i) and a posited condition (al-shart al-ja'li) and identify the source and effect of each in Islamic legal theory.

Core Idea
Conditions in Islamic law come from two sources: divine legislation and human will.

This passage introduces a fundamental classification of conditions (shurut) in Islamic jurisprudence, a key concept for muftis analyzing contracts, oaths, and family law. Understanding whether a condition is imposed by the Shari'ah or created by an individual's will determines its validity and legal effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
The passage divides conditions into two categories based on their source: legal (shar'i) and posited (ja'li). Read the definitions carefully.
The first type is imposed by divine law; the second originates from human choice.
2
Analyze the source of each
For legal conditions, the source is the Shari'ah; for posited conditions, the source is the individual's will and action.
Posited conditions are called 'ja'li' because the person makes something a condition that was not legally required.
3
Study the examples
The author gives two concrete examples: conditional guaranty (kafala) and conditional divorce (talaq). Work through each: how does the condition relate to the effect?
In kafala, the condition is the debtor's travel; in talaq, the condition is the wife's action or omission.
4
Compare with cause (sabab)
The passage notes that the posited condition resembles a cause (sabab) in linking effect to existence. Understand the similarity and distinction.
Both condition and cause have a connection to the effect, but a condition is contingent on human choice, while a cause is a legal trigger.
5
Note the practical implication
The author postpones detailed discussion of posited conditions to a later section on contractual will. Recognize that this classification affects how muftis analyze conditional contracts and divorce.
Modern family law reforms (e.g., Article 90 of Personal Status Law) restrict conditional divorce to prevent abuse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٦/٢٨ - والفقهاء يقسمون الشرط باعتبار مصدر شرطيته إلى نوعين :
- شرط يفرضه الشرع، فيجعل تحققه لازماً لتحقق أمر آخر ربط به عدماً، بحيث إذا لم يتحقق الشرط لم يتحقق ذلك الأمر، وهذا ما تقدم بيانه وأمثلته في الفقرة السابقة.
- وشرط ينشئه الإنسان بتصرفه وإرادته فيجعل بعض عقوده أو التزاماته معلقة عليه ومرتبطة به، بحيث إنه إذا لم يتحقق ذلك الأمر لا تتحقق تلك العقود والالتزامات.
وذلك كما لو علق الإنسان كفالته بأمر يلائمها فقال لشخص دائن : إن سافر مدينك فلان اليوم، أو إذا لم يعد من سفره اليوم، فأنا كفيل بدينك الذي لك عليه فإن سفر المدين أو عدم عودته من سفره يصبح لثبوت الكفالة على القائل، فلا يعتبر كفيلاً ملتزماً بأداء الدين ما لم يتحقق ذلك الشرط الذي شرطه للكفالة
ومثله ما لو علق الرجل طلاق زوجته بفعل أو بعدم فعل، فقال لها : إن فعلت كذا، أو إن لم تفعلي كذا اليوم فأنت طالق، فإن فعلها أو عدم تحققه شرطاً لوقوع الطلاق، فلا يقع هذا الطلاق المعلق ما لم يتحقق ذلك الشرط (١).
-
فالنوع الأول، وهو الشرط الذي مصدره الشرع يسمى الشرط الشرعي ؛ وهو غرض بحثنا هنا.
والنوع الثاني وهو الذي مصدره إرادة الشخص يسمى: الشرط الجعلي. وقد سمي جعلياً» لأنَّ الأمر الذي صار شرطاً للالتزام لم تكن له هذه الصفة شرعاً، وإنما جعله الشخص شرطاً معلقاً عليه في أمر كان له فيه حق التنجيز والتعليق.
وهذا النوع من الشرط له شبه بالسبب من حيث ارتباط مشروطه به وجوداً وعدماً : ففي صورة تعليق الكفالة مثلاً بسفر المدين نرى أنه إن لم يسافر لا تثبت الكفالة، وإن سافر ثبتت . ومثل ذلك يلحظ في كل شرط جعلي .
. وهذا الشرط الجعلي لا يدخل في بحثنا هنا، لأننا سنتكلم عليه مفصلين أحكامه وما يصح تعليقه بالشرط من التصرفات وما لا يصح في بحث سلطان الإرادة العقدية في الفقه الإسلامي.
(1) يلحظ بهذا المناسبة أن المادة / ٩٠ من قانون الأحوال الشخصية لدينا الصادر سنة ١٣٧٣ هـ = ١٩٥٣ م قد قيدت صحة تعليق الطلاق على شرط وأبطلت كل تعليق للطلاق إذا كان المقصود منه مجرد الحث على فعل أو المنع من فعل أو توكيد الأخبار. وبهذا التقييد المتفق مع أدلة واجتهادات شرعية زال كثير من المآسي العائلية الناشئة عن تعليق الطلاق بصورة غير مشروعة حمقاً وجهلاً من كثير من الرجال . (ر: ف ۳/۱۸).
Section 6/28: The jurists divide the condition (shart) according to the source of its conditionality into two types: - A condition imposed by the Shari'ah, making its fulfillment necessary for the fulfillment of another matter linked to it by non-existence, such that if the condition is not fulfilled, that matter is not fulfilled. This is what was explained with examples in the previous paragraph. - And a condition originated by a person through his action and will, making some of his contracts or obligations contingent upon it and linked to it, such that if that matter is not fulfilled, those contracts and obligations are not fulfilled. For example, if a person makes his guaranty (kafala) contingent upon a suitable matter, saying to a creditor: 'If your debtor travels today, or if he does not return from his travel today, then I am a guarantor for your debt that you have against him,' then the travel of the debtor or his non-return becomes a condition for the establishment of the guaranty upon the speaker, so he is not considered a guarantor bound to pay the debt until that condition which he stipulated for the guaranty is fulfilled. Similarly, if a man makes the divorce of his wife contingent upon an action or omission, saying to her: 'If you do such-and-such, or if you do not do such-and-such today, then you are divorced,' then her doing or not doing becomes a condition for the occurrence of divorce, so this conditional divorce does not occur until that condition is fulfilled (1). So the first type, which is the condition whose source is the Shari'ah, is called the legal condition (al-shart al-shar'i); it is the subject of our discussion here. The second type, whose source is the person's will, is called the posited condition (al-shart al-ja'li). It is called 'ja'li' because the matter that became a condition for the obligation did not have this quality by Shari'ah, but the person made it a contingent condition in a matter in which he had the right to make it unconditional or conditional. This type of condition resembles the cause (sabab) in terms of the connection of its conditioned effect to it in existence and non-existence: in the example of suspending guaranty on the debtor's travel, we see that if he does not travel, the guaranty is not established, and if he travels, it is established. The same is observed in every posited condition. This posited condition does not enter into our discussion here, because we will discuss its rulings in detail—what actions can be validly suspended by a condition and what cannot—in the discussion of the authority of contractual will in Islamic jurisprudence. (1) It is noted in this regard that Article 90 of our Personal Status Law issued in 1373 AH = 1953 CE restricted the validity of suspending divorce on a condition and invalidated every suspension of divorce if its intent was merely to urge an action or prevent an action or confirm news. By this restriction, which agrees with legal evidences and ijtihads, many family tragedies arising from the improper suspension of divorce out of foolishness and ignorance of many men have been removed. (See: F 3/18).
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of two types of conditions (shart) in Islamic legal theory. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, is introducing a key distinction in the theory of contracts. The first type is the legal condition (al-shart al-shar'i), which is imposed by the Shari'ah itself. Its presence is necessary for the existence of a certain legal effect; if the condition is absent, the effect is absent. Examples include the requirement of capacity for contract validity. The second type is the posited condition (al-shart al-ja'li), which is created by the individual's own will. Here, a person makes a legal act (like a guaranty or divorce) contingent upon a future event. The author gives clear examples: in kafala, if the debtor travels, the guaranty becomes effective; in talaq, if the wife does a certain act, divorce occurs. The key difference is the source: the first comes from divine law, the second from human choice. The author notes that the posited condition resembles a cause (sabab) in that the effect is linked to it, but it is distinct. He postpones detailed discussion of the posited condition to a later section on contractual will. The footnote discusses modern legal reform limiting conditional divorce to prevent abuse, showing practical relevance.

Key Terms
الشرط الشرعي (al-shart al-shar'i): الشرط الذي يفرضه الشرع، ويلزم من عدمه عدم المشروط
الشرط الجعلي (al-shart al-ja'li): الشرط الذي ينشئه الإنسان بتصرفه وإرادته، معلقاً عليه عقوده والتزاماته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary difference between a legal condition (shart shar'i) and a posited condition (shart ja'li)?
A) The legal condition is created by the individual; the posited condition is imposed by the Shari'ah.
B) The legal condition is imposed by the Shari'ah; the posited condition is created by the individual.
C) Both are imposed by the Shari'ah, but differ in their consequences.
D) Both are created by the individual, but differ in their legal effects.

Key Takeaway
The classification of conditions into legal (shar'i) and posited (ja'li) is fundamental: one originates from divine law, the other from human will.

The Mirror Image Rule: Conformity of Offer and Acceptance
Rule with applied examples from sales and leases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that acceptance must match the offer in all respects, and identify when a purported acceptance constitutes a counter-offer.

Core Idea
Acceptance must mirror the offer exactly; any variation creates a counter-offer.

Contracts in Islamic law are built on mutual consent, which requires a clear meeting of minds. The Hanafi school holds that this meeting is only achieved when the acceptance corresponds precisely to the offer—any deviation, even a small one, breaks the link and transforms the response into a new offer. This lesson unpacks that rule and its practical applications in sales and leases.

Guided Path
1
State the rule (qa'idah)
Begin by articulating the maxim: 'يجب أن يوافق القبول الإيجاب من جميع الوجوه' – acceptance must match the offer in all respects.
This is a ḍābiṭ (sub-maxim) governing contract formation.
2
Identify the scope
Recognize that this rule applies to all contracts, particularly commutative contracts like sale (bayʿ) and lease (ijārah).
3
Work through the furūʿ (derived cases)
Analyze the examples given: different price, different payment terms, splitting the subject matter. For each, confirm that the purported acceptance does not match the offer, so no contract is formed.
4
Identify the legal consequence
Understand that a non‑conforming acceptance is not void; it becomes a new offer (ījāb jadīd) that the original offeror may accept or reject.
This is the practical outcome of the rule.
5
Apply in a fatwa context
When a mufti receives a case of a purported contract where the acceptance differed, he must rule that no contract was formed and that the second statement is a fresh offer waiting for acceptance.
This illustrates the operational use of the rule in iftāʾ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانياً : توافق الإيجاب والقبول : يجب أن يوافق القبول الإيجاب من جميع الوجوه؛ فإذا خالفه لا يعتبر قبولاً، فلا ينبرم به العقد. فلو أوجب البائع مثلاً لبيع الشيء بثمن قدره مائة، فقبل المشتري بتسعين؛ أو أوجب المؤجر بخمسين ديناراً نقداً، فقبل المستأجر بخمسين مقسطة أو مؤجلة لا ينعقد العقد بهذا القبول . ويعد من المحافظة المخالفة أيضاً تجزئة الصفقة بالقبول، ولو مع على نسبة البدل. فإذا أوجب البائع بيع ثوب من الجوخ، أو كمية من القمح بثمن معيّن فقبل المشتري نصف المبيع بنصف الثمن لا ينعقد البيع لمخالفة القبول للإيجاب، إذ ربما كان البائع لا يوافقه بيع بعض المبيع دون بعض (ر المجلة / ۱۷۷۷) . ولكن القبول المخالف لا يبطل، بل هو صالح لأن يبنى عليه، وقد يرضى به الطرف الآخر. فالقبول المخالف يعتبر إيجاباً جديداً يحتاج في
Secondly: Conformity of Offer and Acceptance: The acceptance must match the offer in all respects; if it contradicts it, it is not considered an acceptance, and the contract is not concluded with it. For example, if the seller offers to sell an item for a price of one hundred, and the buyer accepts at ninety; or if the lessor offers for fifty dinars cash, and the lessee accepts at fifty in installments or deferred, the contract is not concluded with this acceptance. Also counted as opposition is the splitting of the transaction in acceptance, even if with the proportion of the consideration. So if the seller offers to sell a piece of wool cloth, or a quantity of wheat, at a fixed price, and the buyer accepts half the item for half the price, the sale is not concluded due to the acceptance contradicting the offer, because perhaps the seller does not agree to sell only part of the item without the rest (Ref: Majallah al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah, article 1777). However, the contradictory acceptance is not void; rather, it is valid to build upon, and the other party may agree to it. So the contradictory acceptance is considered a new offer that needs [acceptance].
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage presents the legal rule that acceptance must correspond to the offer in every detail. The author begins by stating the rule: acceptance must match the offer in all respects. If it differs, it is not a valid acceptance. Examples: if the seller offers at 100 and the buyer accepts at 90, no contract; if the lessor offers cash and the lessee accepts in installments, no contract. Additionally, splitting the transaction—for example, offering two items as a set but accepting only half at half price—also violates the rule. However, such a non-conforming acceptance is not wasted; it becomes a new offer that the original offeror may accept. This is fundamental in contract formation: the mirror image rule.

Key Terms
إيجاب (ijab): The offer made by one party, expressing intention to enter into a contract.
قبول (qabul): The acceptance that must exactly match the offer for a valid contract.
تجزئة الصفقة (tajzi'at al-safqah): Splitting a transaction by accepting only part of the offered subject matter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The contract is formed at $500 because the buyer agreed.
B) The contract is formed at $450 because the buyer accepted.
C) No contract is formed; the buyer's statement is a new offer.
D) The contract is void due to disagreement.

Key Takeaway
A valid contract requires that the acceptance mirror the offer exactly; any variation, even in payment terms or partial acceptance, makes the response a new offer.

The Principle of Intention in Contracts and Correspondence of Offer and Acceptance
Explains the legal maxim 'العبرة في العقود للمقاصد' and the rule of correspondence of offer and acceptance with exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'العبرة في العقود للمقاصد' to determine the legal nature of ambiguous contractual expressions and evaluate when a discrepant acceptance constitutes a valid acceptance or a new offer.

Core Idea
Contracts are judged by substantive purposes, not literal words; a discrepant acceptance that wholly benefits the offeror is deemed acceptance.

This lesson explores a foundational principle of Hanafi contract law: the primacy of intention over form. Through the golden maxim 'العبرة في العقود للمقاصد والمعاني', we see how ambiguous expressions like 'take this' or 'I carry you' are interpreted. Moreover, the strict rule requiring acceptance to match the offer is nuanced by an exception when the discrepancy benefits the offeror entirely. Understanding this framework is essential for anyone issuing fatwas in mu'amalat.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its scope
Identify the golden rule: 'العبرة في العقود للمقاصد والمعاني لا للألفاظ والمباني'. Understand that this applies to all contracts, overriding literal wording when intention is clear.
This maxim is ranked as a wusta (intermediate) universal principle.
2
Analyze the examples given
Work through the examples: mentioning a price turns 'gift' into sale; phrases like 'I carry you' become loans (عارية/قرض) unless gift intent is evidenced; 'take this' becomes trust (وديعة) unless gift appears.
Each example applies the maxim to reinterpret the contract's legal nature.
3
Understand the rule of correspondence
Acceptance must match the offer in every respect—price, terms, object. If the buyer accepts at a lower price or splits the lot, it is not a valid acceptance but a new offer.
The Majallah (Article 1777) cites this rule.
4
Identify the exception
If the discrepancy benefits the offeror entirely (e.g., buyer offers 10, seller accepts 8; or seller offers 10, buyer accepts 12), the contract is concluded at the original offer price, and the concession depends on the offeror's consent.
This exception is rooted in Radd al-Muhtar (4/19).
5
Apply the rule to a new scenario
Given a novel scenario (e.g., offer of cash, acceptance of higher price on credit), determine whether the discrepancy is wholly beneficial. If not, the acceptance is a new offer.
The mufti must consider all aspects of the offeror's interest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٣٠) صيغة العقد وما إليها
وذلك لأن ذكر الثمن أو ذكر المهر قرينة تجعل لفظ الهبة جلي الدلالة على قصد البيع في الحالة الأولى أو على قصد النكاح في الحالة الثانية، إذ إنَّ العبرة في العقود للمقاصد والمعاني لا للألفاظ والمباني، كما تنطق به تلك القاعدة الذهبية المشهورة في الفقه الإسلامي (م/٣).
ولو قال إنسان لآخر : حملتك على هذه الدابة، أو أسكنتك في هذه الدار، أو أعنتك بهذا المبلغ فخذه لا يعتبر هذا هبة إلا إذا قامت قرينة على إرادة الهبة، كما لو كان القائل مَلِكاً مثلاً، وإلا كان عارية في مثال الدار والدابة وقرضاً في مثال المبلغ من النقود، حملاً على أدنى احتمالات اللفظ، كما تقدم.
وكذا لو قال لآخر : خذ مني هذا الشيء، فإن ظهر غرض الهبة فهو هبة، وإلا كان وديعة يجب حفظها لمعطيها .
٣/٣٠ - ثانياً : توافق الإيجاب والقبول :
يجب أن يوافق القبول الإيجاب من جميع الوجوه؛ فإذا خالفه لا يعتبر قبولاً، فلا ينعقد به العقد.
فلو أوجب البائع مثلاً لبيع الشيء بثمن قدره مائة، فقبل المشتري بتسعين؛ أو أوجب المؤجر بخمسين ديناراً نقداً، فقبل المستأجر بخمسين مقسطة أو مؤجلة لا ينعقد العقد بهذا القبول .
ويعد من المخالفة أيضاً تجزئة الصفقة بالقبول، ولو مع المحافظة على نسبة البدل. فإذا أوجب البائع بيع ثوب من الجوخ، أو كمية من القمح بثمن معيّن فقبل المشتري نصف المبيع بنصف الثمن لا ينعقد البيع لمخالفة القبول للإيجاب، إذ ربما كان البائع لا يوافقه بيع بعض المبيع دون بعض (راجع المجلة / ١٧٧٧) .
ولكن القبول المخالف لا يبطل، بل هو صالح لأن يبنى عليه، وقد يرضى به الطرف الآخر. فالقبول المخالف يعتبر إيجاباً جديداً يحتاج في انعقاد العقد على أساسه إلى قبول من الطرف الآخر الذي كان هو الموجب، فيصبح القابل موجباً، والموجب قابلاً.
على أنه إذا كانت مخالفة القبول للإيجاب، إنما هي مخالفة إلى خير من كل وجه بالنسبة إلى الشخص الموجب لا تكون مانعة من الانعقاد لأنها في الحقيقة ليست مخالفة وإنما هي عندئذ زيادة في الموافقة، وليست العبرة للأشكال الصورية بل للمقاصد الجوهرية .
فلو أوجب المشتري بعشرة دنانير فقبل البائع بأقل منها، أو أوجب البائع بعشرة فقبل المشتري بأكثر منها كان ذلك في الحقيقة قبولاً بالمبلغ الذي تضمنه الإيجاب مع التزام حط منه أو زيادة فيه لمصلحة الطرف الموجب. فينعقد البيع بالثمن الذي تضمنه الإيجاب، ويتوقف لزوم الحط أو الزيادة على رضى الطرف الموجب لأنَّ القاعدة: أنَّ الحط من العوض والزيادة فيه لمصلحة الطرف الآخر يتوقف لزومهما على رضاه (راجع رد المحتار ٤/١٩).
أما إذا لم تكن المخالفة إلى خير وأفضل من كل وجه، بل من وجه دون وجه لا ينعقد العقد إلا بموافقة الطرف الآخر، كما لو كانت المخالفة من كل وجه، وذلك كما لو أوجب البائع بعشرين ديناراً نقداً فقبل المشتري بأربعين نسيئة إلى شهر مثلاً، فلا ينعقد العقد، بل يعتبر القبول المخالف إيجاباً يحتاج إلى قبول ذلك لأنه وإن كان الثمن المقبول أزيد مقداراً، قد تكون حاجة البائع أو رغبته في النقد العاجل، أي في الناحية التي خولفت فيفوت غرضه .
٤/٣٠ - ثالثاً : جزم الإرادتين :
والمراد به أن تكون صيغة الإيجاب والقبول مفيدة للبت في العقد بصورة لا تردد معها ولا تسويف وإلا كانت نية الارتباط منتفية لأن التردد في حكم الرفض .
Chapter 30: The Form of the Contract and What Pertains to It. That is because mentioning the price or the dower is an indication that makes the word 'gift' clearly indicative of the intent to sell in the first case or the intent to marry in the second case, since the criterion in contracts is for purposes and meanings, not for words and structures, as expressed by that famous golden maxim in Islamic jurisprudence (Maxim 3). If a person says to another: 'I carry you on this animal,' or 'I give you residence in this house,' or 'I help you with this amount, take it,' this is not considered a gift unless there is an indication of intent to give, such as if the speaker is a king, etc. Otherwise, it is a loan for use in the example of the house and animal, and a loan of money in the example of the amount of money, based on the least likely meanings of the word, as previously mentioned. Likewise, if he says to another: 'Take this thing from me,' if the purpose of gift appears, it is a gift; otherwise, it is a trust that must be preserved for its giver. 3/30 - Secondly: Correspondence of Offer and Acceptance: The acceptance must correspond to the offer in all respects; if it differs, it is not considered acceptance, and the contract is not concluded by it. For example, if the seller offers to sell an item for a price of one hundred, and the buyer accepts at ninety; or the lessor offers for fifty dinars cash, and the lessee accepts for fifty in installments or deferred, the contract is not concluded with this acceptance. Also considered a discrepancy is splitting the transaction by acceptance, even if the proportion of the consideration is preserved. If the seller offers to sell a piece of broadcloth or a quantity of wheat for a specified price, and the buyer accepts half of the sold item for half the price, the sale is not concluded because of the discrepancy between acceptance and offer, as the seller might not agree to sell part of the sold item without the rest. (See Majallah Article 1777). But the discrepant acceptance is not void; rather, it is valid as a basis, and the other party may agree to it. The discrepant acceptance is considered a new offer that requires acceptance from the other party who was the offeror, so the acceptor becomes the offeror, and the offeror becomes the acceptor. However, if the discrepancy between acceptance and offer is entirely beneficial to the offeror, it does not prevent conclusion because in reality it is not a discrepancy but rather an increase in agreement, and the criterion is not for formal forms but for substantive purposes. So if the buyer offers ten dinars and the seller accepts less than that, or the seller offers ten and the buyer accepts more, that is in reality acceptance of the amount contained in the offer with a commitment to reduce or increase it for the benefit of the offeror. The sale is concluded at the price contained in the offer, and the binding nature of the reduction or increase depends on the consent of the offeror because the rule is: the reduction or increase in consideration for the benefit of the other party depends for its binding effect on his consent. (See Radd al-Muhtar 4/19). If the discrepancy is not entirely to the benefit and better in all respects, but only in one aspect, or if it is a discrepancy in all respects, the contract is not concluded except with the consent of the other party. For example, if the seller offers twenty dinars cash, and the buyer accepts forty on credit for a month, the contract is not concluded, but the discrepant acceptance is considered an offer that requires acceptance because even though the accepted price is higher in amount, the seller may need or desire immediate cash, i.e., the aspect that was differed, so his purpose is thwarted. 4/30 - Third: Decisiveness of the Two Wills: What is meant is that the form of offer and acceptance should indicate a definite decision regarding the contract without hesitation or deferment; otherwise, the intention of commitment is absent because hesitation is considered rejection.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage is a lesson in contract formation from a Hanafi perspective. The author begins by emphasizing the 'golden rule' that contracts are judged by intentions and meanings, not literal words. He illustrates this with examples: mentioning a price or dower turns a word like 'gift' into a sale or marriage contract. Then he gives scenarios where words like 'I carry you' or 'I give you residence' are interpreted as loans (عارية or قرض) rather than gifts unless there is a clear indication of gift-giving. Similarly, 'take this' is a trust (وديعة) unless intent to give is evident. The second major point is that acceptance must match the offer in all respects. If the buyer accepts at a lower price or different terms, it is not a valid acceptance but a new offer. However, if the discrepancy benefits the offeror in every way, it is considered an acceptance of the original offer with a voluntary concession, and the contract is concluded subject to the offeror's consent to the concession. The author cites the Majallah and Radd al-Muhtar. Finally, the third point introduces the requirement that both offer and acceptance must be decisive, not conditional or deferred, because hesitation is akin to rejection. This lesson is crucial for understanding the Hanafi framework for valid contracts based on mutual intent and clear correspondence.

Key Terms
الإيجاب (al-ījāb): The offer made by one party that initiates the contract or creates a legal proposal
القبول (al-qubūl): The acceptance by the other party that matches the offer in all respects

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a seller offers to sell a bundle of goods for 100 dinars, and the buyer responds 'I accept half of the bundle for 50 dinars,' what is the ruling in the Hanafi school?
A) The sale is valid for half the bundle at 50 dinars.
B) The buyer's response is considered a new offer; the sale is not concluded.
C) The sale is void because of uncertainty (gharar).
D) The sale is concluded at 100 dinars for the full bundle.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school emphasizes intention and substantive purpose over literal wording in contracts, and while acceptance must match the offer strictly, a complete benefit to the offeror turns a discrepancy into an acceptance.

The Preferred Form of Offer and Acceptance: Past Tense is Preferred, Not a Condition
Defining the general rule for offer and acceptance forms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that any expression indicating firm intention (irāda jāzima) is valid for contracts, and identify the preferred past tense form.

Core Idea
The past tense is the best form for offer and acceptance, but any clear expression of firm intention suffices.

In this lesson, we explore the Hanafi rule on the form (ṣīgha) of offer and acceptance in contracts. The preferred form is the past tense because it most clearly signifies a decisive, immediate intention. However, the fuqahā' emphasize that the past tense is not a condition; any expression that unmistakably shows the parties' firm resolve is sufficient. This includes the present tense used for the immediate moment, specific phrases like 'I accepted', and even imperative forms in certain contexts. We will walk through the rule and its applications in various contract types.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Identify the preferred form for offer and acceptance according to the Hanafi school: the past tense (e.g., 'I sold', 'I bought') because it indicates definitive creation (inshā' jāzim).
The rule is that past tense is best but not a requirement.
2
Identify the scope of the rule
Understand that this rule applies to all contracts (buyū', ijārah, nikāḥ, etc.) where a clear expression of firm intention is needed.
The same principle holds across different contract types.
3
Apply the rule to examples
Examine the examples from the text: present tense used for the immediate moment ('I sell now'), phrases like 'I accepted' after an offer, conditional expressions ('if you want'), and imperative forms ('Take it') implying an offer.
All these are valid if they show decisive intent.
4
Note the exceptions (istithnā')
Learn the exceptions: an imperative used as a request (e.g., 'Sell me') without explicit acceptance does not constitute an offer, but in marriage contracts the imperative is valid due to custom.
The maxim 'al-amr lā yadullu ʿalā al-ījāb fī al-ʿuqūd' governs this.
5
Distinguish from promises
Differentiate a contract from a mere promise: a contract requires firm intention at the moment of utterance, while a promise (waʿd) is not binding in the same way.
This distinction is crucial for understanding khiyār and suspended contracts.

=
فإرادة العاقدين في العقد المعلق أو المضاف أو المخير فيه مبتوتة ليست كإرادة من: يعد بالبيع وعداً أو يقول : سوف أبيع ولكن العقد المعلق مجزوم به من قبل العاقد لا حالاً، بل عند وجود الشرط الخارجي المعلق عليه ؛ والعقد المضاف مجزوم به للحال، إلا أن ابتداء حكمه مؤخر إلى زمن معين؛ والعقد مع الخيار مبتوت أيضاً في أصل انعقاده، وليس الخيار إلا حقاً ممنوحاً لأحد العاقدين في فسخ العقد القائم. فكل ذلك لا ينافي الجزم بالمعنى المقصود هنا في صيغة العقد.
Chapter Six: The Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh
5/3 - The general form that jurists prefer for offer and acceptance to indicate firm intention is the past tense, such as: 'I sold', 'I bought', 'I pledged', 'I accepted in pledge', 'I married', 'I married', etc...
And the word 'I accepted' or 'I agreed' or similar after mentioning the offer is sufficient and dispenses with repeating what was stated in the offer.
However, the past tense is not a condition; rather it is better than others because it indicates definitive creation in the context of contracts.
If both parties use the present tense intending the present moment, the contract is concluded; as if one says 'I sell to you now' and the other says 'I buy now' (see: Majalla, 170).
Similarly, in contracts, every expression that clearly shows the firm intention of the contracting parties in creating contracts according to the subject of each contract is valid.
In a sale contract, if the buyer makes an offer by saying 'This is mine for one thousand' and the seller says 'I accepted', or the seller makes an offer by saying 'It is yours for one thousand if you accept or it pleases you or if you want' and the buyer says 'It pleases me' or 'I want it', the sale is concluded with all these expressions (see: Fath al-Qadir by Kamal Ibn al-Humam, 5/458-459). Even jurists have considered the imperative form and other expressions sufficient if they indicate an implicit offer or acceptance required by the meaning and necessitated by it, as when one says 'I bought this from you for such' and the owner says 'Take it' or 'May Allah bless you' = because it is in the strength of his saying 'I sold it to you, so take it'; they built on the implied meaning (see: Majalla, 172).
The intention of the contracting parties in a suspended, deferred, or option contract is decisive, not like the intention of one who promises to sell or says 'I will sell'. Rather, the suspended contract is decided by the contracting party not now, but upon the occurrence of the external condition; the deferred contract is decided for the present, but the beginning of its effect is postponed to a specified time; and the contract with an option is also decisive in its basic conclusion, and the option is only a right granted to one party to rescind the existing contract. None of this contradicts the decisiveness intended here in the form of the contract.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage is a general rule on the form of offer and acceptance in contracts. The author establishes that the past tense is the preferred form because it best signifies firm intention (al-ijab al-jazim). However, it is not a condition; any expression that clearly demonstrates the parties' resolve suffices, including the present tense if used for the immediate moment. The text then provides numerous applications (furuat): phrases like 'I accept' or 'I agree' after the offer, conditional expressions ('if you want'), and even imperative forms like 'Take it' are considered valid because they imply an offer. The passage also clarifies that suspended contracts (mu'allaq), deferred contracts (mudaf), and option contracts (makhyar) are all decisive in intention, distinguishing them from mere promises. This rule is from the Hanafi madhhab and is foundational for contract law.

Key Terms
صيغة الماضي (sīghat al-māḍī): The past tense verb form used in offer and acceptance to indicate firm intention
إرادة جازمة (irāda jāzima): Firm and decisive intention to conclude a contract
عقد معلق (ʿaqd muʿallaq): A contract whose effectiveness is suspended upon a future condition

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following expressions is considered a valid offer according to the Hanafi view in this passage?
A) 'I will sell you this tomorrow' (using future tense)
B) 'I sell to you now' (using present tense for immediate sale)
C) 'I promise to sell you this'
D) 'Take this as a gift'

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi contract law, the past tense is the best form for offer and acceptance, but any expression that clearly shows firm intention is valid.

Intention in Conditional, Deferred, and Option Contracts
Clarifying intention in suspended, deferred, and option contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the firm intention (irāda jāzima) in suspended, deferred, and option contracts from mere promises, and explain why these contracts are decisively formed.

Core Idea
Suspended, deferred, and option contracts are decisively intended, unlike non-binding promises.

This lesson covers the second part of the passage, which clarifies that contracts like suspended (muʿallaq), deferred (muḍāf), and those with an option (makhyār) are all based on firm intention (irāda jāzima) at the time of contracting, even if their effect is postponed or subject to a condition. The author distinguishes these from mere promises (waʿd) which are not legally binding in the same way. Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining the status of such agreements in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of contracts discussed
Recognize the three types: suspended (muʿallaq) – depends on a future condition; deferred (muḍāf) – effect postponed to a future date; and option contracts (makhyār) – one party has the right to rescind.
All are distinct from a mere promise.
2
Define firm intention in each case
In a suspended contract, the intention is decisive to form the contract now, but the effect is conditional. In a deferred contract, the intention is decisive now, but the effect is delayed. In an option contract, the contract is fully formed, but a rescission right exists.
The key is that the contract is firmly intended and created (munʿaqid) at the moment of offer and acceptance.
3
Contrast with promises
A promise (waʿd) like 'I will sell' does not create a binding contract because the intention is not yet decisive; it is a future commitment that is revocable.
Promises are not legally enforceable in the same way as contracts.
4
Apply the rule to the examples
Consider the example from the text: 'I sell if the purchaser accepts within a week' – this is a suspended contract; it is valid and binding upon acceptance. Similarly, 'I sell effective next month' is a deferred contract; it is valid now but effective later.
The firm intention is present even if the effect is not immediate.

Source Text

عقد مضاف (ʿaqd muḍāf): A contract whose legal effect is deferred to a future time
خيار (khiyār): An option or right to rescind a contract
